IBM's $10 billion bet on what comes after AI (with CEO Arvind Krishna) | Masters of Scale

Masters of Scale(155.0K)·39:34·365 views·2026-06-23
Arvind KrishnaCEO, IBM
TL;DRIBM CEO Arvind Krishna argues that the AI revolution is at "day zero," requiring enterprises to transition from experimenting with massive, expensive foundation models to deploying right-sized, economic alternatives.
To lead the next wave of technological innovation, IBM...

TL;DR

IBM CEO Arvind Krishna argues that the AI revolution is at "day zero," requiring enterprises to transition from experimenting with massive, expensive foundation models to deploying right-sized, economic alternatives. To lead the next wave of technological innovation, IBM is prioritizing hybrid cloud, cybersecurity, and a massive $10 billion commercial bet on quantum computing.

Redefining AI Strategy: Moving Away from the "18-Wheeler"

  • The Commodity of Foundation Models:
      • Low Switching Costs: Krishna predicts that foundation models will become commodities within 1 to 3 years, meaning they will hold value (like gold or iron) but offer very little switching cost for enterprises.
  • The "18-Wheeler" Analogy:
      • Over-Engineering Tasks: Currently, enterprises are using massive, expensive AI models—the equivalent of an "18-wheeler"—for simple, everyday tasks like basic customer inquiries.
      • Shift to Right-Sized Models: Within the next 24 months, rising token and GPU costs (which doubled in the six months prior to the interview) will force a transition to smaller, specialized, and on-premise models that operate at 1/100th of the cost.
  • Strategic Lessons from Watson:
      • Monolithic Pitfalls: Krishna candidly acknowledged IBM’s early missteps with Watson, noting that they built monolithic vertical solutions too quickly in the incredibly complex healthcare domain, despite having no sales experience dealing with doctors or the FDA.
  • "Day Zero" Paradigm:
      • Focus at Scale: Rather than running 100 small experiments, enterprises should select 3 to 5 high-impact use cases and execute them at scale to master change management and data organization.

AI ROI, Productivity, and the Corporate Job Market

  • The 20-80 Power Law:
      • Adoption Divide: Unlike a 50-50 K-shaped economy, corporate AI adoption follows a power law where only 20% of companies aggressively adopt AI and achieve real returns, while 80% struggle to start.
      • Domain Experts over PhDs: Companies do not need PhDs in computer science to deploy AI; instead, they should task curious domain experts with reimagining processes.
  • IBM’s $5 Billion Efficiency Blueprint:
      • Delayed Return Curve: During its first year of AI deployment, IBM spent more on engineers, infrastructure, and tokens than it saved.
      • 10x ROI: By year two, IBM achieved a 10x return on its AI investments. The company is now on track to capture over $5 billion in productivity savings relative to its 2022 spending baseline.
  • The Developer Productivity Paradox:
      • Increased Hiring: IBM’s software developers became 40% more productive using AI, yet IBM tripled its college-level entry hiring. Lower development costs made previously unprofitable products economically viable, triggering net job growth.
      • Back-Office Displacement: While value-generating sectors (sales, marketing, consulting, development) will grow, roughly 30% of back-office roles (compliance, accounts payable, procurement) will be displaced over the next few years.

Cyber Defense and the Democratization of Threats

  • Lowering the Attacker's Bar:
      • The Mythos Impact: The release of advanced models like Anthropic's Mythos has dramatically lowered the technical barrier to entry for cyberattacks, expanding the global threat landscape to average-skilled bad actors.
  • Project Lightwell:
      • $5 Billion Commitment: To counter these threats, IBM launched Project Lightwell, a $5 billion initiative designed to identify and fix open-source vulnerabilities using AI.
      • Anonymized Clearinghouse: IBM is deploying AI agents to automatically write security patches for open-source code and run an anonymized, collaborative defense utility for its clients.

The $10 Billion Leap into Quantum Computing

  • Government-Backed Scaling:
      • The Quantum Foundry: IBM has partnered with the U.S. government on a new quantum foundry, investing $10 billion to scale commercial quantum systems.
  • Exponential Performance Gains:
      • From Atoms to Proteins: In a short timeframe, IBM’s quantum systems progressed from simulating a 5-atom molecule to a 300-atom molecule, and recently reached 12,000 atoms (simulating trypsin, a protein fragment).
      • Disruptive Use Cases: This rapid scaling will soon allow quantum computers to solve complex protein-binding equations in minutes, opening entirely new pathways for drug discovery, aerodynamics, and fluid dynamics.

Overcoming Corporate Risk Aversion

  • The Danger of No Risk:
      • Slow Corporate Decay: Krishna warns that taking zero risk is a company's most dangerous path, leading to a slow, 5-to-10-year decline as competitors steadily erode the organization's most profitable business lines.
  • Counteracting Loss Aversion:
      • Targeting 50% Probability Wins: Because humans are naturally loss-averse, leadership must normalize failure. Krishna encourages teams to pitch projects with a 50% probability of success, rather than waiting for 90% certainty.

Notable Quotes

"An automobile is an automobile, at some gross level, if you step back. I guess you could take your kids to school in an 18-wheeler every morning. You could go milk shopping in an 18-wheeler. Then you'd ask yourself, is it really the most effective vehicle for that? ... Right now we're using the 18-wheeler for everything." — *Arvind Krishna*
"Actually, I recommend we give you somebody from a domain who doesn't really know the depth of AI, but who understands the difference AI could make to your domain. ... That's for the inventors of AI. That's not needed for the deployers of AI." — *Arvind Krishna*
"The most risky route is taking zero risk." — *Arvind Krishna*

Plus, we'll cover what kind of risk-taking businesses need to take right now, how to think about costs versus benefits when implementing AI, IBM's big bet on quantum computing, and much, much more. IBM is a New York institution; it is a global institution. As a company, it's had to continually reinvent itself, you know, from mainframes to PCs, and consulting and cloud, and now AI, and on the cusp, quantum computing. Yeah, look, the advantage is client intimacy, knowing your clients. The advantages are around trust. I don't think we have knowingly ever done anything wrong with a client's IP, or data, or people. Those are advantages. I think our people are incredibly technically adept. I would say they're experts in the areas where they've spent time and energy, so those are all the advantages. Now, technology keeps changing. And I'll be the first to acknowledge, sometimes we are really good at predicting where it's going to go, and we kind of get ahead of the wave and do that. You mentioned a few of those. Mainframes certainly, but this is now 60 years ago, was one of them. I would say the IBM PC may have been another one of them. I think embracing Java in the internet era was another great one. And then you sometimes miss them. It's not for actually lack of knowledge; you miss them because the business model doesn't align. You don't quite know how to get the investments and returns to come together. I would say public cloud is one of them, candidly, that we missed. I would actually turn around and say despite inventing the IBM PC, client-server was another one that we missed. So, I think it comes down to: you could probably miss some. And you kind of know you're going to miss some sometimes, right? And you know you're going to... I think because if you don't take risk, you're never going to succeed. So, part of it is, hey, I'm getting a lot from that one, I kind of want to keep my focus there. And kind of if you're not two, three years ahead of the wave, you're going to miss the wave. But the whole point then is, can you do enough of those where you can be with the wave as opposed to way behind? Because it moves so fast nowadays that if you're two, three years behind, you are not going to catch up. And so right now, we are very focused on hybrid cloud—that is sort of our answer to this cloud movement—and AI, where I think that our play is going to be much more of, similar to a hybrid play. How do we help our enterprise clients take advantage of it fully? I mean, the first-mover advantage is this sort of catchphrase in techland, and I was thinking about AI and IBM Watson. You were sort of ahead in some ways, but you didn't maybe make the splash that you wanted. Was that a missed moment, or is it more that, you know, you were too early, or the tech wasn't quite ready, or...? There's always all those things. I think when we won Jeopardy! with Watson, I think it woke the world up because, for the first time in a long time, AI started doing something people thought it could not do. Okay? Unfortunately, it woke the world up completely in the sense that a number of other companies started investing very heavily. Now, we had an advantage. We might have been able to succeed, but I'm not putting on 20/20 vision looking backwards. The mistakes we made were actually much more of a strategic nature. As opposed to creating building blocks, we wanted to create solutions in verticals. You went too quickly to the application. We went too quickly, and we wanted to make a monolithic application. Mistake number one. Mistake number two: we picked a domain which is perhaps the hardest of them all, which is health. Mistake number three: how many IBMers sell to doctors and how many IBMers deal with the FDA? Like, none. So, you picked the wrong solution set, in an industry you know nothing about, and with a customer you know nothing about. Other than that, it was pretty good. So as you think about IBM today and its role in the AI ecosystem, like what is it? I mean, you're not trying to be OpenAI or Anthropic, you're not trying to be Google or Microsoft. Are you ahead? Are you behind? How do you think about all of that? So, we are not going to be a hyperscaler, which is two of the four you mentioned. And we're not a foundation model provider. I'll say something provocative. I think foundation models are going to become commodities, by the way, not that far out. Is it a year? Is it two years? Is it three years? Commodities doesn't mean that they don't have value. Gold is a commodity. So is iron. So, commodities means that there is very little switching cost to go from one to the other. Second, I think that right now, the token price on all of these is going to go way up. It just has to, to justify the capital investments. You put those two together, and there's going to be a huge motivation then from everybody who's using them to say, "I need to optimize. I need to use each one, but in the most economic way possible." Our role is to, A, let our enterprise clients do that; two, do it in a way that is safe. And right now, there is very little demand for on-premise or smaller models, which are effectively then 1/100th of the cost to run. So, I'll use the analogy in a very crude way. In the end, an automobile, which I'll include trucks into, is an automobile, at some gross level, if you step back. I guess you could, for those in the suburbs, take your kids to school in an 18-wheeler every morning. You could go milk shopping in an 18-wheeler. Then you'd ask yourself: is it really the most effective vehicle for that? But if you're moving homes, which you do every seven years on average in the country, it is the most effective vehicle for that. I think right now we're using the 18-wheeler for everything. And this is the transition that I'll predict will happen within 24 months. I'm not sure it'll happen within 12 months. I'll point out to you underlying GPU pricing, which is what all of these things run on, has doubled in the last six months, on a per-hour basis. So, it's getting more expensive to use these tools. And right now when you're pre-public, it's okay to lose money because you're gearing towards the number of customers. I think we've seen this before, right? It used to be called eyeballs. And then it suddenly became, the economics are important. So, I think we're right maybe a year from that point. I mean, you said something at the IBM Think event a few weeks ago. You said, "It's day zero of the AI revolution." And I think for a lot of folks, it feels further along than that. I mean, you've got, you know, trillion-dollar AI companies, and a lot of business leaders worry that they're falling behind. Does day zero mean, like, you're not too far behind, you don't have to rush too much, or what do you mean by that? Yeah, so first, let me be clear because I can sound a little bit cynical about the economics, and I actually am. That said, I think AI is an incredible productivity tool. I think those who don't take advantage of it will be perpetually disadvantaged compared to those who do. So, let me begin by saying that. It's going to optimize how you market, it's going to optimize how you write code, it's going to optimize enterprise operations, it's going to optimize how you sell, and it's going to optimize how you get your daily work done. So, there is no question about it. It's going to make a profound and deep impact on all of those things. By day zero, I mean, it's time to sit down and take it seriously. You're not in the experimentation phase. This is not like you're in high school. Day zero, the race is about to start. Put yourself in the blocks and start sprinting. But by that I mean, take three, four, five things, not a hundred, and learn how to do them at scale because that'll teach you: how do you get all your change management done? How do you get your data organized? How do you really get people motivated to change a process? So, do a few things at scale, learn how to do that really well, now do ten, and then give yourself the confidence to do the next 20. I mean, there's this expression that's used to talk about the economy these days, that it's a K-shaped economy—you know, some households do great and some don't as well. And I sometimes get the sense that when it comes to AI, we're sort of having K-shaped businesses, that like, the tech companies, folks like you are super excited, and then there are a bunch of other companies, and these may be clients of yours, I don't know, that are like, falling behind. Yeah. So, unfortunately, I think corporate performance is even more of a differentiated K. If you look at corporate performance, actually it tends to be a 20-80 rule, more of a power law. The K is really more of a 50-50, I think, if I follow the economists correctly. Here, it's more of a 20-80. And so 20% get it, they go forward, they are jumping into it, they kind of are going to get the returns. And 80% are either not getting a return or don't quite know what to do. If you're in that 80, figure out what is it that you should do, and it probably doesn't matter where you start as long as you're starting to try to do it at scale, to make a real difference to your bottom line. So, what you're saying though is like, you don't have to know what to do. Like, it's better to pick something and go than to just be like, "I'm not sure what to do." Like, I had a client walk up to me and say, "Look, I get it that I need to do it, but I don't have the right people on my team. Can you give me a deep AI expert, somebody who's kind of done their PhD in AI?" And I looked at him and I said, "Actually, I recommend we give you somebody from a domain who doesn't really know the depth of AI, but who understands the difference AI could make to your domain." So, you don't need to know what to do because those domain experts exist in every company. Find that 20% or 30% of them who are motivated to say, "I want to learn a new way to do things." So, I think curiosity and willingness to adapt are more important. I think we're getting hung up on, "I need to know AI, like a PhD in computer science." I think that's the wrong thing. Because that's for the inventors of AI. That's not needed for the deployers of AI. There's also, you know, this idea that AI is going to save me a lot of money, that it's going to be very efficient for me. So, this was my point of scaling. I would turn around and say that for our first six months to a year, we were probably spending more than we were saving. Because if you think about it, you're putting a couple of hundred engineers to work at it, and that's an incremental cost. The underlying infrastructure, aka the tokens, if you're doing it on public cloud, is an added cost. There's opportunity costs also of not doing other things with these people that could have resulted in revenue; that's a cost. Now, once we learn a rinse-and-repeat method, where you're not doing it across two or three but across 10 or 20, when you're saving a billion dollars a year, well, that's a lot more than the cost of a couple of hundred people. After year two, we were definitely getting a return that was 10x compared to what we were spending. And now at year four, we will be, I think, over five billion from a baseline of '22 spent. So, that's not an incremental five over last year; that's an incremental saving compared to our year-end '22 spending. So, that is tremendous. That is more than enough to offset any extra expense. Yeah, so I actually think that that is an edge case of how you use AI, that I think is actually wrong. Right, but you don't... I mean, as with a human worker, you have to expect that sometimes it will go wrong. It will go wrong. I kind of turn around... If I look at customer service, I think the stat, which would be a good one, is 85% of the time, the human people get it right, and 15% of the time... Don't try to venture into the underconfident range. But I mean, the AI is always confident, isn't it? No, it's not actually, you'd be surprised. Multiple models. Multiple models working, working at the, at the same time. I mean, I guess where this leads me is there, there's a lot of... It's just like humans. You put four eyes; we call it "four eyes" in software development. You put two people: one is coding, one is checking their work. It's like the models: one is doing something, the other is checking its work. And when you have two models instead of two people, does that mean that you need fewer people? I mean, that is one of the... Do you think there's going to be a lot of job displacement? I think this is therefore... So, I'll address both parts of the question because it's an "and", it's not an "or". So, you say, "Wait, that seems off." No, because this is what people are missing. If my cost of software development is going down, that means we can make products that were not economically affordable three years ago. If we can do those, we can get more revenue at an appropriate margin. So, why wouldn't I get more people? These are value-creating: sales, marketing, consulting, and development are value-creating. Then there is the 20%, I'll call it, which is what you need to run the operation. So, is it compliance? Is it accounts payable? Is it procurement? Is it all those things? It's not going to go down to zero, but I would not be surprised if about 30% of the total head count in those areas is not needed within a few years. That's the statement I had made, and I'm actually still consistent. No, but I just said we tripled our entry-level hiring. So, while there is some decrease on this side, in about 20% of the enterprise, there is a big increase in the remaining 80% of the enterprise. So, I actually think net we will have increased demand for jobs, but there is some displacement, which is always a little bit painful. And always has happened with new technology. And always has happened with new technology. What kind of responsibility do you feel like you have, and do you think other CEOs have or should have for, you know, resolving and helping to ameliorate the displacement that's inevitable when these... I mean, what tends to happen is, you know, tech folks are very excited about the future because they're beneficiaries of it, but not everyone is a beneficiary in that way. Well, actually, I think that if we can get five to ten points of productivity in every enterprise around the planet, everybody's a beneficiary. Tech may be the early, early beneficiary, but I will note everybody in tech right now is losing money on it. So, we can claim: is it going to be a long-term beneficiary or not? I think there's open questions in that. I think everybody's going to be a beneficiary. I think that in our societies, at least in the West, the responsibility of business leaders is to provide an opportunity. So, we want to help our people get upskilled; we want to help our people get reskilled. We want to open up other opportunities of jobs. We can't force them to do any of that. So, then it's on them: do they want to take advantage of those opportunities and step up to do it? And I would say that answer has always been about 50-50. Some do, but a lot of people say, "I don't want to get retrained. I want my old job." Okay, I'm sorry, that's not going to happen. And those folks, that then ends up being the responsibility of government and society, not necessarily of the business. Correct. We try to be compassionate. We don't force people out like in a day. But if over six months or nine months they are not willing to learn the appropriate skills where they're needed, that's actually bad for the other 90% who are around them. Are there, are there signs of a bubble that you see in different places? If I take all the verbal promises and we say there's 125 gigawatts of AI data centers that are going to come online in the next 2-3 years, that's 8 to 12 trillion of capex in total—not in one year, in total. That is where I come to; I don't see the economics of that at all. Because that would imply close to a trillion dollars of profit, which in the best case, means 4 trillion of more revenue. Okay, where exactly is that going to come from? The math just doesn't work. The math doesn't, doesn't, doesn't add up for you. Doesn't work. Now, will it really, really work out well for at least half of them? Yes. Some are going to thrive, but some will disappoint. So, and I don't think in a commodity world, there probably isn't space for a dozen foundation models. Is there space for 3 or 4? Probably. So, I'm going to ask you a super basic technology question, which will maybe reveal something about me but may help those in the room. What is the difference between a data center and a mainframe? I mean, aren't they both buildings with a lot of boxes in them? So, first, for the few geeks in the room, a mainframe is actually one box. But a mainframe is designed differently. When we say data centers nowadays, what people are intuitively implying is there is a collection of similar boxes—hundreds, thousands, tens of thousands, maybe hundreds of thousands of them—in a single data center. And the work is such that you can divide it up amongst all of these, and they can talk to each other if they need to collaborate. That's the network or the optics that does all that; that's a data center. A mainframe, while it could be used in that context, rarely is. A mainframe is really useful when you have one piece of work that has an incredible volume. Example: airline reservations. If we sell you the seat, we probably don't want to sell the same exact seat on the same flight to somebody else. That would be inconvenient, yeah. That would be... So, that is a different kind of workload than you asking an AI model a question, and Joe asking it a question, and me asking it a question. That can be divided up because it doesn't need to know all the three answers; it knows what it's going to do. So, the work inherently can be divided or parallelized. Part of the reason I ask is because, you know, IBM is sort of the mainframe shop, the OG mainframe shop, right? And there was a time where mainframes were sort of, seemed like they were, I don't know, passé, everything was going to the cloud, and that has like, shifted. Like, suddenly they're back—I'm sure the mainframe people don't like the idea of saying they're back, but... Remember, in 1993, there was, I think it was Time Magazine, where they were showing a mainframe dressed up as a dinosaur, and it was called "The Death of the Mainframe." That was only 34 years ago. And then every 10 years, people talk about the death of it. I think you've got to be a bit more astute: what is the workload that is great for a mainframe? What is the workload that's not good for a mainframe? And as long as you stick to that, I really am a believer in fit for purpose. The same way as a GPU is probably not ideal for running a smartphone because you kind of want your battery to last all day, not be over in three minutes. So, there is a fit for purpose that is underneath these things: where do you do AI training, that's one kind; where do you do inferencing, that's a second kind; where do you do web serving or streaming? Right. Well, and if you want to keep things secure, you want to have them on your own premises. Sovereignty also comes into play because, especially if you're outside the US, people care deeply about which government has control over the tech stack. All of those things come into play for where do you want to run things. You, IBM recently announced a $5 billion initiative called Project Lightwell to identify and fix AI vulnerabilities in the open-source world. And that was reportedly triggered by Anthropic's release of Mythos. What did you see that sparked this at that time, and what do you think people sort of misunderstand about cybersecurity overall? So, first for the good news. At least in our case from the things that we've been running for the last few months, Mythos didn't find anything that other models didn't and couldn't find. I'll call that the good news. Here's the bad news: we have a lot of people, tens of thousands, who are experts in using these models to try to find vulnerabilities and then go fix them. Okay, so for the expert, they could already do all this using other models. We will completely acknowledge that Mythos is way easier to use than the past. So, what it did do was it opens up the attack surface to where I don't need one of those hundred experts to go do it; I can now do it with somebody with average skills. So, that is definitely something to be worried about. So, when Mythos came along, it's not just Mythos; the ability of these foundation models to actually help you write code, to understand code, is also there at the same time. The same thing which could be used to exploit, we could turn around and say: can I use it to fix at least all open source? And that answer became a very quick "we can." So, we said as opposed to only worrying about, oh my god, I got all these things, and like, okay, I have my list of 10,000 of them, we said: can we do something? By the way, it's not altruistic. It is good for society, but we do intend to charge people a fair price—not a usurious price—for it, to say: can we instead turn this into a utility where people can come to us, we can be a clearinghouse, so that they can get their fix after giving us the vulnerability? But we can share to others who are inside the closed set also that, "Hey, your friend here found a vulnerability." We're not going to tell you which friend, we're not going to tell you where they're using it, so that that information is anonymized and protected, but you can actually get the same fix if you want. And, yes, we are throwing a lot of people at it, but despite throwing that many people, without using the current AI tools, it would have been impossible for us to go about saying that if you give us a piece of open source, we can actually give you what is in our belief a very well-constructed patch or fix against that vulnerability. I mean, it seems like in this AI world, sort of, cybersecurity is like: my AI has got to be better than your AI, right? Mhm. That the attackers are using. It's the old, I think it was Willie Sutton, right? "Why do you rob banks?" "Well, that's where the money is." Why are you attacking cyber infrastructure? Well, today that's where the data is, which is where the money is. That's why I began by saying the good news is that it's not really brand new. The bad news is simply it'll be done faster. So, nation-states have been doing this for decades. But you would say three or four nation-states were capable of doing it; maybe that opens up to a couple of dozen. That means more. And I guess it means organizations that might otherwise not have been targets—smaller, mid-size—the bar is... The potential function has come down, so it's easier to target more. Right, so we all have to be a little bit more prepared even if, even before we reach the scale of... I would turn around and say if you don't think you're protecting yourself, it is only a matter of time. It will come. Earlier this year, the IBM Institute for Business Value released a provocative report called "Enterprise in 2030" and setting the big bets that CEOs needed to be making. And one of those bets was about quantum computing. Now, we've talked here, most business leaders are struggling to adapt to AI. You've partnered with the US government on a new quantum foundry, investing $10 billion in a large-scale commercial quantum computer. Why go all-in on something even harder to understand and control than AI? So, let's go back to your very first question. If you can get ahead of the curve, and if what you're doing is hard enough that you actually have a couple of years' advantage, our industry, the tech industry, has shown that you can create outsized returns for yourself and outsized returns for your clients in doing that. We felt that quantum is going to be one of those. We actually came to that recognition many years ago. Then the question became: can we do the hard science it takes to be able to make progress? I would say earlier this year, we convinced ourselves of that. The evidence of that is both in our $10 billion investment, because that means we expect to see a real return on it, as well as in the government agreeing to invest, because that is a sign that they did their homework and agreed it is now time to scale this as an industry. So, I think you should think of quantum as doing the following. CPUs, we've had them for 60 or 70 years, do a lot of great problems, right? GPUs came around and did a different kind of problem. They did matrix math that allowed AI and other things to happen, but in some sense, it's not that CPUs couldn't do it—they were 10,000 times slower to do it. So, last summer, summer of '25, they could simulate a five-atom molecule. I'll be honest, a five-atom molecule a really good computational chemist, if it's a simple molecule, could probably solve by hand. Maybe an expert, but they could do it by hand. So, you'd say, "Okay, your quantum can do it, but who cares?" Last winter, November, December, they could do a 300-atom molecule. That's good progress. Now, you're getting beyond what you can do by hand, but you could do that on a normal supercomputer pretty easily. So, you say, "Okay, you still haven't told me that this is interesting." In April, they did 12,000 atoms. They're now getting into the protein realm. When you're in the 10 to 30 to 40 thousand atom range, you're in the protein realm. So, this was a piece of a protein called trypsin. We're pretty sure that in another month or two, we'll be at double that range, which means you can solve trypsin. If you can understand the properties of a protein using a few minutes of computation, you can now understand which molecule, aka a drug, may bind to it to stop its bad behavior. You've now opened up a new pathway, possibly, for health, which didn't even exist. I mean, when you give that example, like, part of the amazing part of AI has been the exponential pace that it keeps improving. And as you give that example about quantum, you're implying that that is moving at that similar kind of pace, and we're getting that much closer to not being science fiction? Correct. So, I think we're solving problems now, whether it's in... I talked about biology and molecules because I think most people intuitively get that that's a hard problem. But there are problems deep inside things like fluid dynamics, and computations to do with aerodynamics, to do with how liquids flow inside pipes. All of these are problems that are now coming right about now within the range of quantum computers to solve. Understand where quantum may be in two or three years, so understand what kind of algorithms you may need to develop, so when it is there, you don't then spend two years doing all that. That's what I would recommend to people to do today, but that makes it an easy "and". That's not a choice then. Mhm. It's not a dilemma to say: which of the two do you do? And this report from the IBV, "The Enterprise in 2030," do you know what IBM will look like in 2030? We want to be known for not just being technologically innovative—I think that we have that reputation. We weren't always great at making that easily accessible to clients. So, I think we want to be in the position where we are bringing all of our innovation to clients in a way that they can easily consume. That's one big piece. Two, we were about 20% software in 2019; we are now about 45%. I think that number will keep going up by a few percent a year, so we'll be much more in that space than anything else. I think we'll become known as one of the exemplars of how we are deploying AI and agents to not just improve our own business but to help improve our clients' business. I want to ask you one last thing here. A big focus for you is making IBM's culture more willing to take risks. For the business leaders who are here, and who are listening, who are watching at home, like, what advice do you have on how you make that adjustment? So, I would tell everybody: the most risky route is taking zero risk. What happens in any business that takes no risk? That means that you're kind of trying to extract profit, or what an economist would call rents, from what you already have. But that means you're giving everybody else the opportunity to clone you or copy you, and be innovative from the bottom, so they will pick off the most profitable parts of your business. So, now you begin to have declining profit pools. And if you're conservative by nature, you're going to invest even less because you're getting smaller, so you're going to accelerate your decline and you'll be approaching a cliff without realizing it. Go read history and see how many companies behave like that when you sit and watch them, right? It takes about five years and you begin to hit a decline, and then you're going along, and then five or ten years later, somebody comes along and either buys you up and chews you up and spits you out for parts, or you actually just go and fall over into oblivion. So, I call that—that's the most risky. So, how do you maintain enough innovation that you're actually growing? All innovation does not pay off. Innovation is risky by nature. So, you got to say: how do I manage it where I'm generating enough profit to pay for innovation, recognizing that not all of it will have a long-term return, but enough of it should? That's the nature of... I mean, people will still say like, "Yeah, I'm fine with taking a risk, as long as I don't lose anything." Humans are incredibly loss-averse. If you're given the option of you can gain $10 but lose $1, nobody wants to take that bet because they'd rather not lose $1, even though you can gain $10. Okay, so we recognize that that's human nature. That means the point of leadership in at least corporations is: how do you counteract that base human nature? Because people are not going to take risk if they think their jobs are on the line or if you chastise them in public. So, what I always go to is I want to hear a 50% probability win. I don't want the 90%, because if you tell people, "I need to be 90% certain," that's no risk. So, you have to tell people, "Hey, it's okay that one of the two times, you won't meet the deadline." So, you start building a bit of a buffer in to say, "Okay, I got it that they won't meet the deadline," but if you're doing six things and four work out, fine. And I guess you have to yourself own up to the things that maybe didn't work out the way you wanted, as you were talking about, you know, Watson maybe not working out the way IBM might have ideally wanted. Plenty of things. I mean, I can think about our digital sales channel—something we're still working on. We're probably on our fourth try in my tenure. But we're not going to give up. It didn't work out, which means you got to not just say "go harder at it." Not working out means think deeply about what is there structurally inside that didn't make it work, or is it that the market is different than what we presumed? You got to sort of pressure test all those things and keep trying. Well, Arvind, this has been great. Thank you so much for doing it. It's my pleasure. I found Arvind unexpectedly candid, from acknowledging IBM's strategic lapses with Watson to a looming AI data center bubble. And I appreciated that he could explain quantum computing's impact without getting too deep into the complex science of it all. What sticks with me most is his emphasis on risk. As he puts it, that the biggest risk is taking no risk. That's particularly true in times of tech transition, like right now. Thanks again to Arvind for joining us. I'm Bob Safian. Thanks for listening.

The scientific advances ready to change the world: the Top 10 Emerging Technologies 2026

World Economic Forum(1.1M)·56:32·559 views·2026-06-23
Kimmy BettingerLead, Expert and Knowledge Communities, World Economic Forum
TL;DRThe World Economic Forum’s 14th annual Top 10 Emerging Technologies report highlights a paradigm shift toward hyper-personalized healthcare, highly localized production, and AI-driven scientific discovery capable of solving humanity's most pressing…
infrastructure, environmental,...

TL;DR

The World Economic Forum’s 14th annual Top 10 Emerging Technologies report highlights a paradigm shift toward hyper-personalized healthcare, highly localized production, and AI-driven scientific discovery capable of solving humanity's most pressing infrastructure, environmental, and medical challenges within the next five years.

Reimagining Energy and Climate Adaptation

  • Everything-to-Grid (E2G) Energy: Converts decentralized nodes—including electric vehicles, rooftop solar, and commercial buildings—into smart, bidirectional assets that can store and feed power back to the grid.
      • *Grid Resilience:* Stabilizes grids during extreme weather-induced demand spikes and balances the massive energy workloads of modern AI data centers.
      • *Hardware Advancements:* Enabled by newer lithium and sodium battery chemistries, rapid-charging hardware, and advanced semiconductor control systems.
  • Direct Lithium Extraction (DLE): Extracts lithium from brine in hours rather than up to two years, bypassing the need for massive high-altitude evaporation ponds like those in Chile’s Atacama Desert.
      • *Supply Chain Reconfiguration:* Allows extraction and refining to be co-located, potentially smoothing out highly centralized supply chains (currently dominated by China with 62% of refining, Chile with 13%, and Argentina with 11%).
      • *Commodity Pressures:* A steep decline in lithium values (down 80% since 2022) poses an ongoing investment challenge to scaling DLE facilities.
  • Passive Radiative Cooling Materials: Electricity-free paints, coatings, and window films that cool buildings below ambient temperatures by radiating heat directly into deep space, bypassing the atmosphere.
      • *Energy Savings:* Can cut cooling energy requirements by up to 40%, offering a vital tool for climate adaptation in low-income, high-temperature countries and urban heat islands.
      • *Industrial Utility:* Extends to infrastructure protections, such as specialized power cable coatings that prevent overheating to transmit more electricity.

Paradigm Shifts in Precision Medicine

  • Personalized mRNA Cancer Vaccines: Custom-designed therapies created in a matter of weeks by analyzing unique mutations in a patient's tumor biopsy, training their immune system to target specific cancer cells.
      • *Inverting the Blockbuster Model:* Shifts pharmaceuticals away from "one-size-fits-all" chemotherapy toward highly localized, single-patient medical manufacturing.
      • *Cost and Access:* Early-stage custom treatments exceed $100,000 per patient, though hybrid off-the-shelf and personalized vaccine models are being explored to democratize access.
  • Exosome Drug Delivery: Employs the body’s natural, membrane-wrapped message carriers (exosomes) to deliver therapeutics directly to targeted diseased cells.
      • *Crossing Key Barriers:* Because the body recognizes the courier, exosome-encapsulated treatments can cross the previously impenetrable blood-brain barrier to treat Parkinson’s and Alzheimer’s, or target complex cancers.
  • Quantum Simulation for Drug Discovery: Models molecular interactions directly using quantum physics, bypassing the crude approximations used by conventional computers.
      • *Fixing the Failure Rate:* Aims to lower the current statistic where 9 out of 10 drug candidates fail during clinical trials, altering the economic calculus of drug discovery and making treatments for rare diseases commercially viable.

Advanced Computing, AI, and Chemical Engineering

  • World Models: Generative AI systems that learn directly from multi-sensory physical experiences (video, depth sensors, motion) rather than just language and text datasets.
      • *Physical Understanding:* Enables robots to navigate ambiguous factory environments autonomously and builds highly accurate, reactive climate models.
  • Lattice-based Cryptography: Utilizes multi-dimensional grids and mathematical noise to secure sensitive data in a "fog," future-proofing encryption against upcoming decryption capabilities of quantum computers.
      • *Homomorphic Encryption:* Allows organizations to perform AI analysis on raw data without ever exposing the underlying records, a technique already proven on 300,000 sensitive patient records across multiple hospitals.
  • PFAS (Forever Chemical) Destruction: Breaks the notoriously resilient carbon-fluorine bond in per- and polyfluoroalkyl substances using specialized electrical currents on electrodes, offering a permanent cleanup solution rather than simple containment.
  • Precision Fermentation: Programs microbes (like yeast) to synthesize identical proteins, fats, and chemical alternatives traditionally harvested from agriculture or fossil fuels.
      • *Planetary Boundaries:* Offers a scalable food production alternative to feed an estimated 9 billion people by 2050 without additional land, water, or greenhouse gas strain.

Macro Trends and the New Frontier of Innovation

  • The "Local" and "Personal" Themes: The 2026 cohort showcases technologies that are shrinking geographic dependencies. This is visible in grid power balanced at the neighborhood level, proteins generated in urban bioreactors, and therapies custom-compounded in local hospital wings.
  • AI Redefining the Scientific Method: AI is fundamentally shifting scientific discovery away from the standard linear pipeline of "hypothesis, experiment, and validation."
      • *Pre-Hypothesis Experimentation:* Computers now run open-ended, massive data simulations to identify unexpected molecular anomalies first, transforming physical lab work into a final verification step.
  • The Three Obstacles to Tech Scaling: WEF's 14-year retrospective reveals that breakthroughs typically stall not due to science, but to external bottlenecks:
      • *System Ecosystem Readiness:* Needs complementary systems to catch up (e.g., mRNA was discovered in 2014 but stalled until lipid nanoparticle delivery systems emerged in 2018).
      • *The "First Bet" Risk:* Requires anchor clients, governments, or well-funded companies to adopt crude, highly expensive early iterations.
      • *Regulatory and Policy Frameworks:* Standard-setting bodies must move fast enough to codify security benchmarks, such as standardizing quantum-safe encryption.

Notable Quotes

"We are starting to hear conversations about the energy demands of AI. When we are already at a point where grids can be really stressed by increasing temperatures and we have this new force coming in, it’s really important that we start to think about how we might reshape that grid." — Kimmy Bettinger
"Everyone listening to this will know someone who’s been through cancer and cancer treatment, and it always seems like cracking a walnut with a sledgehammer. It’s going to create a lot of damage." — Robin Pomeroy
"Science traditionally works on: come up with a hypothesis, run an experiment, learn from it, try another experiment. But actually, with AI, what we’re able to do is a bit of experimentation pre-hypothesis... It really shifts the way science is happening." — Kimmy Bettinger
"While precision fermentation can really offer a new way to meet our needs... we run the risk of pulling that production away from the families and the economies that have been doing that for generations." — Kimmy Bettinger

And that electricity creates heat. That heat then turns the surrounding environment hotter, which means we need even more cooling. It's the type of positive feedback loop that you really don't want. Now, with Passive Radiative Cooling, imagine a paint that cools itself. We can use it as a coating, and what it can do is it actually sends heat up directly to deep space. We bypass our atmosphere, and so it allows us to cool things without using electricity. So I really think that this is one of those technologies that has a really wide range of opportunity space. Some of it's kind of old technology as well. They're deliberately designed using that bond, the carbon-fluorine bond, so they don't break. The trouble is when you stop using them or they're polluting the environment, they don't biodegrade. Exactly. So we've found PFAS in places like the Arctic, it's in rainwater on every continent in the world, and even actually in the bloodstream of almost everyone we've tested for it. Historically we've been able to contain PFAS, but we've never been able to break that bond, that carbon-fluorine bond. The exciting thing is we now have figured out how to do that. We're able to... there are a few different approaches that we've found that can break the carbon-fluorine bond. And that's really exciting because what it means is that for, you know, communities maybe that live by contaminated land or water, we no longer just have to contain the problem, we can actually start to solve it. One of the techniques is running contaminated water across specialized electrodes. Yeah, so it uses the electrical current to strip out PFAS molecules which can then be dealt with. Interesting. So precision fermentation is a process where we can give microbes, like let's say a simple microbe like a yeast, a new set of instructions so that it can produce something that we might want, like a protein or a fat molecule or something along those lines. What these microbes are able to produce is identical to what we might get from the other, kind of more traditional ways of doing that. So for example, we can get a whey protein that would be identical to what we would get from a cow. Which is really interesting, in particular when we think about one of the challenges we're facing. So your cells make a tiny packet—kind of a membrane-wrapped packet—that they use to send messages back and forth to each other. And with exosome drug delivery... So this is every cell—we know what a cell is—has an exosome, it's a part of the cell, and its function, or one of its functions, is to deliver information. Yep, yep, exactly. And this is exciting because there are certain places in the body that have been really hard for drugs to reach because we have kind of barriers around them for protection reasons. What we're seeing is, you know, science traditionally works on the kind of come up with a hypothesis, run an experiment on it, learn from it, try another experiment. But actually, with AI, what we're able to do is do a bit of experimentation pre-hypothesis. We're able to ask really, really broad, open questions and develop our hypothesis from that, so that then, typically, the experiment phase becomes a bit more of a validation stage. So it really shifts the way science is happening. And as we think about, okay, the next 14 years of this report, what does that mean? What might innovation look like? We're starting to consider where, where kind of we'll start... where we'll see resources going in science. And for us, maybe that's more, you know, maybe that's more around the models, the compute, and the talent to do this type of work. And as we think about kind of where science happens and how science happens, who's able to deliver on that? Maybe, maybe that is a bit more of the private sector, or or kind of, you know, how can academia build into that? So I think we'll start to see some big shifts in the landscape there as well. So things are speeding up, but also there are fundamental changes to how innovation is even dreamt up in the first place. Kimmy Bettinger, thanks so much for joining us on Radio Davos. Building Startup Cities - Balaji Srinivasan | #84

New Founding Podcast(12.4K)·116:51·572 views·2026-06-23
Nathan HalberstadtHost, New Founding Podcast Balaji SrinivasanEntrepreneur, Investor & Author Nate FischerCo-Founder, New Founding
TL;DR1.
TL;DR Faced with declining societal trust, failing centralized governance, and a looming $193.6 trillion US fiscal crisis, builders are moving away from traditional mega-cities to pioneer opt-in "startup cities" and "network states." By leveraging cryptographic social...

1. TL;DR

Faced with declining societal trust, failing centralized governance, and a looming $193.6 trillion US fiscal crisis, builders are moving away from traditional mega-cities to pioneer opt-in "startup cities" and "network states." By leveraging cryptographic social contracts, decentralized real estate investment trusts (REITs), and off-grid modular technologies, these new communities are restoring self-governance and economic alignment through peaceful competition.

***

The Rise of Startup Cities and Opt-In Societies

  • The Flight from One-Party States: Balaji Srinivasan argues that major blue states like California have devolved into non-consensual, one-party systems that fail to deliver results.
      • Exit as Voice: To restore true democracy, citizens are "voting with their feet" by moving to alternative startup societies rather than trying to fix broken centralized political systems.
  • The Ridgerunner Creed: Josh Abbotoy describes his Tennessee-based project, Ridgerunner LLC, as a neighborhood-level effort centered on traditional Appalachian living and a simple ethos: "faith, family, freedom."
      • Fostering Autonomy: Ridgerunner aims to create spaces (churches, schools, community centers) where families can escape hyper-consumerism and build self-determining, high-trust environments.
  • Techno-Democracy and On-Chain Consent: Srinivasan proposes formalizing physical community entry through digital, "social smart contracts" at the border.
      • Contractual Governance: By signing a public, cryptographic terms-of-service agreement to enter a community, residents make an active, visible commitment to uphold communal rules, combining left-libertarian consent with right-libertarian contract.
  • The Network School Model: Located on a man-made island near Singapore, Srinivasan's Network School serves as a physical-meets-digital hub where 300 founders and builders from over 100 countries co-live and co-work for $1,500 a month.
      • Open-Source Playbook: The school is a practical test case for the "Network State" theory, aiming to open-source its templates so other communities globally can replicate its success.

Quantifying Trust and "Skin in the Game"

  • The Collapse of Social Trust: Nate Fischer emphasizes that high-trust environments are rare and highly valuable commodities in an era of failing centralized institutions.
      • The Root of Decay: Mass democracy fails because a growing share of the population lacks "skin in the game," voting instead for short-term handouts that deplete social capital.
  • Time as Skin in the Game: Fischer highlights physical proximity and shared time as foundational social technologies. Regularly showing up to local institutions (like churches or community groups) builds a reputational credit reservoir that online-only spaces struggle to replicate.
  • The "Man-Month" Metric: To measure community depth, Srinivasan introduces the concept of multiplying the number of people by the duration of their stay.
      • Proven Scale: The Network School has generated nearly 500 "man-years" of co-living over 18 months, proving that virtual networks can successfully transition into dense, high-trust physical interactions.
  • Rebuilding the Web of Trust: Modern communities can utilize Web3 cryptographic technology to map decentralized identity. Unlike AI-generative systems, cryptography cannot be faked, allowing physical communities to safely vet and onboard members using verified on-chain references.

Solving the "Upside vs. Utility" Dilemma in Real Estate

  • The Network Orchestration Challenge: Startup cities face a classic cold-start problem: a new town with 100 people lacks the utility (stores, restaurants, schools) of a mega-city like Singapore or New York.
  • The Crypto REIT Solution: To incentivize early adopters to take the risk of moving to a low-utility community, Srinivasan suggests issuing "crypto equity" or shares in a community-wide REIT.
      • Aligning Incentives: Just as early equity offset WhatsApp's initial lack of utility and token airdrops grew Bitcoin's global network, giving early pioneers a financial stake in the *entire* community's growth mitigates NIMBYism and spurs rapid collective development.
  • Land Ownership vs. Shared Equity: Abbotoy and Fischer note that while tokenized REITs solve rent-seeking, traditional land ownership remains highly attractive. Owning 20 to 30 acres in Appalachia gives families a profound sense of sovereignty and agency, acting as a physical hedge against urban instability.
  • The Solar and Modular Singularity: Rapid advancements in modular construction and cheap, shatterproof solar energy are paving the way for off-grid, cost-effective villages.
      • Modular Levittowns: Communities can deploy cheap, high-quality residential, gym, and church units quickly, reducing the cost of housing to the price of a car while reducing vulnerability to state infrastructure failures.

Macro Geopolitics: America's $193.6 Trillion Debt and Global Realignment

  • The True US Fiscal Crisis: Srinivasan highlights a devastating metric from the US Government's Fiscal Year 2025 Financial Report: $193.6 trillion in unfunded Medicare and Social Security liabilities (calculated in present-value terms).
      • Rapid Debt Accrual: This represents a massive jump from $175.3 trillion in 2024, equating to roughly $600,000 in debt per citizen, or $6 million per top taxpayer.
  • De-Dollarization and Military Retreat: Historically, US profligacy was offset by global dollar dominance and the military projection of 750 global bases. This system is breaking down due to the "precision strike revolution" (drones and cheap missiles making US carriers and bases sitting ducks).
      • Shrinking Tax Base: As the US military withdraws and allies seek alternative security arrangements, the true tax base for "dollar inflation" is shrinking rapidly, paving the way for domestic price spikes and energy crises.
  • The Return of Historical Parity: For thousands of years, the Eurasian landmass held economic and military parity. The post-WWII unipolar American hegemony was a historical anomaly. With Russia, China, and India embracing market dynamics over socialism/communism, the global economy is reverting to its historical, multipolar baseline.

Cultural Cohesion and the "Code and Cloud" Identity

  • The Shift from Market to Competitor: Srinivasan observes that since 2024, Western tech elites have undergone a psychological shift. They no longer view rising global talent (particularly from India and China) as a vast market to capture, but as direct competition.
  • Code and Cloud vs. Blood and Soil: In response to rising ethnic and nationalistic tensions within the US, Srinivasan defines his allegiance by class rather than race or geography. He identifies as a "code and cloud" person rather than a "blood and soil" nationalist.
      • Peace and Trade: Rather than engaging in political or cultural conflict, the goal of the network state movement is to foster peaceful, mutually beneficial trade between distinct, self-governing cultural nodes (e.g., "apple farmers trading with orange farmers").

***

Notable Quotes

"We are restoring democracy by having people have the consent of the governed... If you have cooperation and if you have consent and you have contract, you don't have coercion." — Balaji Srinivasan
"Mass democracy fails because many people... don't have any meaningful skin in the game in a sense. They're voting, they're often voting for something that is pure handouts... If you essentially have your society run by people who are just trying to take from other people, you're inevitably going to destroy the trust that exists in society." — Nate Fischer
"When you own 30 acres in Appalachia, you are functionally like a king on those 30 acres. You have a feeling of sovereignty. There's so much that you can do that nobody can stop you from doing and you have real agency and freedom." — Josh Abbotoy
"I don't want to fight other Americans... Just like the English who left for Massachusetts and left for Virginia, they didn't want to spend their lives dying in the English Civil War... Instead, the smart guys, the proto-Americans, exited and built their own thing." — Balaji Srinivasan

So I think that one—there's kind of a few factors, and trust has been, and what it takes to build high-trust communities has been, a central theme of New Founding since I started it. Really, I started it in response to what I saw as some major disruptive trends, one of those being the decline in societal trust, the decline in institutional trust. So, if you think of us, we're a venture firm, we are looking to solve problems and we're looking to build successful businesses and make money. And if you see societal trust declining—really one of the sort of major, most significant trends of our era—then you're going to recognize that trust has a lot of value. So if you do have trust, then that is something that doesn't just have desirability, it probably has growing market value. It's probably going to be used in new ways. So there's going to be where trust exists, it may find new uses where, let's say, previously something was done via a centralized institution that failed. I look at this sort of at the broad level, both at the local level and at the abstract level. And I think that it's very hard to build trust. I think to maintain trust, skin in the game is one of the critical functions. And I think that's really what—to me, that's why mass democracy fails. Mass democracy fails because many people, and in many cases sort of growing shares of the population, don't have any meaningful skin in the game, in a sense. They're voting; they're often voting for something that is pure handouts. And I know Mitt Romney got in a lot of trouble for saying that. I'm not trying to be a popular politician running for elective office, but I think that's a—it was recognized by the founders. I mean, it's been recognized by people going back to the Greeks. So if there's no skin in the game, if you essentially have your society run by people who are just trying to take from other people, you're inevitably going to destroy the trust that exists in society. I think Taleb does a very good job talking about this—Nassim Taleb—this concept of skin in the game and how baked in it is to almost every aspect of any sort of stable institution. So, I think the question is: Where does that exist, and where can you build that? And I think it's interesting to see crypto attempts to build that. I mean, going back to the lack of skin in the game, the internet is losing—the internet was in some sense a higher-trust environment when it was dominated by cultural norms that allowed a free internet to exist. Well, in a world of bots and the global internet, there's going to be no default of trust because there's no skin in the game. It costs nothing to send an email to someone, so you're going to get unlimited spam email. So we're going to have to see a world reorganized around networks that actually have some degree of trust. And I think it's interesting to build new ones in many cases. It's also interesting to look at communities and institutions that still do maintain trust. So I look at, for instance, churches, and it's the kind of thing where if everyone goes to church every week on Sunday, there's a level of proximity, there's a level of physical time together, there's a level of skin in the game that maintains something that is often lacking in other parts of society. So you look at that as a node, and I think that is a node that has essentially the ingredients for trust. It probably has a degree of trust. How do you look at that community and build on something that remains there, that exists, and build sort of more and more functions around it—functions that might have been stripped away by distant institutions? Fraternal organizations, people who voluntarily get together on a regular basis. I think in some sense there's—one of the classic forms of skin in the game is time together. If I put time in with you—there's actually a line, I think it's by Bagehot about England in the 1800s. And I think he said something like: There may be no great virtue in a man in the city, in the City of London, going to the same club at the same time every Thursday for 20 years, but it makes him a more reliable credit risk. And again, there may be no great virtue, but there's skin in the game. Right? He has gone there, he's gone to that club, he's invested his time in it, and if he turns around and he does something that hurts his reputation, he's essentially surrendered the product, the reputation that was earned over a lot of time in that club. That skin in the game is essentially building up trust that he is then able to expend potentially—which is not usually what people use trust for, but he can certainly leverage that trust by essentially using it to get credit and then stewarding that credit well. And I think that principle can be applied to almost everything. So you look at societies, you look at physical startup societies, and when you move there, when you buy property there, you are putting some skin in the game in a way that is costly if you choose to do things that go against the norms of that community. If you go into an online community, I think online communities face—I think one reason many of them have struggled is because there was often very little meaningful skin in the game. They were too easily opt-in and opt-out. And if it's too easy to opt-in and opt-out, then there's not going to be a lot of trust there. Whereas if people are actually putting something significant in, then they've built up a reservoir of trust that means that the community can actually behave in high-trust ways. So a lot of what I think about is the dynamics related to creating—to, in many cases, identifying existing pockets of trust and how do we build on those, how do we essentially build more and more actions in society, trust-dependent actions around those in a world where the institutions that have provided those functions are losing their credibility. So, I love this. I've got a bunch of things—I've been collecting links while you've been talking. So I want to just show a few quick visuals that might help think about this quantitatively and help with, basically, this broader project, right? So the first is—here is a visual that I made five years ago. Let me show you the one I made five years ago, and then let me show you the one recently. Not five years ago, almost 15 years ago, 2013. So this shows cloud formations taking physical shape. So this is people, okay: 2, 10, 100, 1,000, 10,000, 100,000 people meeting for a day, a month, a year, 10 years. And a way of quantifying this whole thing is the number of man-months that are spent in that community, right? So take the number of people down this axis, take the amount of time on this axis, multiply it together, and that's a single scalar measure. It's like the number of coins times the price per coin is CoinMarketCap. This is like the number of people times the amount of time they're spending in a society is the number of man-months, right? And you can see that what we're trying to do with Network School and with any startup society is we started with a pop-up, which is like 100 people for a month. Then V2 was 100 people for a year. We completed that. We're actually about to complete V3, which is about 1,000 people for a year. Just to quantify that, we're closing in on 500 man-years at Network School, okay? What that means is we've had thousands of people come and if you multiply by the duration that they've stayed, right, we have almost 500 man-years, which is a lot for something that started 18 months ago, right? And that's, I think, a really useful metric for us. So, as you said, Nate, in-person meeting is just necessary to build trust. It's not sufficient, but it's necessary, right? Hang out in person, doing things in person, because in a sense trust is like a credit score, right? Do I trust you to not default? Do I trust you to not defect, right? Do I trust you to cooperate even when it might be in somebody's short-term interest to defect? That's not something that can easily be spent down, and it takes a long time to build it up, and often it can't be rebuilt with the same person; it has to be rebuilt with others. Another related thing is we need to build that on the internet. There's this concept from the late '90s called "web of trust." Do you guys see the screen there? Web of trust basically says: I've got a friend, here's a stranger, here's another stranger, but my friend's friend trusts them, so therefore I can give some reduced level of trust to them, for example, right? And there's a lot of math you can do on things like this when you've got large networks. But this is a way of saying, okay, for example—and this is not everything, obviously, but it's one thing—person A, your friend, has a commercial relationship with person B that you can see some of it on-chain for months or years, and therefore you have some reason—not 100% trust but not 0%—if person A vouches for person B, you might say, "Okay, I'm going to let them into our community on a trial basis," right? The great thing about that is, because it's crypto, it's unfakeable on the internet. You can't—like, AI can solve partial differential equations but not cryptographic equations. So that means that you can have people bring their kind of—and crypto is certainly not the only way of doing this, there's other ways of doing this as well, but it's one of the best ways from a scalable, internet-first perspective. You rebuild trust by lots of micro-interactions. It's like, A hangs out with B and they're cool, right? A hangs with B and C, and then maybe person D takes in 10 bucks from 20 people and goes and organizes a pizza party and pulls it off well. That's what a mayor used to be, right? The mayor was just the guy who you trusted to crowdfund things. It wasn't like he was taking a slice off the top; he was just—he's like the fraternity president, you know, right? Like, what the old-school mayor was: you had a settlement of 100 or a thousand people, and it wasn't like some prestigious role, it was actually a pain in the butt, you know, to be that kind of guy, right? You'd just collect—you know, maybe it's the tithes at the church, for example, right? And so where does the crypto part come in on this? Well, I think that if people are owning individual homes, you might get nimbyism all over again, okay? Where, depending on where it is, people are only incentivized on their home as opposed to at the community level. An alternative thought is if they instead own shares in your REIT—now they're incentivized to grow the community as a whole, as opposed to everything just coming to their selfish little street or what have you, right? So in a sense, you actually realign things by restoring one level of indirection. They're shareholders in the REIT, and then they want the overall Ridge Runner or New Founding to grow anywhere, as opposed to just their piece of real estate there. And it doesn't matter if they have 500k in a home versus 500k in a REIT from there; in fact, they might have more upside in the REIT, right? They have less downside in the REIT because it's also distributed across things—there's a lot more scalability there than, I think, owning a home directly.
