Like, net-net, somehow it's getting done, and again, I'm not saying it's China, I'm not saying it's as organized as that, I'm not saying it has converged with the rest of the world, but I can show you graph after graph where it's converging, right? And the way I think about this, you know, I'll show one more graph and then—this article has a lot of charts, I'm just going to walk you through it just to give my mental model, and then we'll talk about Indians specifically, as opposed to India, right, people of Indian origin. And again, I've said this before, but I really do think of myself as minimum necessary Indian. It's almost as if I was born, I don't know, Bob Salvatore, okay? And Bob Salvatore in America found that suddenly Italy was doing really well. And actually, there are a lot of Italians who are online and they want to build tech companies and so on. And they admire him because he was one of the first who made it to America, right? That's roughly like Balajistras, like Bob Salvatore or something like that. That's roughly how I think of myself, where, wow, the old world just woke up. That's interesting. Oh, they want to cooperate and build cool things? Okay, I'll talk to them, just like I'll talk to Americans, right? That is essentially how I think about it. In the same way, I'll talk to Europeans. You saw, you know, the Ukrainians and Russians here, all these other kinds of people here. I'll talk to people from all around the world, right? Now, by the way, India does have that tradition as well. India is like 1.4 billion people. The whole point of India is—okay, I'll say something. This is slightly controversial. You've probably noticed Indians don't do well at the Olympics, right? They don't have that much muscle mass or height or sprinting ability and so on. There are all kinds of reasons for this. There's probably biochemical, genetic reasons in part because subcutaneous muscle mass is actually expensive metabolically, and the subcontinent had a lot of starvation periods. But I think part of it is also India has a tradition of non-violent dispute resolution. You know, Hinduism, Jainism, dharma—less about the battle-axe, more about negotiation, peace, trying to figure out how a bunch of people of different races and religions near each other could somehow still get along, right? I think that's actually a useful way of thinking. The internet is a lot like that, too. You've got all these different clusters of people. How do you get along? How do you get a win-win? Somehow, by the way, before the modern era, both China and India had amazing genetic business models. That is to say, they supported enormous populations without any welfare or Western aid. They had something going on. You know, other regions of the world weren't like that—they didn't have massive populations before the modern era. Marco Polo wanted to get to China because it had a real economy. Why did Columbus risk his life to get to the Americas? He wanted to get to India because it was a place to trade. The path to India was being blocked by the Turks in 1492. That's why Columbus tried to sail the ocean blue. That's why the Native Americans are named Indians. So India in the past—let me show you some graphs—was not actually total losers, right? Let me show you, like this chart over here. Do you see this one? "2,000 years of economic history in one chart." Yeah, so India and China, depending on how you measure, used to be basically the center of the world economy because they just had giant populations. And supporting giant populations was non-trivial before the Industrial Revolution. They had social technology that worked. Very roughly, China managed all those people in a centralized way, and India managed all those people in a decentralized way. Very roughly, Sinic centralization, Dharmic decentralization, though that's an oversimplification. And then with the Industrial Revolution, of course, you'd never take anything away from Europe or the West—they developed all this technology at that period, they got way ahead, and now actually things are sort of coming back. This axis over here, by the way, this is like thousands of years over here, and this is like tens of years. So the period we've all grown up in is an extremely unusual period in world history. If you look at it on this graph, for example, for thousands of years, there was rough military-economic parity across the Eurasian supercontinent. That's why the Huns and the Mongols could invade Europe, and that's why the Arabs could take Andalusia. That's why, as I said, Marco Polo sought out China and Columbus sought out India. It wasn't just total Western dominance. After the Industrial Revolution, it was. And then, with all these wonder weapons, the Westerners blew each other up. And then you had this extremely unusual period in 1950 of everybody else having blown themselves up. America was the most at peace, it got to write the rules of the world order, like the UN and the World Bank and so on. And then that period of peak America continued until let's say 1991, the unipolar moment. And once the rest of the world went back to capitalism—Russia went capitalist, Eastern Europe went capitalist, China went capitalist (or market economy at least), Vietnam went market economy, India went market economy—then the world economy rocketed back out faster than it's ever come. And so, only with this context, I think, can we see that all of MAGA is infeasible. You can't make America this great again because this is the trajectory of literally billions of people and trillions of dollars across a thousand years. And there's a U-shape where the world is reverting back to the way it was, where there was rough military-economic parity across the Eurasian supercontinent. Basically, these people are—like, Persians aren't dumb. Russians aren't dumb. Chinese aren't dumb. And I would argue there are a lot of Indians who are not dumb. They're actually capable. They were just under a terrible ideology of socialism and the 20th-century political systems that held them back, just like Cubans were held back. Just like North Koreans are held back. Just like, you know, white people in Eastern Europe and Russia were held back, right? And so, that's my macro-macro model. And now we have the question of reputation and so on, and why all of this anger and so on is happening now. So I'm gonna give an answer that may not... I don't know, maybe you'll argue with, but here is my macro answer. If you think of China, China was just sort of grinding away for decades after Deng Xiaoping turned it into a market economy from 1979 to roughly 2015. And they were actually doing very, very well. They had disrupted the Republicans. They had disrupted basically the manufacturing blueprint. In fact, I can show this over here; let me show you this. Essentially what happened was in the late 2000s, or rather early 2010s, China disrupted the Republicans by taking over manufacturing, and the internet disrupted the Democrats by, by taking over media, okay? So if you think about those two graphs—China disrupting the Republicans and the internet disrupting the Democrats—everything that's happened over the last 15 years follows from that. What's my point? My point is that over that period, starting in 2015, Trump reacted to China. And China, which no one had cared about prior to that point, suddenly became "China, China, China, China." Everybody's been yelling about China for the last 10 years. This escalated all the way up to confrontation over Taiwan. Both the Democrats and the Republicans started getting into it in Biden's term, where they tried to put China in a box in terms of economics. They tried to say, "You're not going to have the most advanced chips, and we're going to encircle you militarily with the Quad, and we're going to impose tariffs," and so on and so forth. Ultimately, that underestimated China because it's a great civilization, and it was able to build its own chips. It got around all of the tariffs and the bans, and now it's got DeepSeek, it's got its robots, and it's got all this kind of stuff. Fundamentally, the bet was that if the American market is cut off, China is totally screwed. What China actually did is they just expanded all their trade to the rest of the world. They built up all these markets with the Belt and Road, and they don't need America. Like America, basically, they make everything; they're autarkic. And so that was 10 years of basically fighting China, okay? And now that that didn't work, there's another group that has risen over the last whatever number of decades from total destitute poverty, and that is Indians, okay? Right? The difference is China is a state, and so it's all basically in China, though there is a Chinese diaspora. But Indians are more like a network, where there are people of Indian origin in every major Western country. They actually have, by some measure, the highest income and education across all different Western countries. Obviously, that's a huge shock. Why is, you know, this random guy... why is my boss suddenly Sundar or something like that? Why isn't it Sam, right? Of course, that feels like a total shock, especially when people are having bad economic times. A lot of people will feel, "Oh, that job was just given to this person. They're a loser. They shouldn't have it. Cut off the jobs. Cut off the H-1Bs. Send them home. They're not going to make it without America," and so on and so forth. This is very similar to the argument that China wasn't going to be able to make it without America, right? I don't think that's actually true. I think we're going to see, to give a very macro thesis, that the internet has turned everything into reading and writing. Chinese people and Indian people have a multi-thousand-year history of reading and writing. And so, this century is actually a less physically violent century and a more internet- and tech-focused century. So I don't actually think that even cutting off flows of talent to America is actually going to affect the rise of Indians as a group writ large. I think the internet is going to mean Indians are going to continue to rise. That doesn't mean it's a lose-lose. That's the thing I want to try to communicate, right? If you had an island and you have a thousand apple farmers there, and another island rises out of the ocean or it stops being communist and it's got a thousand orange farmers, those apple and orange farmers can trade, and you can have apples and oranges together, right? It is good when lots of people turn away from socialism as India has done, or turn away from communism in part as Vietnam has done. That's good because you've got new productive people who don't have a zero-sum model of the world. Right now, we have a transitional phase where all of these people are coming out. Now, to be clear, am I saying every Indian is good? No. There's another piece of this, by the way, which was actually the analogy I was trying to make on that other podcast—or I did make, it just got, you know... The late 1800s saw the technological shock of the Industrial Revolution, German unification in 1871, and Jewish emancipation, where Jews came out of the shtetl and started joining the modern world. The unified Germany and emancipated Jewish community were late arrivals relatively to the whole English, French, and Spanish age of colonization and the Industrial Revolution. That's not as well appreciated by Americans—how relatively late it was. That's why there are relatively few German colonies; for example, Namibia is like one of the only German colonies, right? Germans were fighting each other, and they only got their act together after Bismarck. Similarly, the Jewish community was not that prominent in the 1700s. It was only after emancipation that they started modernizing and becoming part of the modern world. So the problem is the combination of that Industrial Revolution shock and the German and Jewish community talent shock led to a lot of fighting in the 20th century. Because all these things rose at the same time, all kinds of resentments arose, and the world order had to reshape to accommodate this, and so on and so forth, okay? I see something very similar happening now, where you have the information shock of the internet revolution, and you have these two talent shocks where China stopped being communist and India stopped being socialist. And so these giant populations have now entered... they've stopped hitting themselves on the head with hammers. They've stopped, you know, practicing stupid ideologies of communism and socialism. They started engaging in the market economy, and they're not all total retards. Yeah, sure, okay, some of them, yes, but not all. And so, as a consequence, these are great civilizations that are now coming back again, but it's a huge shock. It's like going to the gym and suddenly your muscles start bursting your clothes, or like new things being onboarded into the world economy. My hope is that this is done with the minimum conflict possible, and we get to a stage where there's a thousand apple farmers and a thousand orange farmers and we can have peace and trade. India and Indians are much less of a military threat. Like, China you can argue is a military threat. India is not a military threat. Indians are not a military threat; they're a non-violent people in general. That's what Hinduism is. With that said, I will make another argument, which is that I will always take Gandhi over Hitler. Why? Well, for all the obvious moral reasons, but even from a, quote, "rightist" standpoint, a nationalist right standpoint. Hitler started with an independent Germany—and for all the people who are posting Hitler memes online just to engage with them in their own language or whatever—Hitler started with an independent Germany, and it turned into a colony of basically the Soviets and Americans. Gandhi started with a colony and turned it into an independent country. The non-violent approach was actually better than the violent approach, even just judged on the far-right's own terms, okay? And so, my view is, with minimum necessary confrontation, with maximum understanding, peace, and trade, we can build this kind of English-speaking world, right? I mean, the British have worked with Indians for a long time. It's less of a talent shock to them; it's not like this completely novel thing, "Oh my God, Indians!" whereas with Americans, the surge has been a recent thing. One last thing I'll say is all these people who are arguing on the internet are arguing on the internet, not in America. That's a really core thing. It's not like the people who are waving the flag... First of all, a lot of them are actually Pakistanis who hate Indians, right? And it's funny, they're kind of suicide-bombing Indians. Why? Because they just assume, "Hey, if I just say all brown people are stupid and smelly or whatever," most people aren't even thinking that Pakistan exists when they're saying that. So they'll take down themselves by, you know... There was actually a Pakistani field marshal who said something like, "India relative to us is like a fancy car and we're a dump truck, so we can just crash into them over and over again and take more dents." Okay, right? Relative to Pakistan, okay? So that's funny, right? A fair amount of this is actually people... if you saw the face of everybody where they were posting and you saw the location they're posting from (Nikita added some of this), I think you'd have a very different impression of who was spreading hatred against whom or what have you, right? How organic this is versus how inorganic. There's one more dimension to this, which is that up until 2024, India was considered part of the Quad to contain China, right? India, Australia, Japan, and America to contain China. So a lot of anti-Indian sentiment was repressed because India was a military ally, and so there was a left-right consensus on downplaying that, censoring that, and so on and so forth. And then, to my surprise, a lot of the resentment just burst out after 2024. Net-net, I think, you know, I've said the positive things about India and Indians; let me give all the negative things. Absolutely, there's all kinds of dirt that needs to be cleaned up, all kinds of streets that need to be cleaned. I think there's a lot of progress. I can show tons of graphs on this, everything from pipes and stuff like that getting there. I think Indians should improve aesthetics in every sense of the term. All of those things are very good things to say, but those all kind of cost money, and you needed money to get there, right? Like, if you look at the difference between Russians in the '50s and '60s versus the glamorous Russians of today, that's all production values, right? Like orthodontics and hair and all that kind of stuff. You look at the Chinese of the '80s and their teeth then versus now, and it's like a total night and day, right? So Indians have to get wealthier before they can improve their aesthetics. I'm not saying they're all going to be tall and jacked and so on, but I think they can improve, you know? I think they will because things like retatrutide, the GLP-1s, we can actually have. I'm not saying it's in the water supply, but it can become much more common, and so you have much more fit people. I'll show you actually a visual right after this. Hold on, let me show you something. One last thing I'll show you. You know that thing about RFK's MAHA stuff? I don't agree with everything he says, but you know, about how the diet is killing Americans and so on and so forth? You know all that stuff? Yeah, right? So, if all those carbs are bad for Americans... if socialism is bad for Americans, it's terrible for Indians. If sugar is bad for Americans, it's horrible for Indians. And let me show you some fun visuals from a hundred years ago, okay? This is basically photos from before the advent of all kinds of sugary stuff in India. Hold on, let me show you something. So this, "Men of Kerala, 1920s," okay? Look at that guy's jawline. Look at that guy, that guy, or that guy, right? Like, those guys are pretty based-looking guys, right? That was like the pre-carb era. I'm not saying they all look like that or whatever, but I am saying that I think there's potential there if you radically change the diet, and so on and so forth. Net-net is I think India and Indians should... You know how leftists will say, "Oh my god, you're fat-shaming, you're so evil, I want to cancel you," and so on and so forth, right? I think there is a sense in which you can take a volley of inbound criticism, filter out the racial slurs and all that kind of stuff, and then list it as a set of things to self-improve on, okay? I mean legitimate criticism as opposed to... because you'll get a volley. It's very much like how you guys process customer support, right? There will be some customer who's cursing at you about this, that, and the other, but there's also a real issue, which is that this part of the website is broken. So you filter out all the curses, you get the part about the website being broken, and you're like, "Okay, you know what? That's a real thing. Let's go and fix that." And there's a hundred fixes like that that India and Indians need to do. But nobody even cared about India and Indians 20, 10, or 30 years ago, right? It's only that they've risen enough that they're in the conversation now. So to summarize with a TL;DR: A) I think, if you take the long view, this is actually a return of India and China, not coming out of nowhere. B) I don't think it's going to be stopped by blocks on talent—Indian talent—any more than China was stopped by blocks on Chinese trade. C) Nevertheless, I think we can and should have peace and trade. D) As I said, I'm like the minimum necessary Indian. You know, I'm a tech guy. E) I do think India has something to offer the world. Yoga and meditation are very popular, but I think the non-violent dispute resolution of Hinduism has something to say for kind of the world we're entering with all of these different groups. And so yeah, that's kind of how I think about it. Hopefully, that's like a moderate kind of position, right? I don't think saying something positive about one group requires denigrating another group. I think lots of groups—every group—has positive qualities. That's how I think about it. Let me pause there. No, this is... it's great, it's really interesting. And I think to close this out, maybe I'll kick it over to Nate here, but I think the point that stands out most to me is actually the sort of opportunity for building communities and creating the conditions for positive-sum games and also for, you know, mutually beneficial trade and such between these different communities and groups. And I think in a lot of ways that's been a part of the project with Ridge Runner and also New Founding, and many other things happening in the US. But I don't know, Nate, if there's anything you would close us with here. So, I think this is interesting, and in many ways, I think these geopolitical trends are things that it's critical to be cognizant of. I think there are intense debates that can be had about relative trajectories and things like that. The reason I'm doing what I'm doing with New Founding is I think that the things we absolutely can control—the aspect of MAGA, in many ways as you say, certain aspects of MAGA you would say may not be able to return—they reflected the US sort of hegemonic dominance of the 1950s. I think another core theme is cultural cohesion, that ability to have a functioning society. And even if a lot of that reverses, we still in many ways will be materially richer than we were in the 1950s, so we should be able to have many aspects of the lifestyle that people achieved. And so, I think there is tremendous value in understanding the social technologies, the social norms (you could think of them as social technologies), and combining them with new technologies. How do we understand social and political currents? How do we combine those with disruptive technologies like the internet and digital technology? And how do we think about what it looks like to build functioning, desirable, high-trust communities that are capable of engaging in that sort of mutual interaction? So, I think there's a lot of value in these, and we could obviously have hours more conversation about many of the predictions. My view is I try to consider a range of possible scenarios, and any time there's a reasonable prediction, how do we build societies that are resilient to that? So I find these conversations very interesting, and I find them very valuable when directed toward productive building projects. Like, I think really all of these startup communities or network state efforts can be then... Any new theory, as provocative as it may be, adds to the value because it gives us something to prepare for and be resilient to. Yes, and you know, I want to say something else actually just on this point, which is: obviously, I'm not a Mayflower American, I'm not an Englishman, and your culture is not my costume. I wouldn't do cultural appropriation like that, because I recognize basically that America was founded by Anglos of a very specific kind—actually, different kinds of Anglos from England. And over time, there were Polish and other kinds of folks who came, and all these different waves of immigration came. My time in America was basically 40 years versus 250 years. And so, the thing that was founded 250 years ago... if you say, "Who was Google employee number one, right? Who were Google employees number one through a hundred? Who are the founding fathers one through a hundred?" Yes, like, was there a Civil War Srinivasan? No, there wasn't, right? Do I want to be part of the Second Civil War, Srinivasan? I don't want that either; I want to be on the other side of the world, right? But I also don't want you guys to fight, or other people. I want peace and trade. And the thing is, though, I will say this: you know the Vivek tweet from December 2024 that got people so mad across the political spectrum? Let me discuss that now for a second. Disclosure: I am friendly with Vivek, and I disagree with him on lots of things, but I am friendly with him. What was interesting about that tweet is it was a Rorschach test in a few ways. From an immigrant parent's standpoint, that tweet sounded exactly like basically what every Asian or Indian parent tells their kids, which is, "I believe in you. Work hard, you can improve," right? On that Vivek tweet, just to talk about it, it came at a time when 20 million people on the left had left for BlueSky. Vivek's role during 2023 to 2024 was to be the brown guy who could say what white guys further to his right couldn't say. After the Trump election in 2024, he was no longer useful in that role, and 20 million people moved to BlueSky. So, a whole center of mass moved over to the right. And so, therefore, what he thought was actually a centrist or even center-right kind of thing was perceived as actually being on the left because the whole political spectrum had shifted over, right? But the deep point about the criticism of Vivek at the time was, "Who is he to lead us?" meaning "Who is he to lead white Americans?" or something implicitly—Americans, or white Americans, or white American men, or whatever, however you want to classify heritage Americans. "Who is he to lead?" And you know what? I never felt that I had the legitimacy to do that at the cultural level, right? Other people feel differently, or whatever—fine, and that can all be worked out. However, for anything that was founded 250 months ago, 250 weeks ago, or 250 days ago, like Google or Amazon or any of the tech companies (which are genuinely world-historical things), there are plenty of people of Indian origin and Chinese origin and all around the world who have legitimate roles there. No one is going to say Sundar isn't a real Googler. No one is going to say Satya Nadella is not legitimately at Microsoft, right? Who decides who's legitimately at Microsoft? Bill Gates does, right? Who decides who's legitimately at Google? Larry Page does, right? They built it, and they have the authority to decide. Look at all the recent tech founders; a lot of those guys are of Indian origin. Of course, a lot of guys are of white American origin, European, Russian, Chinese, and Persian, right? So technology and Silicon Valley, to me, shows—not in a kumbaya kind of way, because it is competitive and so on; you guys know dollars and cents, shipping things, it's hard—but it is a way where men from different backgrounds, races, creeds, and so on can cooperate and work together for the positive good. And game recognizes game. I would never talk down white men, white guys, you know, like Newton or any of that stuff. I'm not a woke like that, right? I would never say, "Oh, I'm a heritage American, really." You know, what was that meme? "Let me explain why, you know, what your identity is and why I belong to that identity." I would never say that, right, because it's not my identity. Nevertheless, the fact that it's not my identity doesn't mean that we can't be business partners, right, in something, or at least have peace and trade—whatever relationship at whatever distance seems reasonable. And so, I think the difference is to not be woke and not be far-left, but also to not be so far to the other extreme that one thinks every difference means irreconcilable conflict and so on. Just acknowledge like an adult: yes, there are different groups and they have different interests, but let's figure out the maximum, maximum possible alignment, right, the maximum possible cooperation going forward. We should recognize that I have faults, others have faults, and how can we work together despite all that? Well, I think that's a good place for us to wrap things up. Balaji, thanks for joining, and Nate and Josh as well. This was a really fun conversation. Thanks, guys. Build the America you want to live in. What Next for the Middle East?

World Economic Forum(1.1M)·71:26·493 views·2026-06-23
Faisal AlibrahimMinister of Economy and Planning of Saudi Arabia Sigrid KaagSenior Humanitarian and Reconstruction Coordinator for Gaza, United Nations Børge BrendePresident, World Economic Forum
TL;DRFollowing a highly disruptive regional war in early 2026, the Middle East is navigating a fragile diplomatic off-ramp while rapidly accelerating domestic infrastructure, logistics, and renewable energy projects to build long-term economic resilience.
The US-Israel-Iran...

TL;DR

Following a highly disruptive regional war in early 2026, the Middle East is navigating a fragile diplomatic off-ramp while rapidly accelerating domestic infrastructure, logistics, and renewable energy projects to build long-term economic resilience.

Geopolitical Off-Ramps and Ongoing Volatility

  • The US-Israel-Iran Conflict: A highly disruptive war—triggered by divergent US/Israel objectives and an existential fight for the Iranian regime—disrupted global energy markets and led to the closure of the Strait of Hormuz before a ceasefire was reached on April 8th.
  • The Five-Basket Diplomatic Framework: A signed Memorandum of Understanding (MoU) between President Trump and Iranian leadership has initiated a 60-day technical negotiation window targeting five key areas:
      • *Strait of Hormuz:* Reopening, demining, and securing safe maritime passage.
      • *Nuclear Program:* Reaching a final conclusion on long-debated nuclear parameters.
      • *Sanctions Relief:* Delivering economic incentives to Iran from the US, Europe, Asia, and the Middle East.
      • *Guarantees:* Establishing binding frameworks (such as UN Security Council resolutions and regional guarantors) to ensure long-term treaty compliance.
      • *Lebanon and Hezbollah:* Negotiating peripheral parameters to wind down the Israel-Hezbollah conflict.
  • Skepticism and Headwinds: Hardliners in both Washington and Tehran, coupled with low trust and the upcoming US midterm elections in November, present significant barriers to a final deal, meaning further military friction and inflammatory rhetoric are highly likely during talks.

GCC Infrastructure Acceleration and Energy Resilience

  • Saudi Arabia's Strategic Pivots: KSA is shifting investments to prioritize energy resilience, logistics, and alternative transit corridors that bypass the volatile Persian Gulf.
      • *Privatization Momentum:* The National Privatization Center has advanced 160 projects, streamlining public-private partnerships (PPPs) from a typical 12-month cycle to as little as four to six months.
      • *Logistics Hub Expansion:* The Western region is seeing immense traffic, with Jeddah Islamic Port and King Abdullah Economic City processing 15 million containers annually, supported by ports in NEOM, Yanbu, and Jazan.
      • *Alternative Gas Infrastructure:* Aramco's multi-decade Master Gas System projects are accelerating, with Master Gas Phase 4 being moved forward to secure overland energy routes.
  • Renewable Energy and AI Scaling: Saudi Arabia's renewable energy goals have expanded from 4 GW to 60 GW of current capacity, aiming for 120 GW by 2030. This power is strategically earmarked for AI infrastructure, industrialization, and mobility.
  • Currency Stability and Funding: Despite the conflict, sovereign currency pegs to the USD have preserved predictable equity returns, keeping international and local banks committed to financing regional infrastructure.

Shifting Alliances and the Western Influence Vacuum

  • Rising Chinese Influence: As the US shifts its geopolitical focus, China is aggressively filling the vacuum through technology deployments, green energy investments (solar and carbon capture, utilization, and storage - CCUS), and active diplomatic mediation (e.g., the 2023 Iran-Saudi normalization).
  • The Demise of Western Dominance: Business leaders note a distinct decline in Western influence across the Arab world, with regional actors increasingly looking to Beijing and self-reliant regional networks.
  • Cooperation vs. Competitive Resilience: While there is a push to create inclusive regional corridors (incorporating Turkey, Iran, and Israel), there is a competing risk that regional states will build exclusionary, duplicative infrastructure to guarantee unilateral deterrence rather than integrated prosperity.
  • Call for Financial Alternatives: Corporate leaders highlight the need for a joint Chinese-Saudi reinsurance fund to challenge the London-dominated shipping insurance market, which has driven up global freight costs and inflation due to Strait of Hormuz transit risks.

The Business Case for Regional Resilience

  • The "Big Brother" Dynamic: The crisis demonstrated strong intra-GCC coordination, with Saudi Arabia acting as a stabilizer (e.g., hosting diverted civil aircraft from Bahrain and Kuwait during the conflict).
  • Local Company Advantage: While wars destroy infrastructure (necessitating massive rebuilding bills), local players are increasingly better positioned than global firms to capture reconstruction contracts due to deep-rooted local expertise and a highly educated younger generation.
  • The Scale of the Market: Despite its global political and energy footprint, the Middle East represents only 3% of global healthcare/pharmaceutical dollar value (compared to 42% for the US and 20% each for Europe and Japan), indicating immense room for market maturity.
  • Generational Shift: With 60% of the Arab world's population under the age of 18, a new, reform-minded generation is expected to reject historical political frameworks in favor of economic integration and digital-first industries.

Notable Quotes

"Geography is destiny. So it’s going to require pragmatic accommodation." — Sanam Vakil
"The next 10 years for the Middle East will be pivotal in shifting the Middle East from a trade corridor to an industrial and an added value region." — Khalid Sharbatly
"If you’re crazy enough to live in it, you have to understand it in order to cope, how you can survive on a day-to-day basis." — Mazen Darwazah

Good afternoon, everyone. Thank you so much for joining us, and apologies to the people sitting behind me. My name is Karen Young. I'm a senior research scholar at Columbia University at the Center on Global Energy Policy and a senior fellow at the Middle East Institute in Washington. It's my pleasure to moderate this discussion and hopefully you will all be active participants. We'll have lots of time for Q&A on what's next for the Middle East. We have a fantastic set of speakers with us. We are waiting for one more, so she will be joining us in a few minutes. She's just getting her badge. But with us today we have Mazen Darwazah, who's executive vice-chairman and deputy CEO of Hikma Pharmaceuticals. We have Dr. Lena Neieddin, president and founder of Lamar Holding out of Bahrain. Khalid Sharbatly, CEO of Desert Technologies from Saudi, also a young global leader. And my friend and colleague Sanam Vakil, who's head of the Middle East program at Chatham House in London. So when we think about, you know, the changes that are happening in the region right now, this has been certainly a spring of turmoil. But before that, we've seen a region that is showing tremendous capacity for change, for reinvention. And I think the Saudi case in the last 10 years of Vision 2030 has certainly demonstrated that. But even, you know, a case like Syria, which is really showing the capacity for, you know, fundamental changes in governments, for embracing of new economic realities. So I think it gives us hope that things can be different. And I thought it would be good to start the conversation with sort of addressing, you know, the biggest challenge right now, which has been the war between the US, Israel and Iran, started on February 28th, largest disruption to oil and gas supply in really our energy history. But also a rupture in terms of the way regional countries and actors have dealt with each other. And the spillover of that conflict certainly onto the Gulf states maybe was not anticipated or certainly not welcome. So I'd like to have Sanam give us a little bit of an update, where we are in sort of the realities of negotiations and thinking about, Sanam, your perspective, how particularly Iran and its neighbors might envision the future. Okay, thank you, Karen, and looking forward to my co-panelists' comments. This has certainly been an incredibly turbulent time in the region. But I appreciate that you began by thinking about the bigger picture. This is not a region only defined by conflict. There's a lot of ambition, a lot of desire for growth, stabilization. And prior to this war, last year's war, actually the past few years of turbulence in the Middle East, there has been a push to diversification, lots of visions on the regional order. And we've witnessed a rapprochement and normalization across the region. Between Iran and its neighbors, between Israel and some of the Gulf states, and Turkey and the Gulf states. And it's important to keep that in the back of our minds as a scaffold for the changes that have been undergoing in the Middle East. And hopefully out of all of these conflicts there will be a new, more stable Middle East. But how to answer your question, where are we after these few months of deep turbulence? The war did, you know, break out. It wasn't a surprise. I think most people that follow the Middle East as closely as we do knew it was coming, and it was going to be quite dangerous, not just for Iran or Israel and the United States, but obviously for the entire region. This war began with divergent objectives between the US and Israel. It's important to state that. And the objectives range from everything from regime change to addressing Iran's military and nuclear capabilities to disorder inside the country. And for Iran, this was an existential war where a system of the Islamic Republic really fought for its survival. And over the course of 38 days, Iran demonstrated its capabilities. It executed an asymmetrical, horizontal response strategy that was designed to take the impact of this war from inside Iran and spread it far and wide. And so this war, the consequences were borne not just by Iranians, but by Iran's neighbors. Gulf states were attacked in a really unprecedented, shocking way. The Strait of Hormuz was closed very early on in this war, and that created the economic catalyst and shock that I think Iran was trying to achieve. The aim, of course, was not to survive the military strikes, but also to think about how this war would end by spreading the costs of the war long and far from countries in Asia all the way, perhaps even to the United States. This is much more of a regional and international war, so that when the war comes to an end, if we can imagine what that looks like, it will never be repeated. That's the sort of strategic worldview, I think, of Iran's objectives here. Now, where are we today? We've had a ceasefire in place since April 8th. That ceasefire hasn't meant a full cessation of military conflict. There's been an ongoing war between Israel and Hezbollah in Lebanon. And over the past few months, the US and Iran through mediators and through a whole host of regional engagement, countries really did come together to support a diplomatic off-ramp. A memorandum of understanding was signed last week by President Trump and by Iran's leadership. And over the weekend, we saw a second high-level direct meeting, which is important, between Iran and the United States. This all means that over the next 60 days, with perhaps an extension or two, there will now be technical discussions to address four, if not five baskets of issues. The opening of the Strait of Hormuz and what that will look like in terms of security and stability and safe passage for ships, confidence in that safe passage, which is ultimately what's important. Secondly, the nuclear issues that have been debated and discussed ad nauseam but need to be concluded once and for all for this conflict to be drawn down. Thirdly, sanctions relief or the economic incentives that will be offered to Iran not just by the United States, but perhaps by European, Asian, and Middle Eastern countries. And finally, a basket of or a framework of guarantees that would keep this arrangement in place so it wouldn't unravel by another US administration. And this means that there might be guarantees in the form of a UN Security Council resolution, but also guarantors from regional states and perhaps wider countries. Finally, there is an additional basket that isn't directly connected but peripheral, and there will be technical talks on this, and that's the issue of Lebanon and the conflict between Israel and Hezbollah. So it's a complex set of issues, which means that this is likely to go on longer than the 60-day timeline of the memorandum of understanding. It's likely then to be extended perhaps by 30 days, but even maybe beyond that. That's because the technicalities are, of course, very difficult to hammer out in a three-month period. There's also the issue of trust between Tehran and Washington. While we have seen very important and groundbreaking contact between the US administration and Iran's leaders, obviously I would say trust is, you know, at an all-time low because of these wars, but also because of the unpredictability of both sides' leadership, the ongoing social media debates that continue to compromise, I think, the trust-building process. And then finally, I think that there's the domestic dynamics in Tehran and Washington that we need to really understand. For both countries, arriving at an agreement is going to be very difficult. There is opposition in Iran because of this lack of trust, because of the two rounds of war, because of the long history of mistrust and hostility. And there's equal mistrust in Washington. There are hardliners in both capitals looking to obstruct this agreement, and President Trump does have midterm elections in November, and it might seem too much to arrive at a deal before those midterms. So I'll conclude by saying we have made progress. This is positive. The ships will eventually start moving. But instability should be in your minds over the next few months. I imagine more inflammatory statements, more threats to open and close the Strait of Hormuz, maybe even more tit-for-tat strikes. And at the same time, diplomacy will tick along. Hopefully we'll get to an arrangement, but there are no guarantees because of the landscape and the unpredictability and the deep mistrust. Thank you, Sanam. I think you raise a couple of really important points, which I've been stressing recently too, is that we have to prepare ourselves for more volatility ahead, both in terms of the regional politics but what that means for energy markets and for the business community as well. GameStop CEO Ryan Cohen’s $56B Plan to Take Over eBay

All-In Podcast(1.1M)·63:03·78.2K views·2026-06-23
Ryan CohenCEO, GameStop David FriedbergCo-Host, All-In Podcast
TL;DRGameStop CEO Ryan Cohen outlines a $56 billion strategic vision to acquire and revitalize eBay by applying his signature high-efficiency operating playbook, cutting $2 billion in redundant costs, and repairing alienated seller relationships to scale ...
a dominant global...

TL;DR

GameStop CEO Ryan Cohen outlines a $56 billion strategic vision to acquire and revitalize eBay by applying his signature high-efficiency operating playbook, cutting $2 billion in redundant costs, and repairing alienated seller relationships to scale a dominant global marketplace for collectibles and second-hand goods.

The Operational Playbook: From Chewy to GameStop

  • The Chewy Origin Story: Ryan Cohen scaled Chewy into a highly sticky, recurring-revenue business model to compete directly with Amazon.
    • Efficiency Focus: The pet food category is a low-margin "game of pennies" that required aggressive vendor negotiations, zero-tolerance policies on overpaying suppliers, and optimized logistics to achieve a $3.35 billion exit.
    • Hiring Philosophy: Cohen prioritizes "will over skill," seeking highly motivated, relentless "diehards" over candidates who only look qualified on paper.
  • The GameStop Turnaround: After taking a active board role and eventually stepping in as CEO in 2023, Cohen corrected early strategic mistakes of trying to make GameStop an e-commerce-first business like Amazon.
    • Pivoting to Physical Strengths: He transitioned GameStop’s focus toward cost-cutting, retail execution, and high-margin physical collectibles and Trading Card Games (TCG).
    • Financial Results: GameStop's collectible division now makes up 42% of revenue ($350 million in Q1 out of $835 million total). The company holds $9.7 billion in cash, generated $333 million in free cash flow, and reduced SG&A from $228 million to $202 million.

The Strategic Rationale for Acquiring eBay

  • Complementary Strengths: GameStop and eBay share overlapping strengths in the secondary market, second-hand authentication, and collectibles. What GameStop executes in physical retail (such as giving immediate cash for graded PSA 8+ cards), eBay scales globally online.
  • Targeting Circle of Competence: Cohen views eBay as a fundamentally strong business model that falls directly within his expertise in digital marketplace dynamics and e-commerce.
  • Low-Risk Marketplace Model: Unlike Chewy or Amazon, Cohen has no desire to build out a massive first-party logistics and inventory network for eBay. He intends to keep eBay as an asset-light, third-party marketplace.

Diagnosing eBay’s Institutional Decline

  • Stagnant Metrics and Bleeding Users: Despite a strong first-mover advantage, eBay has failed to keep pace with e-commerce growth.
    • Since the pandemic, the platform has lost 30 million active users.
    • Gross Merchandise Volume (GMV) and operating earnings are down, while operating expenses have bloated to over half of total revenues.
  • Alienation of Key Sellers: Under professional, non-founder management, eBay has treated its power sellers—the true "customers" of a marketplace—with complacency.
    • Management eliminated dedicated seller concierge programs.
    • A lack of native tools forces sellers to rely on expensive third-party software integrations to run their businesses.
  • Consultant Culture: Cohen criticizes eBay's current leadership for relying on high-priced external consulting firms rather than rolling up their sleeves to solve engineering and platform issues directly with sellers.

Cohen's Three-Part Vision to Rebuild eBay

  • Part 1: Immediate Cost Extraction: Cohen intends to immediately pull $2 billion in operating costs out of eBay to streamline the business and significantly boost earnings.
  • Part 2: Expanding Live Commerce: Integrating robust live-shopping features to reclaim market share lost to younger, nimbler category-specific competitors.
  • Part 3: Digital and Physical Collectible Ecosystem: Building a digital, in-game collectible trading marketplace that bridges the gap between digital gaming assets and physical collectibles.
  • Seller-First Engineering: Establishing a direct pipeline between top sellers and eBay's engineering team to systematically eliminate seller pain points and build better native management tools.

***

Notable Quotes

"If our suppliers are sending us gifts in the mail, that's a really bad sign. It means we're overpaying. If our suppliers are telling us they never want to speak to us again, it means we're getting the right price." — Ryan Cohen
"In a marketplace model like eBay, the sellers are the customer. You make your sellers happy, you give them the tools, they bring more inventory online, and you end up ultimately doing more sales... It's not that complicated." — Ryan Cohen
"GameStop I don't think was such a good idea [in hindsight]... This [eBay] is actually a really good idea, whether it ends up working out or not. This is within my circle of competence, and I can't stop thinking about it." — Ryan Cohen
"Everyone hates GameStop and it seems like everyone in the media basically wants us to fail and wants them to succeed, and you've got a board that's making hundreds of thousands of dollars a year. They don't buy stock with their own money. They end up showing up to a handful of board meetings and they're making a fortune. You've got a management team that is grossly overpaid. There's nothing more American than basically risking your own capital. So why does everyone want us to fail?" I'm going all in... AppLovin started with an $8 domain and no VC funding. They built anyway and became one of the largest ad platforms in the world. Now they're bringing that same engine to e-commerce through AppLovin ads. Your ads run inside mobile games, reaching over a billion people with full-screen, distraction-free attention. The platform finds buyers and optimizes for profit. You set the target, and it does the rest. A cookware brand doing $4 million tried AppLovin ads, hit 16 million, turned profitable, and they're on pace for $80 million this year. Visit AppLovin.com/all-in to launch your first campaign today. Ryan Cohen, welcome to the All-In Interview. Thanks for being here. Thank you for having me. I think it's been like a decade, which is crazy how old we get, since you and I last had dinner in New York. This was before several chapters of your life unfolded and it's great to be talking with you today. You're doing something really interesting right now, trying to acquire and run eBay, which obviously is a big story right now that takes us back 25, 30 years to the start of the internet. But I want to talk a little bit about your story first, if that's okay. And I'd love for folks that are watching this or listening to this to learn a little bit about you and the journey you've been on that brings you to this moment. Maybe we can go back to the business you started, Chewy, if that's okay. Yes, definitely. Yeah, well maybe tell me why you started Chewy. How'd you get that idea, and how'd you get into building this business from where you were coming from at that time? We wanted to build something online and we were about to launch an online jewelry website. I did not know anything about jewelry. I went to a bunch of trade shows, bought hundreds of thousands of dollars worth of inventory, built the website, and had the distribution. Then, I was shopping in a neighborhood pet store. I had a poodle and I was going every few weeks, and it just hit me on one of my trips that I understood the product much better. It was a recurring revenue purchase. The market was still fragmented. The fact that there were still neighborhood pet stores at the time and they had not been disrupted by Petco and PetSmart was fascinating to me. And then you had Amazon, which was established and had pet products since the '90s, but they hadn't really achieved real scale in the category. So the vision was to replicate the same experience that I had at the neighborhood pet store but do it online and do it at scale. And I looked at Amazon's best practices when it came to supply chain—so fast shipping, having a great selection, being competitively priced. And then the experience at the neighborhood pet store of knowing the products really well. And it was easy to be passionate about the category because I'm a pet owner, and everyone we hired were pet owners. And it was all about market leadership. It was a low-margin business. In hindsight, it was not necessarily the best idea to go head-to-head against Amazon selling 30-pound bags of pet food, but we executed really well and we grew really quickly. And we had negative working capital, so it was a business that was able to get to billions of dollars in revenue and not consume a lot of capital. How did you learn to execute well? So at the time, you had little business experience prior to that. How did you learn those skills? What were the principles and the values that made you excellent at operating that business? I understood from the beginning that the real competition was always Amazon, and they were world-class when it comes to supply chain. So, negotiating very fiercely with suppliers to get the best product costs—and that meant getting to scale, moving from buying pallets of dog food to truckloads of dog food, and moving from distribution to direct. Buying generally, the more you buy, the lower the prices are going to be. Operating efficiently in the warehouses, and so labor optimization, warehouse management optimization, getting competitive prices with shipping carriers. It was a game of pennies, and the goal was to grow quickly and establish market leadership. The difference between failure and success was, you know, pennies in the red is failure and pennies in the black is success. So we had to operate hyper-efficiently, and there was a lot of competition in the space. It wasn't a novel idea; it was going head-to-head against Amazon, and Pets.com was in the backdrop, so it made it very difficult to raise capital. But I'd say at a high level, the market underestimated not the size of the addressable market, but our execution. You know, as a customer, I've had lots of dogs. It was always such a great consumer experience. Did you personally put your finger on that or did you bring great people around you that understood consumer, apart from the supply chain optimization, the labor optimization, getting the cash flows to work right? Was there a lot on the consumer product angle that you spent time on with that business? When we looked at the cohorts, you could see the customers were very sticky, and the reason why I moved from jewelry to pet food was because it was a recurring item. So I loved the fact that it was consumable, and when we started Chewy, for the first few years we just focused on food, treats, litter—all of the things that people are buying all the time. So the vision and the idea was, if we treat our customers well, they're going to continue shopping with us. And that's what we did. And so it was everything from the handwritten holiday cards to the pet portraits to 24/7 customer service, and if there was ever an issue, you know, we took care of the customer. And that's what happened, and the customers continued shopping with us. The best referrals are word of mouth, and pet owners love to tell their friends and fellow pet owners if they have a great experience. So the thesis ultimately played out. So for those who don't know, you built and sold the business in 2017 for $3.35 billion dollars. Subsequently, there was an IPO and that business continued to trade up in value. So you clearly executed well. But help me understand, how did you build and manage the leadership team, the management team, and the people around you to execute so well? What did you learn as a manager, as a CEO, as an operator when you were building Chewy? Staying on top of everything. Just, it's 24/7 watching all of the numbers. I mean, I would stay in Google AdWords till four or five in the morning managing campaigns myself. I was negotiating directly with all of our major suppliers. I had a supplier that told me during one of our negotiations actually—it was like a one-year contract—and he's like, "I'm so happy this is over, I never have to talk to you for..." well it was basically another year, he's like, "I don't have to speak to you for the next year." I was like, that was a compliment. And anytime someone else was doing the negotiation... I mean, it's counter-intuitive. They want to build relationships with suppliers; the reality is it's mostly transactional. And so if our suppliers are sending us gifts in the mail, that's a really bad sign. It means we're overpaying. If our suppliers are telling us they never want to speak to us again, it means we're getting the right price. But getting people into that framework is not easy because the path of least resistance is basically to get along and to be nice. But unfortunately, when you're building a business and you're losing money, you've got to focus on sustainability. So, just being on top of everything. And what about on people? I'm just trying to understand your skill as a manager of people, because clearly you did something right. You've continued to execute at GameStop, which we'll get to in a minute. And I'm trying to understand, how do you find great people and how do you hold people accountable, and what are management techniques that you've developed for building a team and running a team? I look for will over skill. And I had a woman that was running customer service as an example, and she came from... she was working in like an old people's home. And she applied for the job many times and we just... we didn't think she was qualified and we looked over her resume and she kept on applying, she was relentless. Ali Ghodsi, Co-founder and CEO, Databricks kicks off Data + AI Summit 2026

Databricks(161.0K)·57:55·395 views·2026-06-23
Ali GhodsiCo-founder and CEO, Databricks
TL;DRAt the Data + AI Summit 2026, Databricks CEO Ali Ghodsi declared that Artificial General Intelligence (AGI) has arrived, shifting the enterprise challenge from "intelligence" to "context.
" To resolve this, Databricks introduced a suite of open, agentic platform...

TL;DR

At the Data + AI Summit 2026, Databricks CEO Ali Ghodsi declared that Artificial General Intelligence (AGI) has arrived, shifting the enterprise challenge from "intelligence" to "context." To resolve this, Databricks introduced a suite of open, agentic platform innovations designed to transform the lakehouse into the future "agentic system of record" while solving critical hurdles in choice, cost, and control.

***

The Shift to the "Agentic System of Record"

  • The Context Problem: While frontier AI models can solve hyper-complex academic problems, they struggle inside enterprises because they lack real-time corporate context.
      • The Live-Loop Bottleneck: Current agents perform slow, expensive, and low-accuracy "random walks" across document folders and disconnected APIs.
      • Four Pillars of Resolution: To scale AI, enterprises must solve for Choice (avoiding model/cloud lock-in), Control (governance and security), Cost (preventing runaway token budgets), and Context (indexing corporate knowledge).
  • Evolution of the SaaS Stack: Traditional SaaS stacks isolate data in proprietary systems of record. Databricks argues the future belongs to a unified Agentic System of Record where all data, security, and AI models reside on a single, open data platform across multiple clouds.

Unleashing Enterprise Context: Genie Ontology & Agent Suite

  • Genie Ontology: A major architectural breakthrough that automatically constructs a background knowledge graph of an organization’s entire data estate.
      • OntoRank Algorithm: Developed by Databricks’ research team, this algorithm acts like "PageRank for enterprise assets." It maps connections across structured databases, Google Drive, SharePoint, email, calendars, and org charts.
      • Unity Catalog Semantics: Integrates manual business glossaries and curated semantic layers from partners like Atlan and AtScale.
  • The Genie Agent Portfolio:
      • Genie One: A unified, secure, consumer-grade search and chat interface for all business users to access company-wide data via mobile or desktop.
      • Genie Agents: Converts standard user-Genie conversations into deployable, automated agents that integrate into Slack or Microsoft Teams (e.g., automated HR assistants).
      • Genie Code: An agent specialized in low-level data engineering, pipeline writing, and training machine learning models on GPUs.
      • Genie ZeroOps: A background operations agent that automatically detects broken data pipelines at night, tests code corrections in a sandbox, and sends a "one-click fix" push notification to on-call engineers.
  • Agent Bricks & Omnigent: A developer sandbox environment featuring "Agent Memory" and Omnigent—an open-source meta-harness developed by Matei Zaharia that pits different frontier models against each other to optimize output quality.

Open Data Architecture, Lakebase, and Unified Governance

  • Data Format Convergence: Databricks announced the GA of Apache Iceberg V3 support within Databricks Runtime (DBR), achieving a unified data layer that prevents the need to rewrite files between Iceberg and Delta Lake. Delta 5 and Iceberg V4 (targeted for Q4 2026) will finalize the unified metadata layer.
  • Lakebase Postgres: An open-source transaction processing engine (OLTP) that separates compute from storage, utilizing cheap lake storage.
      • Serverless Autoscaling: Scales Postgres down to zero when inactive, significantly reducing Total Cost of Ownership (TCO).
      • Database Branching: Allows agents or developers to clone petabyte-scale databases in less than a second using copy-on-write, enabling zero-risk sandbox experimentation.
  • Unity AI Gateway: A new, open-source control pane within Unity Catalog and MLflow to curb runaway AI costs and manage agent access.
      • Token Spend Management: Provides unified capacity purchasing, allowing companies to spend their Databricks contract dollars directly on frontier models (OpenAI, Anthropic, Gemini) while enforcing strict departmental budgets and rate limits.
  • OpenSharing: An open-source project superseding Delta Sharing. It allows companies to securely share Delta/Iceberg tables, AI models, skills, and autonomous agents across organizations and on-premises environments.

Next-Generation Agentic Applications & Acquisitions

  • Acquisition of Panther Labs: Databricks has agreed to acquire Panther Labs, a Python-based SIEM platform. Founded by Jack Naglieri, Panther's Pythonic detection framework aligns natively with LLMs, which naturally generate and read Python code.
  • Lakewatch: An agentic, cost-effective Security Information and Event Management (SIEM) system built directly on the security lakehouse. It uses autonomous agents to triage high-volume alerts and hunt for zero-day exploits.
  • CustomerLake: An agentic Customer Data Platform (CDP) featuring an LLM-driven Profile Agent for identity de-duplication, and a Campaign Agent designed to run hyper-personalized "Infinity Campaigns."
  • Lakeflow Upgrades: Introduzes ZeroBus (GA) for high-rate, brokerless streaming ingestion; Spark Real-Time Mode (RTM) for sub-second streaming latencies; and Lakeflow Designer (GA) for visual data engineering.

***

Notable Quotes
