We don't need it to be more intelligent. AI does not have an intelligence problem right now. These are not the kind of problems that we need inside of our organizations. The problem we have is that AGI is not really completely permeating our organizations. So, inside of your companies, you don't have hundreds of agents working for you doing autonomous work, collaborating with each other, coming up with proposals, negotiating, and where we're just managing agents. Most of us are just using chatbots and asking them, or maybe we're using coding tools and they're writing code for us and we're using it agentically like that. But we're not actually, sort of, fully realizing that vision that everybody's talking about. And that's probably why maybe many of you didn't raise your hand when I said, "Is AGI here?" So the question is: how do we actually enable this at work? That's what the next couple of days will be about. We want to figure out how to enable all of you to sort of lead this revolution. It's basically our job here to figure out how we can actually make this AI work. That's what you all are doing, so that's what we're focused on. So, in a nutshell, what we want to do is, we have all these AIs. They've been released by many of the vendors—there's open source, there are frontier models. How do we figure out how to take all the data, all the processes in your organization, everything that you have in your head, and provide it as context to the AI? If we can do that, we think that the AI can already solve many of the tasks that we wanted it to solve. Okay, so if we just give the context to these very smart models that can solve those super hard problems, they'll be able to do amazing things inside of our companies. You know, most of the tasks we're doing are not about solving 12-dimensional spin bordisms. They're about, you know, getting the information out of Salesforce, summarizing it, putting it in another place, going through it, preparing it, and those kinds of tasks. So you just need to get all that context. So, it's as simple as that. But getting this context into the AI is harder than one could imagine. It's actually really, really difficult, and getting the perfect enterprise context is elusive. So this is the problem we've been focused on, and we're really focused on this problem at Databricks. So context is actually itself many other things. It's not just saying, "Context, how do we give it to the AI?" Context means: how do we get all the data that you have in your organization and actually connect it to the AI? Okay, data could be... what are all the meeting notes? So every meeting that happens in the organization should be recorded, and we should get the transcripts of that, and we need to reorganize our processes so that we can do that. And actually, at Databricks, we have forward-deployed engineers that come and help reorganize things like that. But it also means connecting to data silos that you have, or data that's sitting tucked away and you don't have any access to it because maybe security has not approved it. This is step number one: how do we get your data AI-ready so that we can actually give it to the agents? Okay, but let's say we figure this out and we figure out how to get access to all the data, then we're still not done. The context problem isn't solved because we could just take OpenClaw and just unleash it on all of your data. Okay, when OpenClaw was released, over 10% of the skills in it were actually malicious. So step number two: how do we do this securely? How do we do that with control? Okay, so first is context, now how do we do this with control? So that's really, really important. If we're going to have agents roaming around doing various things, we have to make sure that they are satisfying our security policies, that we can audit them, and that they don't do something that we don't want them to do. Okay, let's say we solve this as well. It's still really hard. If we just have agents running around loop live checking through all of the data, then this is going to become extremely costly. So we have to also solve the cost problem. Okay, this is sort of just shooting up at most of our customers. We're seeing the cost just completely run away. Uber's CEO said that in one quarter they blew through the whole year's annual AI budget. Okay, if this continues, it's completely unsustainable for most organizations out there. You cannot have your costs completely outrun your revenue—you will go bankrupt. Okay, and your companies will not allow that, so they'll just shut it down or stop it. Okay, so it's just infeasible. So we have to also solve the cost problem. But even if we solve all of these things, including the cost, we get to the most important thing in my opinion, which is: how do we make sure that we can do that without lock-in? How can we do that in a way where you have choice? Okay, this is super important, and this is the hardest one because some of you are representing organizations that are less than 10 years old, so this maybe doesn't apply to you, but most of the organizations in the room—over 90% of you—represent companies who have a long history. They've been around for 20, 30, 50, or hundreds of years, some of them. And the thing I've seen in the last 13 years is that the stack that you have at your companies is just very complicated. Over the years, you keep buying new software, you keep adding more things to that stack, and you just get locked into all those vendors. They come and go, but the software remains, and there's always someone who wants that piece of software in your stack, so it's hard to rip it out. So, how do we do this without getting locked in, both to the data and the context, but most importantly, also now to the AI? We don't want to get locked in just to one particular AI; we want to have choice when it comes to the AI. So, that's what we're focused on today. My whole keynote is just focused on how we solve these four. Okay. So, I hope I can convince you today that we're making some steps towards solving these four. I'm not going to say we've completely solved it—that context is solved—but at least we're making some big leaps towards solving these four things. So, the four things we're focused on: we want you to have choice and not get locked in, we want to be able to get the security, the control, and the cost control, as well as the context, and then get that to the AI, and that way we can have more impact together. Okay. So, how do we do that? Let's start with Choice. Okay, it starts with your data, as I mentioned. So, we have all the data and we want to get that data in an AI-ready form. So, step number one is we've actually made huge progress just in the last year to get that data and move it into an open lakehouse—so, open infrastructure on top of any of the clouds—and we get your data ready there. That's what Lakeflow does. So, Lakeflow has actually been developing a lot of connectors over the last year. Two years ago when I was here, we barely had any connectors, and today we now have over 100 connectors. So, Lakeflow can now connect to all these different systems that you have. It's not just Salesforce, Workday, and NetSuite. It can pull data from Meta, or Google Analytics, or from any of your docs, or any of the corpus. And if there is any data source that you do not have, please let us know, and we can help you get those into the open lake. Okay. And it's not just getting the data in there; we can do it in many different modalities. So, there are three things that I would like to highlight. One is we released ZeroBus last year. It is now GA, and actually, we've added a lot of capabilities to it. So, as the name implies, you don't need a bus anymore for the data infrastructure. Instead of using something like Apache Kafka, you can just hit this API of ZeroBus, and you can give it data as it comes in. It could be bursty; it could be tiny rows. You don't need to worry about having millions of files or any buffering or anything like that. ZeroBus just takes care of taking all those entries that you hit it with at a very high rate, and it just makes sure that those appear in your open lake in a way that you can probe it later. Okay, scalable and manageable. Okay, so that's great. And then last year, we announced Spark Real-Time Mode. Spark Real-Time Mode, or RTM as we call it, is an open-source framework that lets us actually get down to tens of milliseconds. So, this is really amazing. So, we can do now operations super fast. Historically, Spark was great for everything, but you couldn't really get down to 10 milliseconds. Okay, so the real-time streaming would always take this micro-batch of one second or so. But with real-time mode, we can now really make that super fast. So, many of you, for your streaming infrastructure, you might have Apache Flink. That was the only option that many of you would pick. But now you can actually do a lot of those workloads directly in Apache Spark with real-time mode, which is open source. Okay. And the final one is Lakeflow Designer. I personally actually really like doing all of my data engineering and data movement using Lakeflow Designer. So, the way Lakeflow Designer works is that it kind of looks like Alteryx. It's visual. You don't need to see any code, but you speak to the AI, which is Genie, and you tell it, "Hey, I want to do this transformation, I want to join these two tables," and it just visually shows you what that looks like, and then you approve it. But under the hood, it's all just Spark—open-source Spark—and you can actually inspect the code, you can version control it, and you can make it production-ready. So that's super awesome. Okay, so those are three things, they're all GA. So you can now get all this data, use any of these modalities, use all these connectors from these different systems, and you can get them into... well, which format should you pick? Should you pick Delta Lake, or should you pick Apache Iceberg? Okay, so that's a big question, and actually, I want to welcome to the stage the original creator of Apache Iceberg, Ryan Blue, who was the CEO and co-founder of Tabular, which we acquired last year. So, let's welcome him on stage. "Hey Ryan, how are you?" "Good, how are you?" "Good. I want to embarrass you with a video from a bunch of years ago. So this video actually had a very interesting title. It said why you shouldn't care about Apache Iceberg. I mean, listen to it. Okay, we shouldn't care about Apache Iceberg. Actually, I agree. I think we shouldn't also care about Delta Lake. But why did you say that?" "No one should have to care about formats. You—you should be able to use whatever tools you want with all of your data, use the right tool for the job. Which is why we built both formats in the beginning." Yeah, in fact, it's super funny. A few years ago, you know, I would get to customer meetings and they would get into the nitty-gritty of how Iceberg was implemented versus Delta, and they would ask us like, "Yeah, but can you do this, you know, deletion vectors this way and that way?" And I was like, "This is not where the industry should be going." So, how are we doing? Where are we? What's going on with the versions and all of that? "Well, we just—just did the GA release of Iceberg V3 support, managed Iceberg tables in DBR. That is going really well. We're following that up later this year with the unified metadata layer that is going to be built into Delta 5 and Iceberg V4. So, we are very close to the full unification vision." "So what's special about V3?" "V3 is the unified data layer. So you no longer have to rewrite any data files to share them across Delta and Iceberg tables." "That's awesome. So basically, the data that's laid out, whether it's Iceberg or Delta, looks identical—the big data now, right?" "Exactly, yes." "Awesome. And then with V4, just a little bit of metadata that's left. And hopefully, when do we get that?" "We're aiming for later this year. I don't want to make too many promises, but I think that we should finish the spec, you know, sometime in maybe Q4." "Awesome. Super excited about this. Great job by Ryan and the whole community—the Iceberg community, Delta community. You shouldn't have to care about this. Can we stop talking about this now?" "Yeah, I—I got work to do if—if we're done." "All right, thank you." All right, so it doesn't really matter. Actually, if you're using Databricks, it doesn't matter if it's in Delta or Iceberg—we don't care, it's actually the same now. Thanks to V3, it's the same data format that's on the disk. Okay, perfect. So that's that. So now we're done with how you can actually get your data into this open format. It's really seamless, just one way of doing it. It's all Spark-based under the hood. So let's move on and look at how we actually then start doing analytics on that data. The data warehouse that we announced about four or five years ago, we call it Lakehouse. In fact, our tagline was "The best data warehouse is a lakehouse" because it's open, so you control the data yourself and it works with machine learning workloads. And we made huge progress on our lakehouse in the last year since the last Data and AI Summit. We've added over 110 features that legacy data warehouses have so that we can more easily migrate those over. We also released a lot of AI functions. This is the main thing that people want with a lakehouse. So, there are all these AI functions you can just call. So you have your data—maybe they are in tables, that's the kind of thing you work with in data warehousing. But now you can, just in your SQL queries, call these SQL AI functions. You can say, "Can you do sentiment analysis? Tell me for each of these cells what do people think," or you can tell it, "Hey, for each of these cells can you extract for me the company names that you see in it?" Or you can do a prediction of what this number is going to be, and it can just do that at a really high scale. This is the most popular thing that people do actually on Lakehouse. And we've taken LakeBridge, which is an automated converter that lets you migrate from existing data warehouses that you might have on-prem or elsewhere in the cloud, and we can do that now really, really well. So, we're very excited about that. And actually, you all are using this, so we've over doubled the consumption of our data warehouse just in the last year. So, we're very excited about this. But what I'm really excited about is my co-founder Reynold getting on stage and talking about the new engine. Okay, so we have a new engine he's going to talk about. It's called Reyden, and what's really cool about Reyden is that it's the world's fastest engine that we've ever seen. We've benchmarked it against anything else. We've gotten the latency of it down to just tens of milliseconds—so, far faster than anything we had done before. So we're very excited. We will have him in a couple of talks in the keynote today talking about this. Okay, so that's our data warehouse, that's the lakehouse. What about transaction processing? So, I mentioned in the beginning we really love Postgres. We've really embraced Postgres. We think AIs today really prefer Postgres. If you ask any of the AIs today what kind of database should I pick, they will say open-source Postgres. Okay? But we're excited about what we call Lakebase, which is a way in which you take the Postgres and you separate it out so that you have all of its storage just sit on cheap lakes. Okay, so now we're talking Lakebase, so this is OLTP, right? It's like MySQL, Oracle, transaction processing. And once you have that data sitting on the lake, you can now start doing amazing things on top. So, what can we do on top? Well, we have a little animation here. If you look, you can get serverless autoscaling. You can scale up and down Postgres. And this is really fast. So, you can do it in less than a second; you can scale it even down to zero. That's really awesome because if nobody's using it in the middle of the night, you don't have to pay for it—you just pay for cheap storage that's sitting in the lake. Okay, this was not possible before. You know, others had specialized storage, but this is just sitting in open-source format on your lake. So, why do you care about that? Why should your company care about that? Because it significantly cuts the cost of running databases down. So, you get a significant reduction in TCO by doing this kind of autoscaling. So that's a game changer. But that's actually not even the coolest feature of Lakebase Postgres. The best thing about Lakebase Postgres is that it has this feature that agents really love. And that feature is called Branching. So, it kind of looks like that. I don't know, that's our attempt to animate and visualize that. But what that means is that you can take a gigantic database—petabytes—and you can say, "Hey, can you make a clone of this for me?" And it will, in less than a second, say, "Done." What it's doing is not actually copying the whole database; it's just keeping track. Since the data is anyway sitting on the lake, it'll just say, "Okay, we have two copies," but it's actually just one copy. And then when you make a change to one, it'll just keep track of like, "Okay, this change is in this copy; this other change doesn't exist in the first copy," so what's called copy-on-write. So it actually does that for you. Why does that matter? Well, it matters because it actually completely changes the way you organize databases inside of an enterprise. Most enterprises have thousands and thousands of database instances that they're running, and we have DBAs that are running around trying to manage all of these. And the way you do that—the reason you have thousands of these and you pay for all these thousands of copies of the data that are siloed inside of the enterprise—is because you need to have a user acceptance testing database. You need to have production database one, production database number two, you need to have a staging database, an R&D database... so you have all these copies. Okay, but what branching enables is to just have one instance, and then you just have different branches of it, and that's just lower TCO, easier to manage, and agents love that. Because agents love to just branch out and just experiment with the data, try something out, and they want to do it quickly. So, that one-second latency is very important for them; they don't want to wait 10 minutes for a database to come up. And then finally, if—if something goes wrong, they want to be able to go back to an earlier snapshot. If the data gets corrupted or the agents did something by mistake, they should be able to go back to an earlier version. So this is really awesome, so please try this out. If you're doing any development going forward, please use Lakebase Postgres. In the next 12 months, we're going to see more software written than ever in the history of mankind. Okay. All that software that your organizations are going to write using LLMs and coding tools needs a database behind the scenes. Let's make sure it's just open-source Lakebase Postgres with low cost. That's what these agents love. But today we have another talk that I'm really excited about, and I'm not going to talk too much about it here, but Reynold's going to come back and he's going to talk about something we call LTAP—okay, Lakebase and Lakehouse unification. So, it's a breakthrough that actually the industry has been working on for 40 years. We think we finally pulled it off, so he's going to come and talk about that as well. Okay, so we talked about Lakeflow to get the data in, we talked about Lakehouse, which is a data warehouse to do analytics, and we talked about Lakebase, which is transaction processing and the talks that we're going to have on that. So, hopefully we now have the choice—the data layer at the bottom ready for the AI, okay, so that it can be consumed fast by the agents, and it's seamless for them, and they can experiment with the data. So this is really important: we've got to get that data layer right. But as I mentioned, we also need to figure out control. Okay, so we can't just unleash the agents on all of the data like that; we need to make sure that we're controlling what they are doing. So, that's why we started the Unity Catalog project a bunch of years ago. When we started that project, what the industry was doing is that they were having catalogs for structured tables. So, data warehouses were looking at structured tables, rows and columns, and they were saying, "Okay, well this column is somebody's salary information, so can we hide that?" So that's what this was about—access control on structured data like that. But we called it Unity because we think you shouldn't just do control on structured data; you should do it on all the data assets that you have in an organization—any Data and AI assets. Okay? So, you could have unstructured files, PDFs, you might have AI models, and 50 other asset types that we added over the years—we want to be able to do it with all of these. Okay, so that's idea number one for Unity Catalog. Number two, you don't want to just do access control. You can lock everything down and you're done. You want to also be able to do discovery and democratize the data in the organization. You want to also be able to track the lineage of the different datasets—where does this data come from—and visualize that graph. You want to be able to do cost controls on everything that's being done, because otherwise the cost just will run out. You want to be able to look at the data quality of everything you have in your organization. So, these are all the things that were part of Unity Catalog, and it's extremely important. That's why we never actually charge for Unity Catalog. Unity Catalog is just free, and we actually open-sourced it a few years ago. We're doubling down on open-sourcing more—in the last month, we added more people that are open-sourcing pieces of it, so it's moving very, very fast. We're excited about that. And also, we announced that you want to be able to share data. So this was really important for us. The rest of the industry, they were doing this kind of locked-in sharing where they were saying, "Buy my software, and then if you want to share it with someone else, they should buy also my software, and they can share datasets between them." That's why we open-sourced Delta Sharing a bunch of years ago as part of the Delta Lake project. And now, you could take any data assets and you could share them with anyone in the ecosystem. It's actually a great growth, lots of partners in that. But what we're very excited about this year is that we are actually open-sourcing a new project that's a superset of Delta Sharing—we call it OpenSharing. And what this enables you to do is share not just with Delta, but also with Iceberg, because they're now the same format. But also, not just share data assets, but also AI assets. Okay? So now you can share your agents, your skills, and your models, and you can do that also on-premise. Okay? So we're very excited about that. This is very cool. Okay, so we've got all these different things you can do in Unity Catalog, and we have OpenSharing. But in the last year, what has happened is that governance—which always has been... AI governance has always been part of Unity Catalog—is becoming very complicated in most organizations. Okay? So, we're seeing that organizations now have lots of agents. You're all building agents—maybe you're using LlamaIndex, maybe using LangChain. The SaaS providers are now each providing you an agent that they want you to use; they have agent builders that they want you to use; they have their own MCP servers that the SaaS vendors are saying, "Please use our MCP servers, here are the skills you can use with those." Your developers are themselves developing MCP servers, they're themselves developing skills files, and there are all these models that are being developed now by the frontier vendors. There are many different versions of them; they all have different capabilities—you know, they exist, they don't exist. There are coding tools out there, "vibe coding" things, and then now we're starting to see more and more co-worker-like agents in the organization. So, it's kind of a quagmire. It's a complete mess. Okay, yeah, so what is the problem with this? So, problem number one is actually that cost is running away. I think this is the biggest problem right now. Anyone we talk to, they're really worried. The costs of this are just skyrocketing, and no one has visibility. Is it someone's agent? Which model is it? What were they doing? So all this token maxing is happening, and there's no way to actually set limits on everything holistically or rate limit it. So, that's problem number one. Problem number two is that there's no way to control what the agents are doing. How do we make sure that the agents actually have access to the appropriate data, that we can audit them, that we have guardrails for their inputs and outputs, and how do we manage their identities? Who are they acting on behalf of? How do we track this? And then finally—and this is the most important one, as I said earlier—how do we do this with choice, without lock-in? Because the state-of-the-art models now have a lifespan of one month. In November last year, we were super excited about Gemini coming out from Google, and it was like the best model, and we all were switching over to it, and we said, "Okay, that's it, this is the best model ever." Then in the February timeframe—the January-February timeframe—it was like, "Clearly, Opus is the best ever." And then OpenAI came out with GPT-4o, and that was the greatest model ever. And then last week, we had Mythos or Fable and it was out, and we were all going to switch to it, but now they canceled it, and then, you know, it's back and forth. So, how do we make sure that we can actually have flexibility so that we can pick any of them and that we will have choice here? Okay, so those are three problems that are really important. And that's why we're super excited to announce, as part of Unity Catalog, we are breaking out one place where you can manage and control all of your agents, all of your AI spend—we call it Unity AI Gateway. Okay. So what is Unity AI Gateway? It's a single pane of glass, and there you can actually have your one entry point for all of your agents, for all of your AI, for everything that happens—any of the harnesses that you're using can go through this. Again, this is part of Unity Catalog which we open-sourced, we don't charge for this, and also the AI Gateway is part of MLflow so it's also open-sourced. But one of the most important things that we do, that maybe not everyone in the room knows, is that we will actually provide you capacity for the frontier models. So, let's say you committed to pay $100,000 to Databricks. You might use Lakeflow for that to consume things, you might use our lakehouse, but you can also just use tokens for that $100,000 directly from us on OpenAI's models, or Anthropic's models, or Gemini, and you can do this on any cloud. Whether you're on Azure, GCP, or AWS, you can just use this. So this is available on the platform; we actually offer that. Two, you can actually get perfect observability on all of the spend inside of the organization with dashboards that give you the observability of how that spend is happening, and you can set limits. You can set budgets. You can set budgets on different groups, subgroups, all the way down to the individual level. And when someone exhausts that budget, you will get an alert, or you can actually just stop it and rate limit it so that you can make sure that nobody is spending too much, or that they every day get an email saying how much they've spent. So, this is super important. And then finally, we can enforce safety, compliance, auditing, and identity management of all your AI. Okay, this includes harnesses, this includes models and agents. So, we're very excited about this. So that's what Unity AI Gateway is, and now you can manage all these asset types: MCP tools, models, agents, and skills that you have in the organization. And I just want to emphasize, this is not just MCP or the tools that you have in Databricks. These are any MCPs that you have in the organization by any vendor. You register them in Unity AI Gateway, and you will track all the usage and you will see how it's going. And, in fact, it has a really awesome capability where you just authenticate once for the MCPs, and it'll do it for all of them, so you don't have to authenticate again and again and again and again. So, this is really awesome. Check this out. Okay. So now we have also control and we have cost. So now we've dealt with how to get your data ready—so, hopefully, we have our data ready in an open format that's fast for the agents—and we figured out how to do control, so we can do governance and how to do cost management with Unity AI Gateway. Okay, so that brings us to the context layer. As I said, AIs are plenty smart. AI does not have an intelligence problem; it has a context problem. So, how do we actually get that context into the AI? This is the most important thing. So, what do I mean here? So let me give you an example of how it works today. Today you're using these agents, you might be using a coding agent, and if you look at the best agents out there, they kind of work like this. There's a big graph of all the documents and everything that everyone is doing inside of your organization. And what they're doing—in these red dots you can see—they go, when you ask a question (for instance, you say, "How is my product revenue the last two weeks in Europe?"), it goes and it looks at one document. And then it reads that document. And it says, "Okay, is there anything interesting?" And then, "Oh, there's a link," and then it goes and opens up an MCP server to another document. It reads that document. And it's sort of for-looping live through your corpus to get the data. But there's a big problem with this approach that all the agents today are using—all the frontier ones. Problem number one is that it's very time-consuming. This live loop of going and searching for your data live, it's just going to take a very long time. Okay, and we all see it. Like, you ask a question, even a simple question, and it takes it 10 minutes, 15 minutes, to just come back with an answer because it's live going through the MCP servers. It doesn't know where to search; it's just trying live to do this. It doesn't have the context. Number two: the costs are super high. The joke is, you tell it, "Please rename this file for me," and it takes 10 minutes and it charges you five bucks. Okay, so that's kind of how it works because, again, it's going through and it's doing this search, and it's using up a lot of tokens to construct all of this. But these two are not even the biggest problem. This—this gives you a really bad user experience, and it's expensive. The biggest problem is that the quality suffers. The reason that is is that all of the documents and all the data assets in your organization are gigantic, okay? So you're really just doing a small random walk of a small subset to get you the answer. It's remarkable, the answers it gives you—it's game-changing. We're all excited about it, and we are all using a bunch of it, and the costs are going up. But actually, if you look at the accuracy, it's quite problematic. Okay, so how do we solve this? So, we are very excited to announce—and this is one of the most important things I'm going to announce—Genie Ontology. Okay. So, what's Genie Ontology? Let me explain. So, what Genie Ontology does is that it actually connects with all the data that you have in an organization, and in the background—so this is not when you're asking a question, this is happening in the background—it's constructing a graph of the most important knowledge that you have inside of your organization. It's making it ready so that we can give that context to any of the agents. Okay? And one thing that's really important is that it's not just everything that you have in the lakehouse and everything you have in Databricks. It also connects to your Google Drive, it connects to your SharePoint, it connects to your email, Google Calendar—anything that you set up—and it can actually branch out and access all of these things that you have there. And the research team developed an algorithm called OntoRank. Okay, I know it's a bad name, but, you know, the research team came up with it, and it's like "ontology rank." But what is OntoRank? So, what OntoRank does—it's—it's kind of like PageRank, for those of you who remember. So, PageRank was how Google, 25 years ago or 30 years ago, figured out how to take the whole web and create an index for what are the most important sites. Before Google, AltaVista, Yahoo—these sites, they were just looking at how many times this word was mentioned on a site. It led to these SEO (search engine optimization) tricks, where people were adding the same word a thousand times on a web page to get a higher ranking. But PageRank—it could just figure out from the graph itself what's important. Okay? So, OntoRank does a similar thing on all of the assets that you have in an organization, but it's not PageRank. This problem is harder because it's not just web pages with links; it's all the assets that you have, but they're actually different asset types. So, it needs to identify, you know, source code is different from a Google Doc. Also, here we have users. And the users inside of your organization—they're part of an org chart, and it actually matters how often they access things, so we have this information as well. So, we take all of these different asset types and all the people in the organization, and we construct this ontology graph that then helps us actually do amazing things with this for the agent. So, this is the context that we're going to feed. Now, you might say, "Hey, I already put together a context. I already built semantic layers at my company; I already have a business glossary that I built over the years." Genie can take that from any of the sources, so you can actually bring your own semantics as well. As part of Unity Catalog, we have something called Unity Catalog Semantics, where you can bring from our partners—whether it's from Atlan, or AtScale, or from existing BI tools that have semantics, or any business glossary that you already have—and you can have humans curate that. It'll happily take that and it'll help it get smarter. So, depending on whichever camp you're in—you think this should be completely autonomous and happen by itself, Genie Ontology does that; if you want to have lots of manual curation, you can do that with model semantics—they both work well together. Then these are fed into our agents. We'll talk about the agents that we have and we'll give you demos, but there are three categories of agents that are important, and I'll talk about them: Genie One, Genie Agents, and Genie Code. And they all are fed this context, which makes them faster, it makes them much more cost-effective, and it makes them much higher quality. Okay? So, you can much faster get the quality answers you want without paying too much. Okay, so that's what this is. If you're using other agents—you want to use other agents—they can use Genie as an MCP server, and they can just connect to that Genie agent and they can actually get the semantics from Genie. So, you can also do that this way: you can bring any client, any agent you want, and you can connect to this ontology that you have there so that you can get that semantics and the context into any existing AI that you have. So, this is what it looks like in the product. You can actually click on the graph, and you can sort of explore it. In this particular graph that we're exploring here—this is just one instance of Databricks for ourselves, this is our own Databricks that we have for Databricks employees—it has over 4.5 million ontology data snippets that it has collected and constructed. So, obviously, this is nothing you could do live. It would be infeasible for any of these agents to live go one-by-one and look through 4.5 million ontology snippets, but it's done that, and it's constructed this so that you can actually have this. Okay, so that's super awesome. So, this is now fed to our agents. The most important agent that I'm excited about is Genie One. Okay, so let me tell you about Genie One. Some of you might already be using Genie at Databricks. For the last three years, Genie has been part of the platform. This is not that Genie. This is Genie One, so it's the one place you go to access all of the data assets in your organization. Okay, so the Genie that we had previously that you're using—it's still there, but that's confined to a certain domain. You have, say, marketing people asking just marketing questions in a Genie space or room. Genie One is just one place any of your business users in your organization can log into, so you should just enable automatic identity management for all of your employees. If you have 100,000 employees, they then log in and they just get Genie One. They don't get the data warehouses; they don't get all the rest of the Databricks infrastructure. You know, they're not going to be able to go and spin up data warehouses and spend a lot of money; they're just getting a simple interface that looks like this, and they just ask their questions there. So, it looks like any other agent that you might have, but there are some differences here. First of all, it now can get data from any of the sources that you have at the bottom of the screen—so, whether it's Google Drive, or SharePoint, or whatever it is. It, of course, has all the goodies that other agents have, like you can have skills, you can schedule tasks, and it can do co-work activities for you. But the key thing is: it has access to Genie Ontology. Okay, and that changes everything. So, I'm very excited about Genie One. Please enable your organization to have it and also use it. My favorite way of using it is on the phone. Most of us use it on the phone, on our iPhone or Android, and you can see it's visual. You see all the graphs, and it's integrated with our AI BI dashboards, so it's basically like a BI tool or a dashboard. Okay, so that's Genie One. We're also announcing Genie Agents. Something is happening with software, okay, and people are talking about it. So, what is that? What does the classic software-as-a-service (SaaS) stack look like over the last two to three decades? It kind of looked like this. You had a system of record, which was a database. That's where you had the most important golden records about your HR data, or your CRM data, or whatever it was. And then you had some custom workflows inside of that. No one really loved those UIs, but most of us learned them, and then once we learned them, we didn't want to switch to a new one. And we had one of these for each of the domains inside of the organization. So you have one for Sales, you have one for Ops, you have one for Finance. So there's one of these for each. But what's happened in the last two years is that each of these vendors is now providing you an agent of their own. They are saying, "Hey, we have an agent for this. Okay, and this gets quickly very complicated, and most of these vendors don't want to play well together anyway because they're competing with each other. So it's sort of very messy inside of the enterprise right now, and it's very confusing. How do you make that actually work and go across these different systems? So the big question is, what does the future of the software stack look like, you know, or the SaaS software or the apps of the future? What do they look like? What are they going to look like? Okay, and we don't know, but we think it should look something like this, right? You have all your agents, and they should just access an agent system of record or an agentic system of record. They should just be there, and you should be able to access that seamlessly. It should be very fast. You shouldn't have to go through lots of other agents. You shouldn't have to try to go to a production database—that's too risky. You should just be able to have it at your fingertips and let these agents do that really fast, quickly, cost-efficiently, and securely. So what would the agent system of record look like in the future? What do we think that's going to look like? Well, we think it probably looks something like this. You have all your data, and you can access it all in one place in an open format. Okay, you see where this is headed. Okay, so I can just click through the next few slides. On top of that, you want to have unified governance, right? So you want to be able to have control. You want to be able to do the cost management on top of that. And of course, you don't want these agents to go one doc at a time and spin up tokens and not know what they're doing. You want them to have the enterprise context so they can quickly do that, and the context should span all of these different organizations. Okay? So we think that the new system of record is actually the data and AI platform that we've been talking about all along. We think that the world will look more like this. Whether it's Databricks or someone else, this is going to be the structure of the agentic system of record of the future. That way, you can get choice. You get the choice of running this on any cloud, any SaaS vendor, and any of the AI models. You can do the cost controls, and you can do the governance controls. When millions of AI agents meet

Google DeepMind(898.0K)·42:38·35.6K views·2026-06-23
Nenad TomaševSenior Staff Research Scientist, Google DeepMind Hannah FryHost
TL;DRThe future of AI is shifting from passive language models to an "agentic economy" of specialized, autonomous systems capable of executing complex, multi-step tasks.
Safely realizing this distributed "society of specialists" requires navigating systemic risks such as human...

TL;DR

The future of AI is shifting from passive language models to an "agentic economy" of specialized, autonomous systems capable of executing complex, multi-step tasks. Safely realizing this distributed "society of specialists" requires navigating systemic risks such as human automation bias, web-based agentic traps, and the systemic vulnerabilities of cognitive monoculture.

The Paradigm Shift: From Passive Models to Autonomous Agents

  • Active Execution vs. Text Outputs: Traditional large language models (LLMs) only generate textual responses to prompts. In contrast, AI agents utilize an LLM "harness" that allows them to observe the state of their environment, make decisions, and execute multi-step actions (such as booking tickets or sending emails) autonomously.
  • The Tools and Technology: While agentic systems historically operated in narrow, non-linguistic settings (like operational data centers or gameplay), open-source tools like OpenClaw and next-generation platforms like Gemini Spark and Antigravity are rapidly bringing language-capable agents to the mainstream.
  • The Managerial Role for Humans: Humans are transitioning from active content creators to high-level supervisors and decision-makers who define budgets, set goals, and review agent-proposed plans.
  • The Psychological Risk of Automation Bias:
      • Blind Trust: As agents successfully complete repetitive tasks, human supervisors naturally begin to over-trust them, switching off active monitoring.
      • Subtle Failures: This trust allows highly subtle, non-obvious machine errors or hallucinations to slip through unnoticed, necessitating strict protocols to keep humans actively engaged in the loop.

The "Society of Specialists" and Agentic Delegation

  • Replicating Humanity-Level Intelligence: Nenad Tomašev argues that the industry's pursuit of Artificial General Intelligence (AGI) should focus on "humanity-level intelligence" rather than "human-level intelligence." Rather than building one massive, expensive, generalist model, the future lies in a distributed network of cheaper, certified specialist models coordinated by a generalist layer.
  • Parallelization vs. True Delegation:
      • Current multi-agent workflows primarily rely on basic *parallelization*, where tasks are simply chunked into random sub-parts and run simultaneously.
      • True *intelligent delegation* requires establishing formal contracts between delegator and delegatee agents, complete with protocols for managing delays, verifying subjective preferences, and handling dynamic sub-task failures.
  • Reverse Delegation (AI-to-Human): In specialized fields like medical radiology, research shows that the most effective teams occur when superhuman, narrow AI models defer and delegate to human experts only when they encounter ambiguous or uncertain data.

Cybersecurity in an Agentic World: Traps and Monoculture

  • Agentic Traps and Web Poisoning: Because agents consume the web to execute tasks, malicious actors are building environments designed to exploit them:
      • Prompt Injection: Attackers place hidden, non-visual HTML tokens on web pages that redirect the agent's goals without a human supervisor noticing.
      • Dynamic Cloaking: Exploitative websites can detect whether a visitor is a human or an agent, serving altered content specifically designed to jailbreak interacting agents.
  • The Risk of Cognitive Monoculture:
      • Correlated Failures: Because most consumer agents rely on a select handful of dominant foundation models (e.g., Gemini, ChatGPT, Claude), they tend to share identical underlying cognitive biases.
      • Systemic Collusion: When millions of agents make highly correlated decisions, it introduces systemic risks like synchronized financial market "flash crashes" or covert algorithmic auction collusion.
  • Defense through Depth: Mitigating these risks requires layering multiple defensive strategies, including certifying trusted web sources, strictly limiting agent-granted wallet permissions, and building robust model-level guardrails.

Transforming Scientific Discovery through Autonomy

  • The Path to Autonomous Research: The ultimate goal for agentic systems at Google DeepMind is to advance science, medicine, and human welfare by allowing agents to close the loop in automated research laboratories.
  • Physical vs. Digital Safetey:
      • In software engineering, agents can easily verify tasks by running automated unit tests.
      • In physical sciences (such as biotech or material battery design), running physical experiments introduces real-world hazards. Agents must operate under strict safety parameters to avoid hardware damage, overheating, or chemical incidents.
  • The Limits of "Combinatorial Closure": Current agents excel at synthesising, combining, and operating within the boundaries of existing human datasets. However, they have not yet demonstrated the capability to make entirely paradigm-shifting scientific discoveries outside of known human frameworks.

Notable Quotes

"We see [AGI] not as human-level intelligence... we see it more as humanity-level intelligence where anything that any human may possibly be able to do, but there is no single human that is capable of doing so many things at once." — Nenad Tomašev, Senior Staff Research Scientist at Google DeepMind
"Trust is given but it's also earned... Even if it's mostly reliable, it shouldn't be blindly trusted, we should still verify its actions." — Nenad Tomašev, Senior Staff Research Scientist at Google DeepMind
"Maybe replicating human-level intelligence isn't the ultimate goal. Maybe the way forward is to replicate humanity-level intelligence instead." — Hannah Fry, Host
The Podcast. Now, not very long ago, an AI assistant essentially meant a large language model. You asked it a question, it gave you an answer, but it couldn't go off and perform tasks on your behalf. All of that is changing with the advent of AI agents. While Google DeepMind has this long history of developing agents stretching back to reinforcement learning in games, for most of us, they hadn't really arrived. And then we saw open-source tools like OpenClaw released into the wild. And at Google, a new generation of agentic tools is here, including Gemini Spark and Antigravity. But what happens when millions of AI agents are not just working for us, but transacting, negotiating, delegating to each other? Do we end up with a new kind of economy, a new route to AGI? And how on earth do we keep all of that safe? Well, one of the people trying to answer these questions is Nenad Tomašev, Senior Staff Research Scientist at Google DeepMind. Nenad, thank you so much for joining me. Very happy to be here. I think we should probably start at the beginning here, because for people who have only played around with large language models, could you describe to us the difference between that experience and acting with an agent? Yeah, no, definitely. I think this is becoming one of the main trends we're seeing this year. And it's interesting because agents are not a new concept. It's something that we've been looking at in the context of AI for a long time. Even before large language models, we had agents operating in simulated 3D environments, going on collecting items, and completing some tasks. This was back in the days where we were really prioritizing actioning in the world as a way of manifesting intelligence. Now, similarly nowadays, I guess you could say that the main conceptual difference between just a language model and an agent is that an agent observes a state of the world and performs an action—makes an action in the world, in the environment that it's given—whereas a language model just gives you a continuation, a reply to a prompt, to a query. Now, obviously agents that we use nowadays use large language models under the hood, so the two concepts are not completely disambiguated. It still is the large language models formulating the actions; it's just that there is a harness around it made to enact the changes once they have been proposed. But it has a lot more autonomy to chain decisions together, I guess. Correct. And I guess this is ultimately the motivation, right? Because you could do everything—most things that an agent can do—manually, painstakingly, by interacting with a language model very many times and with you guiding the whole process. Whereas, an agent instantiates this harness that automates some of that away, giving you less work and giving the language model—or, you know, the agent—more autonomy to complete tasks. So if you want something done that takes multiple steps, that agent can make a plan and take actions on all of those steps, obviously requiring approval or human input for those actions that are, you know, let's say more sensitive or more likely to go wrong. How is it different though? I mean, if you're used to interacting with a large language model by now, what would it be like interacting with an agent? In many ways, it's similar; your interaction interface is somewhat similar. You're still talking to the agent in a way in which you'd be talking to a language model because there is a language model there. But because the agent is doing more things for you, you're more in a position of a decision-maker to review and approve. But, but an agent—I mean, it would be much more useful really in that kind of scenario. 100%, especially because agents are given access to all of these tools. So you could—you don't have to, but you could—give an agent access to your Gmail and give it permissions to send out an email. Of course, there is a chance of it sending something wrong, so you need to verify what it has composed. But in principle, by giving access to tools to agents, you just empower your large language model to do these things for you. And then the whole job is done. The organization has happened without you having to lift a finger. Ideally, presuming no mistakes have been made, but yes. Yeah, ideally is quite an important point there. So okay, where we are right now, what tasks are agents actually good at? I think that where we are focusing a lot of our energy on—and by "we" I don't mean we as Google, but we as the entire field—is on the coding capabilities of agents. This is just because so many formal processes and tasks can be formulated as software or as code. In terms of where they're currently at in the real world, speaking of coding, we see lots of coding tools get used. We use them here internally, people use them externally, and it's really accelerating the development of software. This is bringing the human focus onto ideas and the design rather than the painstaking implementation of boilerplate around them, which used to take a lot of time, a lot of skill, and very bespoke knowledge. Now, that can just be done by language models easily. But then at the same time, we are still at a stage where you have to keep a human in the loop throughout this. I mean, why? What can't these things do at the moment that means that it requires human oversight? I wouldn't even make a distinction between whether they can or cannot. It's more that every single thing that they can do, they don't do with 100% accuracy. So every action, like with humans at the end of the day, has a certain failure rate. And the more complex the action, the higher the expected failure rate—again, like with any form of intelligence, the human one included. So while, you know, you may expect that an agent will execute the task correctly, it may still make a mistake. And this mistake may be obvious, or it may be very subtle. This is actually an important point, because there is this thing that has existed in other domains as well for a long time where different machine learning models have been deployed, and that is automation bias. In this context, if you're using an agent and it does well—it builds one thing well, it builds the second thing well—eventually you switch off. You start trusting it too much, and you fail to verify, and you fail to find some important issue underneath. Then mistakes slip through. Exactly. So for humans, it's important not only to be in the loop—because we are obviously designing these harnesses to keep humans in the loop—but to really be engaged and be switched on. As soon as you switch off, you're rolling the dice. So okay, in the long term then, I mean, it sort of sounds like we're in this transition period where these things are sort of becoming more capable. But in the long term, I mean, how much of a difference do you think that this is going to make? I mean, will this completely transform the way that we use artificial intelligence? 100%. I think it's impossible to envision a world where there isn't some kind of a deep disruption. What we're all trying to figure out is exactly what that is going to look like. Just explain to me how that, how that might work. Yeah, so this is the idea that one of the bottlenecks—one that we haven't mentioned yet—is that where we would really like to get help from agents are very complex tasks. When managing a big event, some of the bookings fall through, some accidents happen, and some things don't arrive on time. So whenever you have a big coordination challenge, there are lots of things that go wrong, and in the process of managing that as a human, you need to deal with all of those delays and problems. Similarly, an agent that delegates to a group of agents needs to manage all of the problems that may arise. One thing that's currently the case in many of the multi-agent systems that we see is that they act more as parallelization than delegation. You may have many agents working on things, but rather than there being an intelligent framework around how the work is split up, it has to just be chunked into sort of random sub-parts that are handed off. They get done in parallel, so you get a speed-up—presuming that all of this is reliable and each agent can complete its task independently. But this is not the intelligent delegation framework that we talk about. So okay, if the tasks are split up in an essentially random way, you could have one agent that is buying the wine and another one that is buying glasses, not realizing that it's wine glasses that are required. There's sort of no communication between them. Is that the kind of potential problem that could arise? Potentially, but you're also, I guess, hitting on another point, which is that many of the uses we see are uses in, again, software engineering, for example, with agents at the moment. That is a part of the reason because, in software, when you're building software, you can write tests—unit tests, as we say, right?—and run them and verify that the code has been written, at least in isolation, and performs the function. But when it comes to many of these real-world tasks, verification is not necessarily as straightforward. Maybe there is a subjective element involved. How do you define nice tasting wine, for instance? Maybe, yes, a bit of subjectivity in that. But this is actually quite important when it comes to AI and language models because there is a notion of reward hacking that has existed in various contexts in the field for a while. There could be situations where it does something that meets the request but isn't in the spirit of the request, technically. For that reason, you know, you really want to emphasize verifiability and to be very formal about the contract that is made between the delegator and the delegatee in that setup. At the same time, for tasks, we need to recognize that some are completely reversible. If something goes wrong, there is no harm; you just rerun the task, retry, or re-delegate.
