Now, for these more general systems, again, if an AI can recognize when it needs approvals and permissions for sensitive actions, then it does make sense to delegate those decisions to humans at the very least, right? Just looking at the other side of this, I also want to think about the sort of cybersecurity element of this. Because as more and more agents are out there interacting in the world, on the internet and so on, there are inevitably going to be people who are trying to exploit the vulnerabilities of agents. Tell me a little bit about agentic traps that people are laying. This is a scary and a fascinating topic at the same time, I'd say. And I think it's one of the main reasons why these kinds of deployments at scale cannot work right away. Because, as we said, if there is not complete reliability of individual interactions, any system at scale that has many interactions is naturally going to statistically fail. And because these systems take a lot of compute, and therefore energy and money to run, if they're not reliable, it's just a non-starter. Agentic traps are something that we have been thinking about for quite a while now. They can manifest in different ways—there are many types of traps—but it boils down to the fact that agents operate within an environment. In this context, the environment is the web. If the environment itself is poisoned, if the traps are laid, agents may stumble upon them when interacting with the web. And then, yes, malicious people—or malicious agents deployed by malicious people—can place those traps and then compromise systems, really. So, I don't know, the—the wine buying agent for the wedding goes onto a particular wine merchant where there is, essentially, a prompt injector in the website that changes the agent's goals. Is that the sort of thing that we're talking about here? That is one way this could happen, yes. And the reason why that may potentially go unnoticed is, you know, in terms of how web pages are encoded, there are elements there that are just not rendered visually. So if we're talking about an agent that isn't a visual computer-use agent that sees the web page—I mean, the pixels the same way a human does—but rather consumes the actual format of the page in its, you know, raw format, then it could inadvertently consume those hidden tokens that can make it do, do different things than what the intention was, right? But this is not the only way it may happen. What malicious websites could potentially do, they could do what we refer to as dynamic cloaking as well. They display pages differently for humans and agents because, based on the behavior on a page, you can make a very good guess as to whether it is a human or it is an agent interacting with the page. Then, only if an agent is interacting with a page with a specific intent do you tweak the content in such a way so as to induce some kind of jailbreaking. But just kind of going a little bit further on this, you could have agentic traps out there that are designed to, sort of, take money from you, to—I mean, do all kinds of things. Yes, and this has happened to people who have experimented with agents and have given them access to wallets, right, to do things. As you say, in the early days of this all when we are especially experimenting internally, or anyone else is, this is done in a trusted environment. So you don't necessarily, in your early prototyping, have to deal with any of this. It's not in the wild. Yes, but once you deploy on the web—especially now with AI really being used in all sorts of places—the more agents there are, the more incentives there are for malicious people to do malicious things because they have a higher surface area to target. And I think we're at the point where most of the web is currently being generated by agents and consumed by agents, that the agentic use of the web is exceeding that of, of humans, which is maybe happening for the first time. Okay, two things. First of all, it sort of sounds like you're describing that we're entering into this phase where there's, like, two different forms of the web: the sort of human version and the agentic version with dynamic cloaking and so on. It's a sort of a version of the web where adverts don't mean anything anymore; you know, it's not sort of human eyeballs that you can possibly sell to. But I think that the second point about this is: how on earth do you mitigate against it? If you don't have control over the environment—which you don't, over the web—how on earth do you protect your agent from going rogue? In some sense, it's not a new problem, right? Because the security of the web has, in other ways, been—been an issue before, and computer viruses could spread if you open the wrong attachment in your inbox, right, or you click on something on an untrusted page. So it's not the first time we're experiencing the need to certify the—the resources we're interacting with. When it comes to machine learning systems, let's say adversarial examples have existed for a long time, where imperceptible changes in images—imperceptible to humans—can jailbreak models. Here, you can do the same, whether it's a few pixels here and there, or you modify the least significant bit of the—of the encoding in a number of places. So you can adjust things ever so slightly in a way in which a human may not be able to spot, and still have some kind of a negative impact on an agent. It sounds a bit like you're saying here that—that when it comes to building guardrails, thinking about safety, you have to think about it external to the agent itself rather than just—just what you are specifically building. I think that the lesson is you need to think about both. One notion that we talk about in some of our other work—this is, I guess, relevant here as well—is the notion of defense-in-depth, which is not a new idea, again, by any means. This is just the recognition that because the problem is so hard, there is not going to be one solution to resolve all of the issues. Rather, we need to be building mitigations upon mitigations upon mitigations. And when layering them, hopefully the—the net is tight enough that very few things would slip through. So in the context of this, yes, you may want to certify and attest the content of web pages, have very good notions of trust for resources you're interacting with, and also have some mitigations on the agent side. You should have mitigations on the model side when it comes to foundation models run underneath, have meaningful human controls to be able to step in if something happens, and be very mindful of permissions granted to the agents so that, even if it gets jailbroken when interacting with something, the damage is minimal. All of those things combined together should then, hopefully, lead to some sort of safety that we are comfortable with. Just going back to what you were—what we were talking about earlier—this, this idea of there being multiple agents that are interacting with each other. Just tell me a little bit more about this idea that you have of a formal agentic economy. Just explain to me how that might work. Right, so in the context of us, let's say normal users of the technology on a day-to-day basis, you may have a personal assistant that has some persistent memory of you, has a good understanding of your desires and preferences. And, depending—again, depending on how much agency you want to grant this assistant—it may go on and negotiate some things for you. You may grant it some budget for that, and there can be a kind of a localized economy of these assistants negotiating stuff. I think I want to get a sense of how this might actually work if you have, you know, lots of people who are using agents as their own personal assistant. So okay, let's say that there's a concert—there's, like, a Taylor Swift concert, a live event—and tickets have just gone on sale. How would that actually work if you have all of these agents rushing the site all at once? I haven't been purchasing highly contested tickets very recently, but in principle... Not a Taylor Swift fan? No? No, my music tastes go in a very different direction, I'm afraid. What kind of music do you listen to? Well, obscure sub-genres of metal probably, so maybe not, you know... Okay, there is an obscure sub-genre of metal who are holding a concert, and there is an auction being held between the various agents. How do you decide what wins the auction? Is it just whoever can pay the most? This is a design choice, and that's also an important point to make: if we are to ever do something like that, then we are in control in terms of how we're making the system fair. It's an explicit decision made by someone who is setting up the auction. If you want to make things completely fair, in the sense that everyone gets equal access to some goods—concert tickets, in this case—then you can give each and every agent participating in these repeated auctions the same budget. We're not talking about one ticket on one auction in particular, but maybe for all the ticket purchases. And then the agents, knowing both your overall preferences—your desire to go see a certain artist—and also your travel schedule, time availability, and other constraints, can decide to allocate that budget in the best way possible. Whatever that means, they do it in the way that reflects what you want the best, so that they're more likely than not to win tickets in the way which works for you. In aggregate, when you distribute that across all people, you would hope to get a sort of a fair outcome at the population scale. I mean, I guess there are ballot systems, point systems, and various types of—of ways around this that—that people in human-based systems have come up with in the past. Just sort of stepping up from the trivial example of concert tickets—although not trivial for some, as I understand. I'm thinking here about some of the disruption that, for example, high-frequency trading algorithms have made in the—in the stock market. But agents, too, could end up having a really catastrophic impact on the stock market if deployed in a particular way. How do you prevent something like a flash crash from happening? Obviously, there is a higher risk, as you say. But financial markets have dealt with this risk for a while; they've obviously had their fair share of early bad experiences where things have gone wrong. But I think we can just learn about mitigations from the economies that have dealt with that already, so there is no need to reinvent the wheel. Admittedly, some things are slightly different in the agentic case. One particular thing that's different when we're talking about AI agents at the moment is that there is a handful of highly represented language models used in agents. If you look at the overall use of Claude, ChatGPT, Gemini, etc.—there are, obviously, open-source models, and many other models—it is that they tend to have similar opinions, and they take actions in similar ways. And this is what we often refer to as cognitive monoculture. So when you deploy, suddenly, hundreds of thousands or millions of artificial decision-makers, and they tend to make similar decisions, then failure points become correlated because the decisions are correlated. So one of the things that we need to be thinking about is how to diversify the decisions within our agents. Obviously, you can do this as a user—as a power user of a system—because you can make a very intricate system prompt that grants your agent some kind of a personality that biases it towards or against certain types of decisions. You can do that, but most people don't—don't do that with their—with their agents, with their models at the moment. Groupthink, essentially, agentic groupthink. Groupthink, and also collusion. You were talking about auctions before, and in human auctions, this notion obviously exists as well, where bids can be coordinated by groups to gain some kind of an advantage of a system. With agents, this is different in a sense that they may also coordinate through the environment in ways which are not obvious. They can potentially coordinate without communicating directly, so we need to be thinking about anti-collusion measures as well. Once you're detailing all of these, you know, potential concerns in safety, really, of the way that these agents might end up acting once out there in the wild, it does make a lot more sense as to why you guys have been slightly cautious about releasing them carefully and slowly, right? Yeah, that—that is true. I mean, this is the... this has been the story of every major technological disruption. I think if I take self-driving cars as an example—this is admittedly a very different piece of technology—but we have also been very excited about them for a very long time, seeing demos of these vehicles drive themselves. Getting them to the streets safely still took many more years and a lot more time, because that last mile is where most of the work tends to be. And I think when it comes to orchestrating and coordinating agents, at least because we want them to be doing human-like tasks, what we also need is not just technical solutions. A lot of this has to do also with policy and just the broader societal understanding of how to integrate these systems. At the end of the day, unless we have these fully autonomous agentic economies—which are maybe going to happen in the future but are not happening right now—we need to have humans in the loop in these systems. Therefore, we are integrating AI into human structures, and the two need to mesh well together. I guess there is a flip side to all of this, because human societies, when they come together, can actually achieve really remarkable things collectively. So presumably, the same can be true for agentic societies. One would hope. I mean, this is the idea behind why we would want to use multi-agent systems. I was talking about parallelization at the start of the conversation, right? If all of the agents are equally competent and they're doing similar things, then whether you do things sequentially or in parallel with many agents just gives you a little bit of velocity. But if we have agents that can do different things in different ways, then this is where things become really interesting. Actually, this is one thing we've not really brought up because we've been talking about these generalist agents: a part of the idea of an agentic economy is the existence of specialists, not just the existence of generalists. Now, we're obviously all trying to build agents that are as general and capable as possible—and there is "G" in AGI, there is artificial general intelligence that we're trying to achieve—but in an economic sense, and this is my, you know, personal view, this is not the point of convergence. This is not what we're going to arrive at. Look, I play chess unhealthily too much—a bit of a chess addict—and I've done work on AI for chess here, which is why I bring it up. But let's take that as a very non-controversial example. It's a game we all love. Gemini can play some chess, so can other models—actually, they were not able to for a very long time, so there has been some progress towards that—but you're still always going to use a chess engine instead. It's much faster, much more accurate, and far cheaper, because they are trying to do just one thing and one thing very well, which can be done with fewer parameters; the model is also entirely focused on that one thing that we're doing. And going back to humans, we are kind of like that as well. I think one mistake we sometimes make when we talk about AGI is that we see it not as human-level intelligence—even though this is what it's supposed to be in spirit—but we see it more as humanity-level intelligence, where anything that any human may possibly be able to do, but there is no single human that is capable of doing so many things at once. There are many things I don't know how to do. For some of them, I would wish I knew how to do them, like playing some instruments or doing some such thing. But brains have limited capacity, and we have limited time. So, at the end of the day, rather than having one humongous model that's very expensive and very slow, maybe we have instead a society of specialists, each of which can, in principle, be general if scaled up, if a bit larger, etc. I mean, I'm not talking about breakthroughs in architectures, more just how we split things up. And then those specialists are certified for those specific skills and cheaper to run, and because they are cheaper to run and more reliable, there is no economic incentive not to do that. So, there is a future in which there is some, maybe, more generic, general layer that's like a connecting tissue of this economy that knows everything and orchestrates everything, and then for very specific tasks you call other models. I mean, I guess what you're describing is like a distributed intelligence rather than an AGI. Correct. Yeah, which is what humans have, as you describe. If that does end up being the sort of—the version of AGI that we end up with—I'm sort of using inverted, inverted commas here—will that have to change how we think about safety and alignment if it is distributed across a number of different agents? 100%. I mean, you're no longer then aligning a single entity—or, maybe, yes, a single entity if you see that distributed entity as the entity. But our alignment approaches, as they exist at the moment, have to do with taking one model, observing its behavior, and trying to align that behavior with what we see as permissible or preferable or desirable, right? But then, when you have maybe 10,000 agents interacting in very intricate ways, it's not super trivial to align that whole system suddenly, or even to know what the system is. In this distributed world, agent A may be interacting with agent B today, but then, on a different task, it's interacting with agent C tomorrow, and C is sub-delegating something to agent D, and D is maybe consulting a human for something at some point in the loop. So, how this whole system gets coordinated—one of the ways in which we know how to do this in human societies is through economic incentives. And if these economies were set up for agents carefully, so that they're not causing some harm when they're profit-maximizing, right? This gives us at least a starting point through which we can try to align distributed agentic societies. This is not to say that, you know, obviously what we're doing today isn't relevant, because you need to have individual agents be safe as a prerequisite for groups of agents being safe, but we need to do far more on safeguarding against groups than we are maybe doing at the moment. An awful lot of work to do. Correct. In a very short span of time. Yes, indeed. That was absolutely fascinating. Thank you very much. Really enjoyed that. There is this idea of agents as AI that requires less from us—you know, less back-and-forth prompting, less waiting for a response, just something that gets on with the task at hand. But what I thought was really interesting about what Nenad said is that focusing on the idea of a single agent misses the bigger picture. Because, instead, each agent might end up forming part of a much bigger agentic society, where there are specialists and generalists, and agents who delegate and agents who focus on the details. That, I think, is the bit that's going to stay with me—that maybe replicating human-level intelligence isn't the ultimate goal. Maybe the way forward is to replicate humanity-level intelligence instead. You've been listening to Google DeepMind: The Podcast with me, Hannah Fry. If you like this episode, please make sure to subscribe to our YouTube channel. We'll see you soon.

Investing Starts with Education | Material Matters with SEC Chairman Paul Atkins

U.S. Securities and Exchange Commission(22.6K)·28:28·1.0K views·2026-06-23
Paul AtkinsChairman, SEC John MosesDirector of the Office of Investor Education and Assistance
TL;DRU.S.
Securities and Exchange Commission (SEC) Chairman Paul Atkins and John Moses, Director of the Office of Investor Education and Assistance, discuss the critical importance of bottom-up financial education, highlighting the transformative rollout of tax-advantaged...

TL;DR

U.S. Securities and Exchange Commission (SEC) Chairman Paul Atkins and John Moses, Director of the Office of Investor Education and Assistance, discuss the critical importance of bottom-up financial education, highlighting the transformative rollout of tax-advantaged "Trump Accounts" for children and modern defense strategies against increasingly sophisticated AI and cryptocurrency scams.

Fostering a Bottom-Up Investment Culture

  • Global Market Leadership: The U.S. capital markets represent half of the world's total global capitalization. Chairman Atkins notes that international counterparts often ask how to replicate America's investing ethos, which he argues must be built from the bottom up rather than top down.
  • The "What We Own" Mindset:
      • Traditional personal finance focuses heavily on what people earn (income) and what they owe (debt).
      • The SEC's educational mission focuses instead on what individuals *own*, positioning asset ownership as a core pillar of the American Dream as the nation approaches its "America 250" milestone.
  • Increased Personal Responsibility: Market innovations have made investing more accessible than ever, coinciding with a shift where individuals are increasingly responsible for funding their own retirement, higher education, and long-term expenses.

Grassroots Outreach and Financial Literacy Tools

  • Investor Assistance Services: Through `help.sec.gov`, any American investor can submit inquiries to receive assistance in locating old 401(k) accounts, understanding financial products, or navigating problems with financial professionals (though the SEC does not provide specific investment advice).
  • Digital Tools on Investor.gov:
      • Compound Growth Calculator: One of the most popular calculators on the web. Moses stresses that if financial assets are the "what" of investing, compound growth is the "why."
      • DRIPs: The SEC educates investors on the power of Dividend Reinvestment Programs to accelerate compound interest automatically.
  • Active Community Outreach:
      • The SEC conducts over 500 in-person and online outreach events annually, targeting military communities, older adults, high schools, colleges, and Historically Black Colleges and Universities (HBCUs).
      • Military Engagement: Atkins and Moses recently visited Camp Pendleton in California, speaking to 200 new Marine recruits about saving, investing, and starting early to maximize their greatest asset: time.

"Trump Accounts" and the Future of Youth Investing

  • The Core Concept: A new legislative initiative designed to make every eligible child’s first financial account an investment account, shifting the cultural paradigm around early wealth generation.
  • Key Features and Benefits:
      • Seed Funding: Eligible newborns receive $1,000 in government seed money. Moses advises families to "take the free money" by opening accounts promptly.
      • Structural Guardrails: Accounts are legally mandated to be low-fee, tax-advantaged, and diversified across broad-based index funds.
      • Long-Term Horizon: Funds are locked until the beneficiary turns 18. Upon reaching adulthood, the accounts can naturally transition into traditional or Roth IRAs to build a foundation for retirement.
  • Philanthropic Integration: The program allows private philanthropists to contribute to youth accounts. For example, the Michael & Susan Dell Foundation is actively contributing, and other donors are "adopting" entire states to fund local children's accounts.
  • Educational Impact: A dedicated resource hub is available at `investor.gov/trumpaccounts` to help families integrate these accounts into their broader financial planning.

Protecting Investors from Modern and AI-Driven Fraud

  • Digital Assets and Crypto Safety: Digital assets represent the most common category of inquiry for the SEC's investor assistance team.
      • Regulated Alternatives: For investors seeking exposure to digital assets, the SEC highlights regulated options like exchange-traded products (ETPs) and ETFs rather than complex, unregulated instruments.
      • Classic Red Flags: Investors are urged to watch out for high guaranteed returns, high-pressure urgency, and pitches that sound "too good to be true."
  • Emerging Scam Trends:
      • AI and Impersonation Scams: Scammers are using AI tools on an industrial scale to clone voices and mimic authority figures. The SEC recently issued an alert warning investors against bad actors using the likeness of SEC Commissioner Hester Peirce to endorse fraudulent products.
      • Relationship and Romance Scams: Often referred to as "long cons," these schemes build trust over time through business or personal relationships before introducing a fraudulent investment pitch.
      • Affinity Fraud: Scammers exploit trust within tight-knit communities, such as religious organizations, churches, and social clubs, to bypass investors' natural skepticism.

Notable Quotes

"If things like stocks and bonds and funds are the 'what' of investing, compound growth is really the 'why' of investing." — *John Moses*
"This idea of every child's first financial account being an investment account—it's really a big change in how we think about money... I think it has the prospect of really changing lives." — *Paul Atkins*
"We think actually the best defense against fraudulent crypto scams is good information and good awareness about investment basics overall, because many of the red flags of scams are similar or even the same." — *John Moses*
Investing is for everyone, or almost everyone. It is about the American dream—it is about increasing what we own as individuals to support ourselves and our families. There are a lot of complicated questions, a lot of things to consider. We want to arm people with the resources to do it in an informed way. Thanks to the President's initiative, a new development are the Trump accounts. This idea of every child's first financial account being an investment account is really a big change in how we think about money. And I think it has the prospect of really changing lives. Hello and welcome to another episode of Material Matters. Today, it's my great pleasure to sit down with John Moses, the SEC's Director of the Office of Investor Education and Assistance. While not always making headlines, the Office of Investor Education and Assistance plays an invaluable role in fulfilling our mission here at the SEC. By providing Americans with education resources and financial planning tools, John's office works every day to help American investors invest wisely, avoid fraud, and lay the foundation for a secure financial future. So today, we are going to discuss the work that the office is doing towards that end and much more. So let's get started. So, John, thanks for doing so much for American investors and for being here to discuss this. Well, it's a pleasure to be with you, Chairman Atkins, and it's an honor to help lead this team on the SEC's behalf. Well, you know, it's interesting. The U.S. now has, and has for a while had, the world's largest capital markets, and we are fully half of the global capitalization in our markets. Obviously, there are investors that are participating in our markets from around the world. Whenever I go abroad, to Japan, or the UK, or the European Union, my counterparts over there basically just say, "Well, how can we replicate this investment attitude, the ethos for investing, risk-taking, that Americans exemplify?" And, you know, they talk about doing it from the top down. You know, "We have to have this, that, and the other" to try to attract them. And so, how do you approach that, and maybe you could just cover what your office is doing and how you address these sorts of issues? Yeah, I think it's a good point. We have an opinion at our office, and I think it is something we feel here at the SEC throughout, that investing is for everyone, or almost everyone, as part of their, you know, financial planning and their financial journey. It is more accessible than it's been ever before, and we have all kinds of innovations and marketplace development, I think, to thank for that. This is in addition to a situation where we have people responsible for more and more of their long-term expenses, whether that's retirement, or education, or just saving up to buy a home. And there's a tremendous amount of enthusiasm and interest in personal finance. More and more, we think that enthusiasm and interest goes towards investing. Some, a lot of what we see in personal finance, might be about debt reduction, which is obviously really important, or about increasing one's salary, which is important. So, you know, what we earn and what we owe. Our office and the SEC really is about what we own. And, you know, as we think about America 250, more and more, it is about the American dream being about increasing what we own as individuals to support ourselves and our families. In terms of our office, maybe I would start with the work that we do on the investor assistance side. It might be they're trying to find an old 401(k), or they don't understand a certain product, and we can help them connect with the right resources. Of course, we can't give investment advice, but we can help them navigate definitions and navigate problems that they might be having with their accounts or their financial services professional. So, the assistance team, I think, is a really big part of what we do, and it helps give the thousands of people that contact us every year the confidence that they're on the right track. Another part of our office is the team—our Chief Counsel's team—that helps put out alerts and bulletins. We had a bulletin recently, for example, updating information on investing through health savings accounts, or HSAs. Yeah, it's amazing to see how money grows so quickly through compounding of interest or if you reinvest your dividends through a DRIP program—a direct reinvestment investor program. That sort of thing is really amazing, you know, how that works to your advantage. Absolutely, and that's the idea is that you can be doing something else, right? Like we just were out and talking to service members in California. They're doing another job; hopefully, they can have their money work for them while they're working to earn money. Yeah, it's a great question, and it's probably the most common type of question that we get at our investor assistance team. And so, there's a couple of things we're doing. First of all, of course, we have another site for digital assets on investor.gov where we're trying to inform individual investors about what's happening in this space. So, we want people to be informed investors and to be skeptical investors that are asking questions about product types, asking questions about people that are promoting products that maybe the investor doesn't fully understand, and to not let excitement about a new and newsworthy product type overcome the knowledge that they've built around, you know, investing fundamentals. And so, we try to get some information out there on investor.gov, and we'll be increasingly updating that. And we are also trying to make sure that people engage with this knowing what their options are. For example, if an investor just wants some exposure to some established digital assets, as you are well aware, they can do that through, you know, an SEC-regulated product like an exchange-traded fund—an exchange-traded product that allows them to get access—and they don't necessarily need to do something complex that they don't understand if they just want that basic access. And you were explaining this to the Marines, and I think that's, you know, an important foundational piece of information. As other information comes out, obviously there's more and more around tokenized securities, for example, and we've been working to put out information that's relevant to retail investors as those topics come up, so that people don't get confused and can't be tricked with information from promoters that is not true or is trying to make them do something that they're not ready to do with their digital asset investments, if they decide to go that way. I think overall, you know, sort of as we've been discussing here, there's a lot of things that individual investors can do in terms of tax-advantaged accounts, in terms of diversified, low-fee accounts, maybe in terms of investments that come with a match like from an employer. And we want to make sure they understand those basics, even as they are considering new types of investments like digital assets. So, again, I think it's all about context and basic information. Of course, we don't give advice about what to invest in, but we do encourage folks to look out for, you know, key red flags of fraud, and those exist in the digital assets and crypto space just like every other type of investment fraud. So, if you're getting pressured to act too quickly, if you're being promised high guaranteed returns—we know from investing fundamentals that all investments have risk. So, we think actually the best defense against fraudulent crypto scams is good information and good awareness about investment basics overall, because many of the red flags of scams are similar or even the same. Right. Well, and just basically, if it sounds too good to be true, it is, right? It's too good to be true, and you have to be very aware. Just because—you know, why is this person coming to you of all people? You don't know him from the man on the moon, and he doesn't know you either, and why is he pitching you the best thing since sliced bread? So, you have to be—and it has to be done immediately, like you were just saying, and the pressure-type of campaign that people can put on. So, all of those red flags, you know, are true for traditional things as well as novel issues. But are there any sort of—are you seeing any trends with respect to scammers these days? AI is a thing that comes up—its ability to mimic people's voices even by just, you know, taking snippets of your voice off of what you have on your voicemail answering greeting. So, things like that—are you seeing any trends there? Unfortunately, yes, we are seeing some, and certainly others, other regulators and others in the industry, are seeing these too. So, you mentioned AI. Certainly, we see frauds and scams are being perpetrated increasingly on an industrial scale, right? So, we see scammers that are, you know, doing full-fledged campaigns against American investors and trying to trick them. Maybe the platform is broad, but the pitch to the individual is specific based on what they know about you, going to what you just said. And then a few other sort of elements of that—and AI is being used for a lot of exciting things, but also in the commission of crimes. When it comes to things that we see a lot lately, I think maybe I'd flag two. One is impersonation scams. So, we see folks, as you suggested, being mimicked. We even see this, actually—Commissioner Peirce was kind enough to let us use her likeness in an alert we did late last year to warn people against scammers that are impersonating government officials, including SEC officials, including specifically Commissioner Peirce, because they know that she speaks on certain issues. So, they can sort of leverage that to suggest that she has endorsed products; of course, she doesn't do that. They make up wholesale accounts that she doesn't even have, and suggest that either fees need to be paid or things like that. And so, we want people to see these warnings before they fall for the scam. Again, if folks have a question, they should go to investor.gov or contact us at help.sec.gov to try to understand if something is fraudulent or not. So, impersonation scams are a big one. Maybe a second one is something that was really prominent a year or two ago, but certainly persists, and that's what we call relationship investment scams. So, often we think about these as romance scams, and we've seen those for years, maybe since the dawn of the internet. But these are not always romance-based. They can be fraudulent business pitches or other types of relationships that are built on some common interest, and maybe the investment scam doesn't come until much later, right? So, these are often what they call "long cons" that don't really reveal themselves to be based on an investment scam until later. And then you find yourself trying to follow this new friend or this new relationship that you have, follow their supposed success, and that's the beginning of an investment scam that can cost too many Americans too much money. And we see both of those increasingly. Well, these sorts of affinity frauds have plagued us for years. Bernie Madoff was one example of how he preyed on people of his same religion and his social circles and whatnot, and we see too often with churches and synagogues and other sorts of things where, you know, that affinity building up, breaking down the barriers of somebody having his guard up, and then using that to go in and scamming the people. It's one of the worst types of fraud out there. So, I know we're here at our time, but it's been wonderful to chat about this. It's a very important topic, obviously, for savings and investing for the future, but then also to be mindful about all the ne'er-do-wells who are out there who want to relieve you of your money and your investment. So, you have to be on guard. It can be a dangerous world, but if you are forearmed and forewarned, then hopefully you can avoid that—is, I guess, the timeless type of warning that we have. Absolutely, and I think, as you started at the beginning, there's an incredible amount of enthusiasm for investing as a way to build wealth, and we want to arm people with the resources to do it in an informed way. Exactly. So, all right, well, thanks for joining me today, and I'm sure we'll do this again too, as things are updated and move on in the real world. But thank you very much for doing this.

Top 10 Emerging Technologies 2026

World Economic Forum(1.1M)·33:31·714 views·2026-06-23
Jeremy JurgensManaging Director, World Economic Forum Mariette DiChristinaDean, College of Communication, Boston University
TL;DRThe World Economic Forum’s launch of the *Top 10 Emerging Technologies of 2026* report highlights breakthrough innovations—including quantum simulation, PFAS destruction, and AI world models—that are redefining scientific possibilities, decoupling…
resource capabilities...

TL;DR

The World Economic Forum’s launch of the *Top 10 Emerging Technologies of 2026* report highlights breakthrough innovations—including quantum simulation, PFAS destruction, and AI world models—that are redefining scientific possibilities, decoupling resource capabilities from geographic constraints, and demanding agile, non-siloed regulatory frameworks to scale responsibly.

The Track Record of Foresight

The World Economic Forum’s annual report has a proven track record of identifying revolutionary technologies years before they achieve global recognition or major scientific accolades.

  • Historical Accuracy:
    • CRISPR Gene Editing: Featured in the 2015 report, five years before winning the Nobel Prize in 2020.
    • mRNA Vaccines: Featured in the 2017 report, six years before winning the Nobel Prize in 2023.
    • AI-Led Molecular Design: Featured in the 2018 report, two years before DeepMind's AlphaFold breakthrough and six years before winning the Nobel Prize in 2024.

Redefining What is Scientifically Possible

New paradigms in computational power and biotechnology are dramatically lowering the cost of experimentation, allowing scientists to pursue complex, high-stakes hypotheses that were previously considered economically or technically unviable.

  • Quantum Simulation for Drug Discovery: Traditional drug discovery suffers from a 90% candidate failure rate. Quantum molecular simulation provides unmatched precision over classical computing, identifying and filtering out weak drug candidates early in the pipeline. This makes exploring treatments for rare or complex diseases financially viable.
  • Personalized mRNA Cancer Vaccines: Enabled by falling genomic sequencing costs and established mRNA manufacturing infrastructure, these therapies use a patient's own tumor biopsy as the exact blueprint to train their immune system to fight specific cancers.
  • AI World Models: Unlike traditional AI, these data-driven physical AI engines build simulations of physical reality. They can generalize and predict behaviors in complex physical systems even when encountering entirely novel scenarios.
  • Passive Radiative Cooling: An advanced thermodynamic innovation involving specialized material coatings that reflect solar radiation and emit heat at specific wavelengths, achieving a net cooling effect without consuming electricity.

Decoupling Resource Capability from Geography

Emerging localized technologies are reducing dependence on highly centralized global supply chains and heavy infrastructure, allowing remote or disadvantaged communities to access critical materials, energy, and food.

  • PFAS Molecular Destruction: Rather than merely containing and relocating toxic "forever chemicals" (PFAS), new chemical technologies are now capable of actively destroying these resilient molecules, offering a permanent solution for polluted soil and water.
  • Everything-to-Grid Energy: Intelligent optimization platforms allow local communities to link electric vehicles (EVs), building-integrated batteries, and micro-renewables (solar/wind) into self-sustaining local smart grids, bypassing centralized power plants.
  • Direct Lithium Extraction: Breakthroughs enable localized harvesting of lithium from geothermal and desalination brines, allowing countries without traditional lithium mines to secure battery materials locally.
  • Precision Fermentation: Utilizing localized biofactories to synthesize specific proteins, vaccines, or food resources. This technology has the potential to dramatically curtail global deforestation and supply chain emissions, though it raises distinct ethical and social transition questions.

Strategic Scaling, Regulation, and the "Future-Back" Approach

As technology advances at an exponential rate, traditional siloed management styles and slow regulatory timelines represent the primary bottlenecks to positive societal impact.

  • The Silo Trap: Decision-makers frequently evaluate emerging tech through isolated perspectives—such as immediate efficiency, risk mitigation, or regulatory compliance. Successful deployment requires balancing technological, societal, ethical, and regulatory conditions simultaneously.
  • The "Future-Back" Methodology: Organizations should avoid asking how to implement a technology and instead define the target future state (focused on growth, prosperity, and well-being) and work backward to establish the necessary deployment pathways.
  • Regulatory Agility: Traditional frameworks cannot keep pace with the speed of technical transformation. Governments must actively utilize regulatory sandboxes and show a willingness to dismantle outdated regulations to foster innovation.

Workforce Evolution and "Critical Doing"

The panel discussed how rapid technological disruption is shifting the demand for human skills, requiring a fundamental redesign of corporate and government training programs.

  • Openness to Change: With hard technical skills becoming redundant more quickly, the primary skill required of modern workforces is a psychological and structural openness to rapid workflow iteration.
  • Transition to "Critical Doing": While "critical thinking" remains highly valued, future resilience requires "critical doing"—developing hands-on agility, building collaborative prototypes, and iteratively adjusting tools in real time.
  • AI's True Workplace Value: Rather than replacing human workforces, advanced AI tools (such as those used in academic publishing) are automating routine administrative tasks, freeing employees to focus on relationship-building, qualitative analysis, and human-level collaboration.

Notable Quotes

"At least nine out of ten candidates for drug discovery fail... Quantum simulation opens up many, many new disease candidates that all of a sudden become economically worth pursuing." — Fred Fenter, *Chief Executive Editor, Frontiers*
"There are many communities around the world that have polluted water, soil, air... Forever chemicals may now not be forever, and I think that’s a really exciting breakthrough." — Katherine Daniell, *Director of the School of Cybernetics, Australian National University*
"What actually matters is asking an earlier question: what kind of future do we want this technology to enable... and kind of work backwards from that." — Abdulaziz Al Jaziri, *Deputy CEO, Dubai Future Foundation*
"We've done a lot of critical thinking... but the 'critical doing'—how do we actually get our hands on the tools, how do we continue to build together—is one of those skills of the future." — Katherine Daniell, *Director of the School of Cybernetics, Australian National University*

My question is: how do you think governments, employers, and education systems should work together so that technology and AI adoption becomes an opportunity rather than displacement or, you know, further impact on climate change? I'm keen to get the insights from the panelists. Thank you. All right, a very short final reflection. Maybe... Well, I mean, I'll just say quickly from my point of view as a publisher, what we're seeing is that, you know, we're adopting AI very, very deeply in the organization. And it, it will, it is affecting the jobs, but it's affecting them in a very positive way. It's taking, it's taking away a lot of the routine tasks, allowing people to, to connect at the human level—at the relationship level that is critically important for our business. And so, just to say, that's, that's the example that I know. I just see the very, very positive, the beneficial effects that AI is bringing to, to our organization. Yeah, I would say that it's really important to train politicians, leaders, and boards in what these technologies are, so they feel comfortable being able to ask questions about them. They need to figure out in their own organizations where they should be using these systems and where they might want to refrain from using them. Giving people the confidence to make those human judgments, I think, is one of the really important tasks. And I think maybe at the UAE level—both at the federal and at the local level in Dubai—we're embarking on the biggest training program for AI, teaching, kind of, civil servants how to use it. And I think, again and again, it has allowed us to do many different things, as simple as: we prototype much faster, we try out things much faster, we're able to think about things much faster, and we're able to put things on the table much faster to have a discussion with different partners around. The Next Billion Jobs

World Economic Forum(1.1M)·65:06·2.1K views·2026-06-23
Saadia ZahidiManaging Director, World Economic Forum Gilbert F. HoungboDirector-General, International Labour Organization (ILO) Faisal AlibrahimMinister of Economy and Planning of Saudi Arabia
TL;DRWhile the World Bank projects a 400 million youth job shortfall globally, the rapid expansion of AI and digital platforms offers an unprecedented engine for productivity and employment, provided governments, businesses, and educational institutions…
aggressively coordinate...

TL;DR

While the World Bank projects a 400 million youth job shortfall globally, the rapid expansion of AI and digital platforms offers an unprecedented engine for productivity and employment, provided governments, businesses, and educational institutions aggressively coordinate to prioritize entrepreneurship, continuous reskilling, and human-centric industrial policies.

Redefining Skills and the AI Productivity Paradox

  • The AI Value Gap: According to Peter Brown (Global Workforce Leader at PwC), research from the AI Jobs Barometer indicates that only 20% of organizations are capturing 74% of AI's economic value. These leading companies succeed because they fundamentally redesign work processes rather than simply layering technology over outdated frameworks.
  • The Human Skills Premium: While technical AI fluency is critical, employers are seeking human-centric skills—such as critical thinking, problem-solving, and qualitative judgment—five times faster for AI-exposed roles.
    • Candidates possessing these robust human skills command an average wage premium of 25% to 45%.
  • The Entry-Level Experience Paradox: PwC’s CEO survey revealed a stark market contradiction: 49% of CEOs expect entry-level hiring numbers to decrease, yet 89% of those same CEOs demand that incoming entry-level hires possess higher levels of experience and proven capabilities.
  • Adapting Traditional Industries: Chen Jiehui (President of Guangzhou Baiyunshan Pharmaceutical) highlighted how AI is compressing the traditional pharmaceutical "3 10s rule" (10 years, $1 billion cost, 10% success rate for a new drug) to just 5 or 6 years and a fraction of the cost, shifting demand from manual labor to high-level R&D and cellular technology specialists.

Fostering Entrepreneurship and Decentralized Job Engines

  • Shifting from Job Seeking to Job Creating: Vijay Eswaran (Executive Chairman of the QI Group) argued that the traditional 9-to-5 corporate career path is failing to keep pace with economic shifts. Educational systems must pivot to cultivate entrepreneurship and prepare youth to build new enterprises rather than compete for dwindling legacy roles.
  • The Myth of the "Solo" AI Founder: Addressing concerns that AI "agents" will enable solo entrepreneurs to scale without ever hiring employees, Neeti Mehta Shukla (Co-founder and Chief Impact Officer of Automation Anywhere) noted that AI will actually speed up the entrepreneurial lifecycle. While founders may start solo, increased scalability will allow them to hire and expand much faster than previous generations.
  • Democratizing Work Outside Urban Hubs: To combat the heavy concentration of new jobs in major metropolitan areas, Nourreddine Ouadah (Minister of the Knowledge Economy, Startups and Micro-Enterprises of Algeria) highlighted Algeria's digital freelancer initiative. By creating a simplified online registry, the government enabled 100,000 self-employed youth to secure digital contracts from rural and underserved regions within two years.

Reforming Policy and Academia through Integrated Collaboration

  • Connecting Industrial and Workforce Strategies: Peter Brown asserted that economic development, infrastructure investments, and industrial policies should never be designed in isolation. They must feature an integrated workforce strategy that outlines clear, non-negotiable pathways for youth entry, such as mandatory apprenticeship programs.
  • The "Innovate or Pay" Mandate: In Algeria, the government enacted a policy where legacy corporations must actively co-invest in university-level research, development, and startup incubators. If they choose not to innovate alongside academia, they face an equivalent corporate tax penalty.
  • Transforming University Infrastructure: Algeria transformed its higher education model by establishing business incubators, early-stage funds, and startup headquarters directly on university campuses. Students are now permitted to submit a functional business plan or a startup launch as their final graduation thesis instead of a traditional academic paper.
  • China's Planned Talent Pipeline: Chen Jiehui outlined China's highly coordinated model where the government acts as the macroeconomic planner, aligning long-term demographic data with industrial pipelines. In Guangdong province alone, this strategic coordination successfully funneled 1.1 million university graduates into targeted employment sectors last year.

Notable Quotes

"We are in institutions that were designed in the 18th century with teachers and lecturers from the 19th century preparing students for the 20th and 21st." — Vijay Eswaran, Executive Chairman, QI Group
"If you're still hiring just for domain expertise, you're probably hiring for yesterday." — Peter Brown, Global Workforce Leader, PwC
"Exactly the same thing we were saying about robotics decades ago, today we are saying about artificial intelligence... Now the challenge is in quickly gaining the right skills, because things are moving faster than university curriculums." — Nourreddine Ouadah, Minister of the Knowledge Economy, Startups and Micro-Enterprises of Algeria
"If the workforce is not taken care of... they are your IP, they are your competitive advantage in every form or fashion, doesn't matter which business you are." — Neeti Mehta Shukla, Co-founder and Chief Impact Officer, Automation Anywhere

So we are going to have, hopefully, a very dynamic panel talking about one of the most important issues facing most economies and societies today. And that is really around where will the next set of jobs come from, and are they going to be enough for this youth and future talent that will be coming into most workforces around the world in the next years. And there are some particular upsides and opportunities. So, we've never had young people be as educated as they are today, as tech-savvy as they are today. So there's potentially a huge boost coming to productivity, innovation, and creativity across most economies. And on the other hand, there are also some concerns. Are we looking at a very uncertain economic picture in which hiring has slowed down? Are we looking at potential effects from artificial intelligence that may create further risks? So the number of youth that are coming in is going to outstrip the number of jobs that will be created by about 400 million. Now talking about job creation, maybe one of the most important ways on my plate is to do innovation, but the right way, not just any type of innovation. Talking about innovation, we can talk about investing in research and development, which is the heart of innovation, but there are also other types of innovation that sometimes are taken only as financial return KPIs. And this type of innovation is not often the best way to have jobs created and sustainable jobs. So I think, dealing with innovation, what we are trying to do in our department is very important. But this innovation must be strongly linked to a deep technology and a big investment in human capital—education, health—but also in investing in research and development. And innovation can be one of these tools of massive job creation, not only jobs in big numbers, but also in a sustainable way. Thank you. Maybe I'll just ask a very quick follow-up question here. Pete, how do you see this issue and particularly some of the trends that you've picked up in the latest jobs barometer? Thank you. And I'd agree with much of what's been said. Everyone has started talking about how we need that better partnership between business, government, and how that could be put in place along with education system reform. Where has that worked, and what can we learn from it? So this is a very interesting topic. And it is true we are embracing AI; we proactively embrace AI. It is true that the number of graduates from university is huge in China. The total number of graduates in China is over 12.5 million. So that creates a big issue for employment. The Chinese government has a great advantage, really focusing on the plan for employment and industry. I quite agree with the previous experts. What you said is that in the short term, a new type of technology is a blow, but in the middle and long term, technology brings benefits. Usually, it will bring benefits to the industry, and probably people find they are not used to it at first as there is a structural conflict, but it generates new demand. And in China, you know, the government plays the role of a planner, a leader, and a coordinator. But the corporations, the companies, are the demanders, and universities are the suppliers. In our practice, usually speaking, the government has two plans. One is the employment plan. And for this year's graduates, actually 20 years ago we already made expectations when they were born. We knew when they would graduate from university, and that's why there was a plan made many years ago to bring these 12 million graduates into the workforce. The other one is the industry plan to create jobs. So employment is a priority of livelihood. The Chinese government put forward three industrial directions: one is the upgrade of the traditional industries, the emerging industry is to be scaled up, and the future industry is being planned and deployed. For example, biomanufacturing and quantum computing are part of the future to cultivate new sectors. To be specific, for example, I'm from Guangdong province. The Guangdong provincial government put forward an action plan—a million talents get together action plan—which means that 1 million graduates from university would be employed. Last year, 1.1 million graduates were employed. Guangzhou city alone employed 300,000 university graduates. For example, we employ graduates from Peking University and Tsinghua University; this year, our company already employed 10 graduates from Peking University and Tsinghua University. But also we have global recruitment because China has a very good planning system. What impresses us the most is that the guidance and plan of the Chinese government can strike a balance between the supplier and the demander. This sounds quite good. And our company, Baiyunshan Pharmaceutical—just now we talked about AI—fully embraces AI in different sectors. We talk about the application of AI in R&D, AI plus manufacturing and production, AI plus supply chain, and AI plus sales. So we fully utilize AI across every sector. In the past, we were a company focused on TCM or generic drugs, but now we are switching our role into innovative drugs. So AI provides very good opportunities. Twenty years ago when we were graduating from university, we had the internet revolution, but now it is AI. We expect all kinds of blows, this kind or that kind, but after we go through it, we find it quite good. In the times of AI, which is a very good technical revolution, we need to actively embrace it. In the past, we had the "three tens" rule: a new innovative drug takes 10 years, $1 billion, and the success rate is only 10%. But now, AI technology will accelerate this process. Maybe a new drug only needs 5 or 6 years. The investment may be not $1 billion, but 1 billion yuan. So that is a great advantage and progress, and that's why we want to use AI very well. Thank you. Thank you very much. Thank you. You've just spoken about the role of coordination that the government plays. Minister, let me bring this back to you. Industrial policy is also back in vogue in many parts of the world. How should the goals of those policies be oriented towards job creation alongside growth and competitiveness? And what's your experience in Algeria around that? Okay. So yes, of course, industrial strategy is very important and is back in several regions of the world. If we go back to the economic engine that has been set up by Clayton Christensen, who is one of the most famous authors on the knowledge-based economy, he says that we can do innovation in several ways. We can talk about empowering innovation, which is investment in research and development. We talk also about sustainable innovation when we are making this value chain for industry. And we talk also about financial innovation. For this last case, we are just focused on KPIs and financial return, and this is not really very productive or very effective for job creation. So, in one word, any industrial strategy must focus on empowering innovation. And at the same time, talking about empowering innovation, we are talking more about long-term investment than short-term investment. So the long-term is ensuring more job creation and more sustainability. The other very important point that has been mentioned by some of the esteemed experts on this panel is to allow the opportunity for the private sector to create jobs, and entrepreneurship is the key. Talking about university, we are talking about transforming the university. I can in a few seconds summarize what we did in Algeria. More than transforming the curriculum, we also transformed the mindset of the students in a way that today you can have your final project as a business unit project, as a startup project, or as a small SME project. And this transformation gives us very nice feedback in a way that the teachers and researchers will think about a concrete problem before talking about a project of final study. It is directing the research and the curriculum toward concrete problems and then having more sustainably created jobs. So this is a very interesting experience that we tried these last three years in Algeria, and today in each university in Algeria you have a business incubator, which was not the case three years ago. We then gave funding for these projects, and now we are creating funds inside the universities. So we have incubators inside the university, funds inside the universities, and more than that, we have startups inside the universities. We even have startups coming out from business and having their headquarters inside the campus. This mix between academia and business gives the opportunity for the young, for entrepreneurs, to be these job creators, and we are just paving the way as a government for them by putting a focus on innovation and making incentives. The last point we put in place this year—in general, in the world, we say "innovate or die." In Algeria, we have implemented what we call "innovate or pay." Algeria is a social country and we are still standing on the social aspect of our country, so we just work with the companies. You have to innovate, otherwise, you'll pay a tax. And this is a way that these companies are more involved in universities, are more involved in research and development, and if they don't innovate, they just have to pay a tax to the government. So it is better to innovate. Thank you very much. Thank you. Vijay, let me turn it over to you. So this role of the private sector—and I know that we're all focusing primarily here on what can be done to support SMEs and entrepreneurs. But what about some of the large corporations? What role can they play? They are often amongst the earliest adopters of new technologies. They do often provide a signal to the rest of the market and to the rest of the value chains around them, including some of those new and emerging entrepreneurs. How should they be thinking about workforce development in the future? The real question, I think, is not whether AI changes work, but whether AI changes the opportunity. It either can refine it, or define it, or narrow it. So the question here is, I think, it's very important that the reskilling starts and continues all the way throughout. Because at whichever juncture they join the workforce, they need to keep on reskilling because AI is moving at a phenomenal speed. And the reskilling should be part and parcel of their strategy, so to speak, moving ahead for all students coming out of it. So private-public partnership on reskilling initiatives between the university, for example, and students, as well as startups, you know, hatcheries can be put into place and all of this. The issue is that it needs to be worked together to develop it because AI is something we are still trying to figure out where it will be. And even the best of us are still making an educated guess, so to speak. It's moving faster than we can anticipate it. And AI is going to be programming AI moving forward, which is a whole new dimension, so to speak. So, having said that, entrepreneurs are going to be the ones who need to drive this. We have lost the entrepreneurial skills to a great extent because a nine-to-five existence has been part and parcel of what we have inherited, you know, from the West, as well as what we have inherited within the last 100 years. And entrepreneurs don't function that way. Hence the gig economy, per se, which is now the "in" thing that's happening, has opened the doors for them to do, you know, more than one skill set and develop it, and essentially the current generation prefers to work from home, even. So all of this is going to be interesting to see how it will melt together. But I think that the key to this is to be able to adapt, adjust, and accommodate because AI is going to move faster than we can anticipate it. But as a group of companies where we are focused on entrepreneurs as our backbone, so to speak, we build entrepreneurship and we are reinstilling those thought processes into the new workforce. Because the workforce over the last 100 years, or 50 years at least, has been very much a nine-to-five existence, they are coming out for ready-made jobs. So right now they need to reinvent that. They need to not be the finished product, but they need to actually keep finishing the product as they come along because the job market is not the same as it was. And that's, of course, part of the anxiety that a lot of young people are feeling at the moment because what they have been trained for and thinking about—especially those that are in professional degrees in universities and are expecting to go into sort of a knowledge worker economy—is that expectation. And I think, Pete, you referred to what you're seeing in some of those surveys and the viewpoints of younger people, as well as those that are already in entry-level work. So, Pete, you get to be Minister of Labour for a day or maybe longer. What are those one or two policies that you think could really change things in the next couple of years? Particularly, what are those where governments and business could work together on creating a smoother path for some of that workforce development to take place, and for younger people to get integrated into the workplace? You say for a day. So look, I think not working together is not an option. So let's assume you've got that close collaboration between, you know, business leaders, educators, and policymakers. I think really, for me, it's the connection between your industrial policy through to your workforce strategy. And for me, they are one and the same thing. So I think for me, if I was minister for the day, for any industrial policy, or anything to do with economic development, infrastructure projects, etc., I would want to see and make sure there is a workforce strategy connected to that. Is it around creating new apprenticeships? Is it around work experience? Is it, you know, project-based development by some of the employers? But fundamentally, what is the strategy around creating entry-level pathways? Because I think, you know, the new jobs will be created, as I've said before, and I'm very confident that will happen. But it's creating those access points and that door of opportunity into the world of work; we've got to make sure that doesn't narrow. And I think it's incumbent on all participants there to make sure it widens, actually. Thank you. Jiehui, would you like to add more to that? Actually, I feel that for the laborers and for the new job opportunities, we also are preparing and trying to create new tracks for talent development. We are in the medical and pharmaceutical industry. This is a traditional industry and we are now facing the pressure of the traditional jobs offering. So we are now looking at the new track like for the CGTN, and for the genetics and cellular technologies, and the antibody drugs, and also for the small nucleic nucleic acid and also the other innovative drugs. We will use fewer traditional workers, but we will have requirements for R&D people and for higher and more complex tasks. So this is the demand of the talent we are facing. Thank you. Neeti, I'm going to give you the final question of this round before we open it up to audience questions. In the same way that governments need to think about balancing sort of the work they do on the employment side with industrial policy and broader economic strategies, do you think that is happening in business? Or do you think there's a rush to automate and absorb and integrate new technologies without taking into account where people might be? No, I think it is top of mind for every business. And I think it's inherent in their competitive advantage, and it's inherent in their strategic planning for the long term and short term. If the workforce is not taken care of—I mean, they are your IP, they are your competitive advantage in every form and fashion, it doesn't matter which business you are—so definitely there is attention to workforce development. At the same time, yes, you're right, there's just a lot of technology coming forth. And because the productivity gains are faster than ever before, and the efficiency can be seen, and the ROI can be seen, there's a rush to push that through as well. So it's a balance of both. But I think in most businesses—or at least let me put it another way, most successful businesses—it is a balance; it is a yin and yang. And I think they're very, very much aware of it. The strategic leadership is aware, and the workforce is aware. At every level of the organization, there's a push towards reskilling, a push towards "how can I make sure my career is progressing the way it should, how can I continue contributing, where is the company going, where is the product going, where is the innovation going, and can I meet that demand?" So I think people are aware. There's another report by the GFC Council on Jobs and Frontier Technologies where they bring out, by industry, you know, where that balance is. And yes, some industries are moving faster, some industries are a bit slower, and some industries just have a lot of workforce that has to transition, so it's a bigger problem to scale, while some are a little bit more niche. So, I think, in short, the answer is yes, I do see a significant attention to detail for workforce development. I also see a significant push towards using these new technologies and not being left behind. And like I mentioned, I think government plays a big part and industry plays a big part in helping make sure that every organization is well positioned in order to deliver both. Thank you.
