Nature Is Infrastructure

World Economic Forum(1.1M)·61:19·346 views·2026-06-23
Jane GoodallFounder, Jane Goodall Institute Razan Al MubarakPresident, International Union for Conservation of Nature (IUCN) Gim Huay NeoManaging Director, World Economic Forum
TL;DRIntegrating natural capital into economic strategy is a core driver of long-term business competitiveness, profitability, and talent attraction.
To secure future prosperity, leaders must transition from regulatory compromises like "net zero" to absolute decarbonization...

TL;DR

Integrating natural capital into economic strategy is a core driver of long-term business competitiveness, profitability, and talent attraction. To secure future prosperity, leaders must transition from regulatory compromises like "net zero" to absolute decarbonization ("real zero") while deploying advanced biological and technological innovations.

Redefining Ecological Conservation and Capital

  • Pragmatic Adaptation over Restoration: Angela Moles (Professor of Ecology and Chair of the New South Wales Threatened Species Scientific Committee) argues that conservation must move away from trying to restore ecosystems to a historical, pre-colonization ideal. Because climate change makes past baselines obsolete, policies must focus on maximizing the biodiversity and biomes we can actively carry forward.
  • Systemic Financial Reporting: André Hoffmann (Vice-Chairman of Roche Holding and Co-Chair of the World Economic Forum) emphasizes that businesses are flying blind by only measuring financial flows. To prevent depleting foundational resources, companies must adopt standardized reporting systems—such as an integrated profit and loss account—that account for impacts on natural, human, and social capital.
  • Aligning Corporate Purpose: Hoffmann asserts that for industries like pharmaceuticals, saving human lives is fundamentally linked to preserving natural systems, which supply essential molecules, clean water, and clean energy.

Decoupling Growth from Fossil Fuels

  • The Economic Power of Biology: Ester Baiget (President and CEO of Novonesis) highlights that the biological transition is already commercially viable. In the 1980s, generating $100 of GDP required one barrel of oil; today, it takes less than half. Novonesis leverages biological solutions (enzymes, microbes, and proteins) to replace fossil fuels, chemicals, and fertilizers with biodegradable alternatives.
  • Proving Planet-Profit Alignment: Novonesis has reduced its carbon emissions by 63% since 2018 while growing revenue by more than 30% and maintaining a 37% EBITDA margin, demonstrating that planetary stewardship and corporate profitability are mutually reinforcing.
  • Securing Top-Tier Talent: Baiget notes that committing to planetary health is a primary driver for attracting younger generations of workers, who prioritize employment at companies that actively align with their values.
  • The Case for "Real Zero": Andrew Forrest (Executive Chairman and Founder of Fortescue) strongly critiques "net zero" frameworks and carbon credits, calling them bureaucratic loopholes that enable continued fossil fuel burning. He demands a commitment to "real zero"—the complete cessation of fossil fuel combustion.

Industrial and Engineering Innovations

  • Heavy Industry Electrification: Fortescue is systematically greening its heavy operations, transitioning massive haul trucks and 3-kilometer-long, 30,000-ton transport trains to green energy. This shift eliminates the use of 1 billion liters of diesel annually, directly boosting the company's bottom line.
  • Sparing Ground Resources: Danny Cheng Jing (Global VP of Jinko Solar) details how solar innovation has increased cell efficiency from 15% to 27% over the past decade. This technology increases energy density, reducing the physical land footprint required for solar installations by 60% to 70%.
  • Off-Grid and Space Infrastructure: To bypass terrestrial land constraints, Jinko Solar is investing in Building Integrated PV (BIPV) and researching space-based solar infrastructure, such as solar-powered satellites and orbital data centers.
  • Smarter Resource Consumption: Forrest argues that massive infrastructure projects, like data centers, should not plug into public grids and water mains. Instead, they should operate off-grid on cheap, open land, utilizing green energy to run reverse-osmosis systems that generate more water and power than the facility consumes.

Overcoming Policy Failures and Geopolitical Obstacles

  • Eradicating Perverse Subsidies: Panelists criticized the estimated $3 trillion in annual global fossil fuel subsidies, which artificially distort the market and force clean technologies to compete with one hand tied behind their back.
  • National Security as a Transition Driver: Decarbonization is increasingly propelled by national security and energy independence rather than climate policy alone. Countries like India, Brazil, Indonesia, and China are aggressively scaling localized, homegrown green energy to decouple from volatile fossil fuel import routes.
  • Scaling Green Hydrogen and Ammonia: Forrest urges nations—specifically China—to support the International Maritime Organization (IMO) proposals to mandate green fuels. Achieving green hydrogen production at $2 per kilogram would immediately make green ammonia highly competitive against fossil fuels for shipping and agriculture.

Notable Quotes

"If you do [economic growth] at the expense of nature, it's a short-term win at best." — *Angela Moles*
"If we continue to destroy the planet, we are going to go with it." — *André Hoffmann*
"We are showing the world that planet, profitability, and growth are two sides of the same coin." — *Ester Baiget*
"We are going green because it's good business, baby." — *Andrew Forrest*
Security and climate change at the Council on Foreign Relations in New York. It's my great honor to be your immoderate moderator today for our session on nature infrastructure. We have both a full house here in Dalian where I'm delighted to be back at Summer Davos, but also we welcome the audience joining us online. This session is, of course, on the record. We will have a robust discussion with our panelists whom I'll introduce in just a moment, and we will look forward to questions as well from the participants here. So, welcome to all of you. The topic, of course, is one of the most pressing ones, if sometimes a neglected topic—that is, the enormous opportunity as well as the essential nature of nature. Nature, a recent analysis by the World Economic Forum, has built on decades of literature in the area of what's been called natural capitalism or ecosystem analysis. This has really shown how essentially the global economy depends on the goods, the essential input of nature, both as an input but also really as a sustainer and ultimately as an enabler of the future of humanity. And yet, we underinvest by almost every measure in this, even setting aside the philosophical, moral, and ethical components—perhaps even the economic and business case for being engaged in investments in nature. So, we want to talk about this, and we want to talk about what works, but also what might be obstacles and challenges to moving ahead. We want to engage in this conversation with that spirit of can-do that we can bring to a difficult topic like this, because it has historically been an area where many people will get together, reasonable people say "yes, this is very important," and then everyone goes back to their corner and doesn't do very much about it. So, I want to get past the platitudes and get some concrete examples of what works and what challenges we need to overcome in this time that we are together. Let me introduce my distinguished panelists. As I mentioned, we are very fortunate to have, starting from my right, Ester Baiget, President and CEO of Novonesis of Denmark; André Hoffmann, Vice-Chairman of Roche Holding and also the Co-Chair here of the World Economic Forum; Andrew Forrest, Executive Chairman and Founder of Fortescue; Danny Cheng Jing, who is Global Vice President of Jinko Solar; and Angela Moles, Professor of Ecology and the Chair of the New South Wales Threatened Species Scientific Committee from Australia. Please give them a nice round of applause to welcome them to our stage. Angela Moles, let's start with you. Since you look at the big picture, you've spent a career, a lifetime thinking about this. You've worked on government commissions and helped your region develop policies in this area. Can you state for us the challenge ahead? I think the nature of the challenge is that for too long we've viewed economic growth, human well-being, climate, and nature as separate things. We have ministers of those things and they don't talk to each other very much. We have government parties purporting to represent one or the other of those things, and we have businesses who are aiming to maximize economic growth. If you do that at the expense of nature, it's a short-term win at best. So, you see this as something where there can be progress. Again, you've worked on this issue in your own home country. Have you made progress, can I ask, in Australia? A little, yes. Oh, so one of the things that we have to face as ecologists is that a lot of our past has been about putting things back to how they were when the world was perfect. Now, in the face of climate change, that is increasingly not actually possible. It's trying to hold back the tide. So, actually reframing biodiversity conservation away from "let's put it back to the pre-European colonization condition," or whatever it is in your country, to "how do we maximize what we're taking forward, instead of how do we cling to a vanishing past?" Interesting. So, in a sense, those are controversial words in some ecological corners, I know, but you make the case for a pragmatic way forward. Don't let the ideal be the enemy of the good, certainly, am I right? I think it's the only way. If we try to keep our favorite ecosystem in its current place, in the face of climate change, those component species aren't going to be able to survive. And so, what we need to do is to maximize what we do get to take forward. And this is, this is what we see if we look at past periods of rapid climate change—which I know weren't as rapid. What we see is species moving around a great deal, communities reassembling, and whole biomes popping up that weren't here before. So, if you look, for instance, at North America after the retreat of the giant ice sheet about 20,000 years ago, you only have to look back about 4,000 years before you get whole biomes that aren't actually present on the current planet. And those biomes existed under climatic conditions that don't exist today. Taking that from the past and looking forward, we're going into a new world of what climate-ecosystem interactions look like. We're going to have new biomes. It's not going to be tumbleweeds and dust. It's going to be different to now, and it's up to us to maximize what we can take forward, what we can keep. Interesting. Okay, so you've put forward a good provocation. I thank you for that. Maybe, André, if you'll allow me to come to you. You're a leader of industry globally, but particularly in Europe, and you come from a background of an industry that historically has, you know, made a lot of investments that people question. As our friend Andrew Forrest will also explain from mining, where there's been a tough relationship with nature, you have been a pioneer. You wrote a book on this topic that was well received a couple of years ago, encouraging fellow business leaders to take investments in nature more seriously. Can you tell us, how has this gone? How has this been seen by fellow global leaders that you interact with? Are they rising to the opportunity? Do they see it as something of a philanthropy or a pet project, or how do they see the opportunities as being more core to the business that they do day to day? Well, first of all, thank you very much for mentioning my book. That's always good for sales and things like that. The reality is that we—and I very much welcome your comments—we have these mental blocks which tell us, you know, we are separated from nature. What we do in the boardroom is not what we do in the factory, is not what we do in reality. And the reality is that we are all dependent on nature, and that nature, as the life on earth, is something that we need to look after if we really want to construct long-term models for sustainability and prosperity. So, the idea that in the pharmaceutical industry you did something against nature is, in fact, a little bit antinomic because we define our purpose as trying to do something for the patient. And if you want to work for the patient, we need to take into account not just how we're going to make them better once they are ill, but how we're going to prevent them from going ill. And so, if our purpose is to save lives, we need to also save life, and we need to do something about biodiversity. That's the broad comment, yeah. Now, individually, there are, of course, examples. For the pharmaceutical industry, the molecules that we search are present in nature; it's a way of looking after it. The processes we use—the use of water, the use of energy, the use of knowledge in our group, in our companies—you know, the social capital, the human capital, and the natural capital, are all equal components of the value creation process, which is what we try to put in place. So, without going too specific into examples, we do need to treat nature with respect if we want to continue to exist. So, a lot of these inputs are not necessarily priced appropriately. What do we do about the challenge of externalities, about these sort of deficiencies in the way that we value nature—what's been called natural capitalism—and thinking differently about this? Do you have some ideas from your own experience and that of Europe? Yes, so I think in many ways we are flying blind, you know. We're going into solutions, into ways of trying to optimize what we have in front of us, with a very little amount of information. We measure financial flows; we don't measure the impact we're having on the social, the human, and the natural capital. We do measure it in an ad hoc way in each individual company, and in our company we do this, of course, but we do not have a standardized approach to what it means to really understand an integrated profit and loss account. How can we get to profitability with the minimum possible impact on the three capitals, and how can we manage the interdependency of these three capitals in the best, optimum manner? If you want to create long-term prosperity, you cannot over-privilege one of the capitals. You know, if you have to grow at the cost of nature, or grow at the cost of the social system, or grow at the cost of the individual, you're not setting yourself properly for the future, and I think that's important. Ester, let me come to you. Your company leans in on innovation. How do you see this challenge of natural capital? First, I would like to maybe suggest to pause on the magnitude and the need for change. I love the way you describe it, Angela: "you only look back 4,000 years." I'm going to take that moving forward—only if you look back 4,000 years! But only if you look back and you pause today, it's important to embrace that the change is already happening. Not enough, not fast enough, and we need to talk about how to make it faster, but for sure embracing that we're already moving in the right direction. In the '80s, $100 of GDP needed a barrel of fossil solutions. Today, the same amount, actualized on net present value, needs less than half of a barrel of oil. So, we're already decoupling from fossil. It is not at the right speed, but it's already happening. And the reason why it's happening is because those solutions are already competitive. Green alternatives, green electricity, green decarbonizing, but also good carbons that are competitive to fossil are already in place. Here is where our company is helping. Novonesis, on bio-solutions—I would say broader than only what we do—is bringing solutions based on nature. It's already happening. Our solutions today touch more than 2 billion people every day, but we do that silently. We have to explain it more, that the change is already a reality and it's competitive. But the contribution we make is not only on what we produce, it's also on how we are producing, embracing the imprint we have on this planet. We have, since 2018, reduced our CO2 emissions by 63% and we have improved revenue by more than 30% at a 37% EBITDA margin. So, we're showing the world that planet and profitability and growth are two sides of the same coin. We're embracing circularity; 100% of our biomass is recycled. We're embracing water, a very precious feature that we're not speaking enough about, which is becoming more and more relevant. Investing a $120 Billion Balance Sheet with No Outside Investors

Invest Like The Best(78.5K)·76:29·4.3K views·2026-06-23
Vlad BarbalatPresident of Global Risk & Capital Solutions and CIO, Liberty Mutual Insurance Patrick O'ShaughnessyHost, Invest Like The Best
TL;DRVlad Barbalat, President of Global Risk & Capital Solutions and CIO at Liberty Mutual Insurance, manages a unique $120 billion balance sheet entirely free of third-party capital.
This permanent-capital structure enables a highly resilient "fortress balance sheet" that...

TL;DR

Vlad Barbalat, President of Global Risk & Capital Solutions and CIO at Liberty Mutual Insurance, manages a unique $120 billion balance sheet entirely free of third-party capital. This permanent-capital structure enables a highly resilient "fortress balance sheet" that prioritizes long-term investment hygiene, entrepreneurial risk-taking, and structural flexibility over short-term market pressures.

The Power of a $120 Billion Permanent Capital Balance Sheet

  • Structural Autonomy: Because Liberty Mutual is a mutual insurer, it operates without public shareholders demanding dividends or buybacks. This structural design allows the investment team to make long-term, value-driven decisions rather than expedient ones.
  • The Flywheel of Float: Underwriting premiums are systematically funneled into the investment platform. By investing this capital to support the broader economy—such as funding infrastructure, real estate, and private credit—the firm strengthens its capital base, allowing it to underwrite larger, more complex, and long-tailed risks.
  • Capital Allocation Breakdown: The $120 billion balance sheet is segmented into two primary components:
      • Reserves (~$70–$75 Billion): Tightly managed to ensure policyholder promises are met. Rather than passively holding low-yield bonds, LMI actively optimizes this portfolio to act as a dynamic liquidity provider in the markets.
      • Surplus Capital (~$45–$50 Billion): Allocated across "growth credit" (leveraged corporate credit, direct lending, asset-backed finance) and "growth equity" (private equity, real estate, energy, and infrastructure).

Investment Philosophy: "Prepare, Not Predict"

  • Agnostic to Macro Forecasting: LMI deliberately avoids tactical macro forecasting. Instead, the firm focuses on building a resilient, long-term portfolio capable of weathering diverse economic eventualities.
  • A Multidimensional Toolkit: Unlike traditional LPs constrained by rigid product mandates, LMI evaluates the exposures it desires first, then determines the best acquisition channel—whether via direct lending, co-investments, club deals, or backing specialized GPs.
  • The "Branded Capital" Proposition: LMI positions itself as an agile, creative, and highly responsive partner for general partners (GPs).
      • Relationship Velocity: The team operates with the speed of a GP rather than a slow-moving corporate LP, establishing LMI as a primary hub for off-market, highly structured transactions.
      • Strategic Philanthropy: LMI frequently acts as a connector, facilitating valuable, non-monetary introductions between business partners without requiring direct financial involvement.

From Soviet Moldova to American Agency

  • The Immigrant Perspective: Having immigrated to the US from Soviet-era Moldova in 1990 at age nine, Barbalat attributes his investment worldview to the profound contrast between oppressive systems and American individualism.
      • Persecution to Agency: Surviving anti-Semitic persecution in the USSR shaped a mindset characterized by zero entitlement and a deep appreciation for the permissionless innovation of the US.
  • The Croissant Analogy: Barbalat highlights America's unique economic engine through the lens of human creativity: while the Soviet system viewed bread as a mere utility, the US market allows individuals to endlessly iterate, perfect, and commercialize something as simple as a croissant.
  • Fostering Institutional Entrepreneurship: To prevent the complacency typical of large insurance firms, Barbalat has intentionally structured LMI's culture to incentivize curiosity and calculated risk-taking, ensuring the team remains hungry to "make things better."

Navigating the Invisible Future: AI, Volatility, and Private Markets

  • The Valuation Paradox: Rapid technological disruptions (especially AI) and shifting geopolitical supply chains make the long-term future highly unpredictable. This uncertainty is driving internal debates about whether corporate valuation multiples should be structurally lower across all industries.
  • Creative Destruction by 2030: LMI anticipates massive corporate turnarounds by the end of the decade, predicting that multiple trillion-dollar companies of 2030 do not yet exist, while several current hundred-billion-dollar giants will completely disappear.
  • Credit Duration Risk: While short-term corporate debt (under four years) remains highly secure, holding 30-year paper on legacy tech giants (such as Oracle or Salesforce) carries significantly higher structural risk due to the threat of AI disintermediation.
  • The Public-Private Divide: LMI maintains a strong preference for private equity over public markets. Private markets now offer the scale and prestige of public markets without forcing executive teams to sacrifice long-term strategy for quarterly earnings compliance.

So, we are about 120 billion dollars in capital. Like, why bother at all applying your craft and your career in this space versus, like, a more traditional asset manager? Like, why, why is the juice worth the squeeze? I really do believe what I described before, which is a really unique place to sit in the financial system, in the fabric of the economy. You sit and you support the economy in two different ways. But specifically, why not just invest the whole thing in a bond portfolio and, and, and go away? That would prevent you from, ultimately, being a balance sheet that can adapt new technologies, because that's obviously a, a, a constant in our world. It is a balance sheet that is able to adapt to the evolution of the economy. Risks are evolving all the time. The risks that insurance companies took on 25 years ago are very different than they are today, and for sure they will be different tomorrow. Give you an example: data centers. This is a totally different scale asset and values than of an asset that have existed before. Insurance balance sheets aren't large enough to just absorb that. That's why you have an extension into all forms of third-party capital coming in. But that's an example of where, if you build a fortress balance sheet, you're able to do things that others will not. How do you build that fortress balance sheet? Through two main engines of profitability. One is the underwriting part, but that's a thin-margin business. And then through the asset side, where if you simply take the approach of, "Let me buy a 4 percent investment-grade bond or 5 percent investment-grade bond," versus trying to achieve a 7, 8, 9, 10 percent return on the totality of your portfolio, it's all the difference in the world. And so, it is a competitive business where the amount of capital you have will dictate the opportunity set that is available to you both on the liability side and the asset side. But, but, but in a mutual structure, I'm especially interested—like, in a mutual structure where there aren't shareholders... I would totally understand it if there was, like, "I was an equity shareholder, you can invest this at a high rate of return, like, that's good for me." Maybe, maybe close the loop for why that's good for the holistic thing. I would actually reverse that. I think as a public insurer, you're not likely to be able to pursue what we're doing. Because if you're a shareholder of a public insurer, you can bifurcate these two things and say, "You have historically not been a sophisticated investor; you've been much more conservative." "And so, what I ask of you, management, is to deliver me a very consistent margin on the underwriting, and I'd like to get as much capital back from you in the form of dividends or buybacks. I don't need you to recreate an investment firm on the asset side of your balance sheet because, if I wanted you to do that, well, instead I could just take that and invest." I could do it. I could do it. It's a classic example of conglomerates—you know, the shareholders don't generally welcome that approach. And it's especially difficult if you're starting from scratch. Like, why should you have the right to build a world-class investment organization if you're not really starting there? So, I think the public sphere, therefore, operates differently and is held to a very, very tight standard on the underwriting side. Now, the flip side of that, if you're a mutual, you don't have the forcing function of shareholders to ensure that you are operating at your best. But that's an optional feature of mutuality, that's not a requirement. The only requirement is that you can't raise equity. And so, I think we've made the choice that we want to be an exceptional operator, and that includes exceptional underwriting results and an exceptional investment organization. How it benefits our policyholders? Well, first and foremost, again, we are going to be there through thick and thin when, inevitably, trouble strikes. The other component I mentioned is that our insurance businesses are incredibly diverse. So if you think about other large insurers, take a Progressive—incredibly successful company, lots to admire. But they are very focused on a particular vertical: motor in the US. They're incredible at it. Those risks require a certain types of balance sheet; they're not particularly long-tailed. If you think about our mix, we have risks that can come back from 20, 30 years ago and be very fat-tailed. So it is really, really important to differentiate the balance sheet that a company like Progressive needs versus a company like Liberty Mutual. Our tails are fatter, and our balance sheet requirements are very different. That goes back to this what we describe as a flywheel. If we do well for our policyholders in terms of underwriting—underwriting efficiently, creating various products that tailor and suit their risks—and then invest our capital well, we can perpetuate the strength in the service of our policyholders. So if I were to sum that up, the success on the investment side unlocks product or, or service quality for policyholders. Product breadth, future risks that currently may not be visible to you but are going to evolve—we can be your partner in, in solving them. And risks that you can underwrite that maybe others couldn't because of the nature of the balance sheet. Correct. Got it. And, and so, the, I would say at the, at the, at the most extreme, you could think of Berkshire as, as being that. I mean, people think about Berkshire's insurance operations—the most visible, obviously, is Geico, the original—but they are the insurer of last resort very, very often. The reason they can do that is because they've got this incredible balance sheet. Again, I think Berkshire is in a universe of its own, particularly in the way shareholders have regarded Berkshire, not asking for capital back. But that's an extreme example of what you can do when you have a balance sheet like that. You take certain things for granted that are just not. You assume they are like gravity because they just exist. But they're not. So, I was born in Moldova, a former Republic of the Soviet Union, currently an independent country right outside of Ukraine. This was something that people could only dream of, and we were very fortunate for a number of different reasons to take the path that we did, which was a direct path to the, to the United States. I think what is true in the US, always has been true, and is still true today despite all the many ways you could criticize America, is that the level of agency you have as a citizen of, of the US or as a resident of the US is unparalleled to anywhere else in the world. It is a vast country with a vast amount of regional differences and cultural differences along the way. We have done an incredible job of, ultimately, integrating people into our society. And if you have talents, if you have motivation, there's an infinite amount of ways you can both define what success is, define how you will contribute, and ultimately live a life where you have the option to thrive. Not everyone thrives, but you have the option to. And that option is not available to the vast majority of humanity, because you'll be burdened by your family's history, your ethnicity, your religion, your government's oppressive system, or an inability to move up the socioeconomic ladder because of the way the economy is set up. All those things, in versions of or combinations of, are present just about everywhere. But in the US, you have an opportunity. And I think—and you and I had a chat before—like, one of the ways I describe the fascinating thing about America is, believe it or not, through the lens of a croissant. I remember as a kid in the Soviet Union, at about six years old or so, my mom would send me to go get bread. And the way you get bread there, you go to a bread store, and there is really one, maybe two types of bread. And there'd be lines outside of it, and you'd get the bread. We never went hungry, so I don't want to create those impressions. But the view was very simple: bread is bread. So why would you need more bread? You get your loaf and you go have your calories. In the US, we take the exact opposite view. If you want to reinvent the croissant, which exists in a thousand different ways right around Union Square, you can do that. And if you can figure out a way to make it special to you and to your customer, there'll be a market for that. And what is the act of that? That is human creativity. That is humans iterating and perfecting and continuing to apply themselves and to express themselves on something that doesn't need, necessarily, a different way of consuming calories. And if you wanted to grow your business, you would have to go to the public markets. There was a clear element of prestige with going public. So that was a milestone in a company's history to go public. That was a thing. And the tradeoffs would be you give up a significant amount of control, the ability to make decisions for long horizons, and sit through difficult moments because public markets punish you, shareholders react, and boards react. Private markets have more or less addressed those challenges, right? You can now raise gigantic amounts of capital. So the capital need has been solved. The prestige and milestone thing, that's kind of gotten diluted. These companies have grown so large we all know what they are. And now the cost of being public is actually quite high, whether that is a literal cost of the amount of compliance required, or people being really careful about saying, "Do I want the kind of pressures that are naturally present in public markets in the way I'm going to run my company? And what is it that my company does? Can I operate on more of a quarter-to-quarter or maybe year-to-year basis, or is my business really going to suffer if I have to operate that way? I really need that three to five year window which public markets very rarely give." So I think that's what's driven the growth of private markets. And some of those things may—mean revert, so maybe regulatory burden reverts. That's at least fixable. But the main reason of capital being available to you as a private company, I think that stays. And so this balance will persist. And so I think if you are an equity investor, you should look at the equity risk first and then decide what is the best way to get it. So we have not stopped, by any means, investing in the private markets. There are other reasons why our balance sheet specifically is probably not best suited for public market exposure. That is specific to our balance sheet. It doesn't mean we don't participate, doesn't mean we don't take opportunities when they look particularly compelling. But we will continue to largely focus in the private space for our equity exposure. You and your senior team came from Goldman? Yeah. What cultural crossover happened there? What did you take with you? What did you leave behind? Think of a very, obviously Goldman, especially when you were there, very distinctive culture. Goldman's one of these places where when you're there, you're amongst such talented and—and driven people. It's a hard place to be and it's a thrilling place to be. But you particularly actually appreciate it once you're not in it, because of all the things that you didn't know you were learning that you were in fact learning. I think we brought a drive for excellence with us. I'd say that's the most overarching theme: I find it implausible to just sit still, to take something and say, "This is good enough. Why should we do this? Like, this involves risk." It's just this motor that I think people that come and have been reasonably successful in those organizations, it's inherent to them that they are always pushing forward, even though another person may look at that and say, "Why—why do you bother?" Why is that enjoyable? Why is excellence—like what is it about excellence that's fun or enjoyable or rewarding? I think about this a lot. I don't, first of all, I clearly don't know the answer to it. But I think as I've gotten older, I think about this in a very existential way. When you have a family, when you think about the incredibly important lens that provides, particularly through the lens of your kids, you can split your identity in so many different ways. And if you overswing to one or the other, something suffers. And everybody's got their own equilibrium. But I've continued to find that I get an incredible amount of personal satisfaction from building and being part of an organization that is making progress, that is making things better—by things I mean, that could be—I can mean product, I can mean customer experiences, I can mean the careers of the people that are in my vicinity and surroundings. My god, it's one of the most satisfying things in the world to have you help other people progress through their careers. You don't think about that when you're younger, but it is truly satisfying. And so I've gotten more comfortable through time with spending more of myself at work than I have before. Before I would be very wary of that because I didn't want to have somehow my kids see less of me because I am a workaholic. But my whole frame around that has evolved. I don't think I'm a workaholic, but I am obsessed with making things better and being part of this organization and team, because it's not about you, it's about the organization as a whole and how it can continue to get better. Gives me real, real satisfaction. I have three boys, so inevitably I want to set an example for them best I can, through my experience, of what it means to be a productive human being, what it means to be a good father—unbelievably important—what it means to show them love and supportive care. Try to do that best I can every single day. I think they would find that they get a healthy balance. And that balance moves through time. I—my 17-year-old, my firstborn, literally needs a lot less of me today than he did 10 years ago. I think that's less of something he thinks about because it's so natural. It's something that I think about and I grapple with, like, is it okay that we interacted for 10, 15 minutes? It was maybe a high-quality interaction, but it's all it was, 10 to 15 minutes. I go through thinking about that sometimes, but I also think about this as a forever thing. Like, you think about your parents a lot less than they think about you. That will forever be true. It's true of your kids. It's true of the way you probably interact with your parents. But your parents probably think about you much more than you think about them. It's just reality. I'd love to close asking about two concepts that I think are so powerful that you've alluded to a bunch. One is just permanent capital, like what it's actually like—everyone talks about this as like a nice thing to have, but what it's actually like managing it? Like, you're not permanent, nobody's permanent. Ideally, if you do a great job, this thing will go far beyond you. So careers are not permanent, they're transitory. And then the second thing is this notion kind of related to the question on where you came from, where you grew up, of—I think you call it like pragmatic optimism. These two concepts are interesting to me in—in combination, and I thought it would be a fun place to have you riff as we wind up. The downside of permanence is—as—is that people change. So even though the capital may be permanent, people come through the organization ultimately, and you're always dealing with some version of, "But another team made that investment," or there was a decision made in the past. And you can dwell a lot on that. Usually the ones you're discussing are the difficult ones and not the good ones that people are happy to absorb into their window of time. Let me—let me actually come back to that. What I have found about managing your own balance sheet as opposed to managing third-party capital, which is inherently not permanent, it completely changes what you think about. Because when you're in a fund cycle, when you have to deliver returns to a fund and to your investors and then think about the next one, you are consumed by the business that you're running. Investment outcomes are kind of a product that you sell, but you are ultimately running a business. And so your business strategy is going to always dwarf your investment process, no matter how many times you can talk about your long-term horizon or whatever else. Ultimately the time comes where new funds need to be raised. If you're a public alternative asset manager, you care about how the market will give you the highest multiple, which will then drive a certain way you will structure your business. And the investing, the craft of investing, is inherently diluted one way or the other. It just is. It doesn't mean that there aren't excellent, excellent investors. But they have to think about other things. In some cases that really does take the business away from the bespoke nature. In some cases people stay small—there are many examples of that—and ultimately try to just deliver truly outsized returns because of how they approach that problem. When you don't have to think about any of that, and all you're thinking about is, "How do I take my capital, deploy it into the world, get the right rate of return on it, and see the fruit of what that capital does?"—it's—it's just inherently different. You are able to sustain what I describe as much better investment hygiene. You don't have to react. You don't have to worry about doing an investor update where some—where some of your investors may have circumstances or priorities that are different from other investors in your fund and therefore creating tension, polluting your investment process with the nature of the business. We just don't have that. We are singularly focused on being in the service of our policyholders, in the service of our balance sheet, and doing the best we can to deploy capital for the right opportunity, for the right rate of return. The other part that you mentioned is very real. There's a downside to—to having that permanence because you can make the argument that it makes people a little bit more complacent about this notion of the long term. I mentioned that before and I always am careful with saying we can make long-term decisions that others can't. I think when people say that, for the most part what you end up with, and I do mean the most part, is some form of excuses as to why, sure, this is not great, but it will be if you wait long enough. Especially like you said before, the volatility, the—you know, the rising uncertainty. Yes. So—so I've always almost had like this strange fixed-income version of—of my thinking around long-term versus short-term. It's like the 10-year rate is just a series of shorter rates that build up to it. So, yes, you can talk about the long term. The long term is constructed of a bunch of short terms. And so you have to actually hold both truths. The ability to make long-term decisions and focus on the long term is really valuable, but if it becomes a crutch and an explanatory variable as to why you're either inconsistent or things are not going the way that you'd like them to go, then it's not very useful, and in fact it's an impediment. And I would just add one more thing because this is—this is really important. I think all businesses have a constraint of an annual calendar. That's just the way we've structured ourselves. Some have it very acutely, have quarterly constraints. But the annual one is important for everybody. Everybody has some notion of a financial plan or objectives they're trying to hit on an annual basis. But very few businesses actually have those horizons. And so this is another one where you have to hold both truths, particularly when you're responsible for the organization. Your business is not a one-year business. We know this. And anything can happen in a one-year window. And yet you know that that one-year result has importance to your stakeholders. The way I therefore try to navigate that is to, one, be cognizant of the—of the one-year, of the calendar year, but try to establish some three, five-year targets. And do put yourself on the hook for those in a much more meaningful way than the one-year. Or at least say, more people in this organization really are on the hook for the three to five year and be explicit about what that is than it is for the one-year. The other part is all businesses that have this dynamic, they require a great degree of transparency from all your stakeholders in order for you to ride those waves. Because if your business is opaque, not understood, and volatile, that's a recipe for ultimately not being supported through difficult times. And so I try to always remind myself and—and my leadership team that transparency is what allows you to have autonomy. No transparency, no autonomy. Critically important, difficult to deliver, and people don't always focus on it. I love doing this with you. I love your story. I love the way you built this thing. I think it's extremely distinctive. It certainly is one of those 10 referent, you know, branded capital LPs that I think everyone out there wants. It's so interesting to me. Maybe I should make this a series or something to—to really help the world understand how each of these huge pools of capital thinks and works. I think you know my traditional closing question for everyone: what is the kindest thing that anyone's ever done for you? This is the one question I knew was coming. I've thought about it. I had so many thoughts on who I should describe. I've been fortunate to have many. I'm going to come back and—maybe do something that I haven't seen others say on your podcast. I'm going to come back and say the kindest thing is this—group of people that have fought for constructed pathways for legal immigration to the United States. So my gratitude is to America, and my gratitude is to the people that, for reasons they didn't have to, allowed people like me to come to America to have a very, very, very different life than I would have otherwise had. To allow me to have an impact on people that are in my vicinity and surroundings—hopefully a positive one—and to find a way to learn from—from that gratitude and continue to have pragmatic optimism that America is essential to the world. It is still the shining city on a hill, and I want to do whatever I can to remind people of that, to contribute to it, and every single day be grateful for being an American citizen. Beautiful. First—first answer of its kind. Of course it's hard to do 500 of these in. Thanks for the great answer and for your time. Awesome. Thanks. Invest Like The Best.
