Algorithmic Storytelling

World Economic Forum(1.1M)·38:23·449 views·2026-06-23
Victoria FardMultidisciplinary Artist
TL;DRMultidisciplinary artist Victoria Fard uses custom 3D algorithms to craft monumental "digital sanctuaries" that merge technology with human memory, inviting public audiences to pause and experience shared moments of awe.
Rather than relying on automated generative AI,...

TL;DR

Multidisciplinary artist Victoria Fard uses custom 3D algorithms to craft monumental "digital sanctuaries" that merge technology with human memory, inviting public audiences to pause and experience shared moments of awe. Rather than relying on automated generative AI, Fard advocates for human-controlled technology that prioritizes emotional resonance, physical scale, and universal public accessibility.

The Philosophy of Digital Sanctuaries and Public Art

  • Defining Digital Sanctuaries: Fard designs immersive, digital environments inspired by nature, culture, and architecture to offer viewers places to pause, be present, and experience reality differently.
  • Transitioning Observers to Participants: The goal of spatial, digital storytelling is to shift the viewer from a passive observer to an active participant whose attention is completely absorbed by the atmosphere.
  • Democratizing Art via Public Spaces: Fard advocates that art should meet people in their daily lives rather than existing only in ticketed or planned-visit venues.
      • Times Square Installation (July 2023): Displayed on over 100 screens for three minutes nightly, Fard’s work brought a rare collective stillness to over 2 million in-person and online viewers in one of the world's most sensory-heavy locations.
      • The Outernet (London): Fard exhibited her work on free, publicly accessible, four-story-high screens operating on a massive 16,000-resolution canvas to allow pedestrians to walk in and experience art unexpectedly.

The Creative Process: Human Intention vs. Algorithmic Automation

  • The Value of Sweat Equity: Each digital world takes between six months to a year to complete, yielding project files that can exceed 700 gigabytes of data.
  • Custom Algorithms vs. Generative AI: Fard deliberately chooses not to use generative AI in her creative work, identifying a key limitation in its predictability and micro-level control.
      • The Controllability Issue: By writing her own scripts and parameters, Fard maintains absolute control over the geometry generated by the system.
      • The Human Element: Fard emphasizes that systems cannot understand *why* a choice matters because they lack lived experiences, cultural backgrounds, and memories.
      • Human-Driven Decisions: While technology acts as her "paintbrush," the artist makes thousands of active decisions on what to keep, remove, and allow to exist.

Scaling, Interactivity, and Future Collaborations

  • The Impact of Physical Scale: Massive displays are critical to Fard's work because they physically engage the viewer. A piece viewed on an iPhone lacks the bodily impact of a 16K, four-story installation.
  • Integrating Augmented Reality (AR): To push past purely visual experiences, Fard collaborated with the tech company Augmento and the World Economic Forum to integrate her piece, *Momentum*, into an AR environment where users can physically walk through and explore the digital space.
  • Combining Storytelling with Music: During the session, Zhang Jinjun, a museum curator, proposed a future integration of Fard's digital environments with orchestral and historical music (such as Beethoven or ancient Chinese chimes) to deepen the cross-cultural and sensory impact of her sanctuaries.

Sustaining Creative Imagination and Preventing Burnout

  • Cultivating Daily Imagination: Fard draws her creative resilience from family values, the experience of being a new mother, and remaining open to learning from different languages, cultures, and travels.
  • Strategies for Managing Burnout: For creative professionals navigating high-pressure projects, Fard highlights key practices for longevity:
      • Listen to the Body: Recognize when it is time to rest rather than trying to force productivity.
      • Practice Self-Grace: Understand that artists cannot control every variable, and giving oneself the grace to recover actually builds the strength to complete projects excellently.

***

Notable Quotes

"Storytelling isn't only about what is created, it is about how it is experienced." — Victoria Fard
"The system does not recognize why one choice matters more than the other. It has never walked into a cathedral, it has never traveled, nor has it ever carried a memory of a place. That belongs to me." — Victoria Fard
"I don't think that storytelling is being reinvented. I think it is expanding. Technology allows stories to be shared more immersively... But the tools will change, especially with AI. The meaning comes from our human experience, our intention, our values, our memories, and the things we pay attention to." — Victoria Fard
Ensure your phones are on silent so we don't disturb the session once it begins. This afternoon's session is called Algorithmic Storytelling, and it will be given by multidisciplinary artist, Victoria Fard. Please give a very warm welcome to Victoria Fard. Thank you, Joseph. Good afternoon, everyone. Did you know that before I stood here today and said a single word, the story already began? You moved through it, you explored it, and you engaged with it. For me, that's what matters because storytelling isn't only about what is created, it is about how it is experienced. My name is Victoria Fard, and I create digital worlds for us to step into—not just to observe, but to experience together. Long before stories were written down, they were shared through gatherings just like this one, around circles, around tables. Stories, well, they lived in people's voices, in the atmosphere, in gestures, and in shared attention. People weren't just listening to the storyteller; they were listening together. Over centuries and across cultures, storytelling was used to exchange ideas and to share different ways of seeing the world. Now, today, the tools have changed, but that human impulse of sharing stories has not. My journey into immersive storytelling began through architecture. I was interested in a simple question: how does a person feel the moment they walk into a space? I remember as a child walking into a cathedral and being so captivated by the scale, the light, and the atmosphere. Can you think for a moment, has there ever been a place that has stayed with you? And what made it unforgettable? For me, I wasn't just walking into a cathedral. I remember feeling something inside, and that feeling always stayed with me. As I journeyed on through architecture, digital fabrication, and computational design, I became interested in ornamental geometry, different forms, and structures. But underneath all of this exploration was another question, which is: can a digital space create the same sense of awe? Over time, I realized I didn't just want to recreate cathedrals; I wanted to recreate what they do, which is to create spaces we can step inside and see differently. That question became the foundation of my work. Today, I create digital worlds that we can experience spatially. The worlds themselves are always different. Some are inspired by nature, some by people, culture, and place, and others emerge from imagination alone. But what remains consistent in all of them is the meaning behind them, which is to create spaces and worlds people can step inside and experience for themselves. What moves me the most is the moment a person stops feeling like an observer and suddenly feels like a participant. Their feet are on the ground, but their attention is taken elsewhere. Can you think of a time when maybe a place or a story completely absorbed you? If yes, then you know exactly what that feels like. The piece that you see here is currently being displayed at Outernet in London. The screens are over four stories high, and I was working with a 16,000-resolution canvas. The scale of this really allows people to experience the piece, not just visually, but physically as well. So, how do I build these worlds? Well, technology is the medium I work in, and my paintbrushes are 3D programs, 3D tools, and custom systems that generate a lot of possibilities. I create the rules, the different parameters, and different behaviors within the scripts, and these generate a lot of beautiful geometry, forms, and structures. But did you know that each of the worlds you see here takes me about six months to a year to complete? It's not because technology does the work for me, but because each world comprises thousands of decisions: what to keep, what to remove, and what deserves to exist. What fascinates me is the dialogue between the artist and the system, because the system does not recognize why one choice matters more than another. It has never walked into a cathedral, it has never traveled, nor has it ever carried a memory of a place. That belongs to me. The worlds that you see here are shaped by my humanity, by my intentions, and by my values. So, the tools help me build the worlds, but I decide what matters. The worlds I build are shaped by my lived experience, my memories, my culture, and travel. I grew up between landscapes, between languages, and between different perspectives. Over time, all of these shaped the way I see the world, and they've shaped my worlds and became the foundation of my work. I often describe the worlds I create as digital sanctuaries. They're not places to escape reality, but they're places to experience it in a different way. I'd like to take a moment for you to experience and be immersed in Milagro. It's a world inspired by nature and the coexistence between urban life and the wild. The worlds I create are invitations—invitations to pause, to be present, and to rediscover the familiar with fresh eyes. It is an invitation to step into a shared space and experience something personally meaningful. Because in a world that's constantly moving, constantly consuming, and constantly distracted, sometimes it's easy to forget to see what's right in front of us. In July of 2023, I had the opportunity to present an immersive work in Times Square. Every night for three minutes on over 100 screens, one of the most sensory-charged places in the world became unexpectedly calm. Over two million people experienced the work in person and online. What resonated with me the most wasn't just the scale of it all—because it truly was amazing—but it was how people responded to it. They suddenly stopped, they looked up, and they lingered, and together we shared a rare and quiet stillness. Has there ever been a time where something took your attention completely? And it's not because someone told you to stop, but it's because something captured—captured your heart and captured your attention. For me, this entire experience really reinforced something I care about deeply, which is that art should not only live in places we travel to. I think that art should meet us where we already are: in public spaces, in our daily lives, and in places where we don't expect to see wonder. I had the opportunity to continue this idea at the Outernet in London, where art could be freely accessible. People didn't have to purchase a ticket, nor did they have to plan a visit; they could just walk in and be immersed. For me, what I really love is when someone walks in, and then they suddenly stop and experience something they weren't expecting that day. In our world that's constantly competing for our attention, those moments really become increasingly rare. I don't think that storytelling is being reinvented; I think it is expanding. Technology allows stories to be shared more immersively, it's more accessible, and it reaches people more than ever before. But the goal remains remarkably familiar, which is to move people and allow people to see the world differently. The tools will change—especially with AI, it will surely evolve. But the meaning comes from our human experience: from our intentions, our values, our memories, and the things we pay attention to. The worlds I build are digital sanctuaries that transform stories into shared experiences that remain personally meaningful. Storytelling is not only about what we see, it is about how we experience it. Because sometimes, how we experience changes how we see. Thank you so much for stepping inside. The worlds I create are just the beginning, but their meaning lives on with what you carry back with you. Thank you so much. I'd like to invite any questions you may have or any thoughts. Maybe there's been a story that has captivated you. Oh, yes, sure. Thank you so much. That's quite amazing, especially the scale of the Times Square installation and harking back to cathedrals. I did have a question about your process, though. You mentioned this takes 6 to 12 months. How much of that sweat equity, if you like, is part of the product and the value of the product, do you think, to you? I ask that because, of course, somebody might look at this and think that with an AI model, I could recreate this in 10 minutes or an hour. Do you think that the human effort leads to something different in the storytelling? Yes, absolutely. Thank you for a great question. I really love what I do, and I'm really privileged to be able to do it. For me, I learn in the process of doing all of this. But sometimes, the worlds I make, as I mentioned, come from my imagination, and creating that—like creating a building—takes time. So, as I build it, suddenly I discover little pockets where I'd love to develop and build more. For me, each world has a part of me in it. So yes, the process and the effort really, I think, adds to it. My worlds are, some of them, over 700 gigabytes of data, but it's because I love detail and I love being able to portray it and share it through the skills that I have developed of creating worlds digitally with modeling. Thank you for the question. Yes? Hi, lovely artwork. My question is, have you explored making a game where you can have someone actually walk through and just keep exploring the world? Something like a beautiful GTA sort of thing, where basically you have a person just walking through and exploring the world? Because I think a lot of us, especially in the younger generation, would actually love to play such games where there's no mission—you just keep exploring and you keep seeing beauty as you walk through it. I think that's a wonderful question, and that's a great next project. But also, for the first time actually, one of my worlds, Momentum, has been integrated into AR. We worked with an amazing technology company, Augmento, and of course the Forum, to give that experience. So now, you don't just see it visually, but you can actually move through it. And so, that's a great question, and I think that's definitely something I would love to pursue so that people can also be immersed in it, as if the thing is right next to them. Also, before I end and answer the next question, I would love to personally invite you downstairs after this. We have our exhibition there. I'd love to tell you the story behind the work of Momentum, how we integrated it into AR, and tell it to you in person. It's just downstairs—go down the escalators, and I will be there to await you. Yes, sir? Good afternoon. It's a very beautiful video and also a very good experience. I'm the curator, Zhang Jinjun. We have a lot of music from across the world. Just now, we've noticed the beautiful scenery you've created. Music can inspire people; it can inspire people's hearts and minds. Art can elevate our taste and enrich our lives. For such a beautiful scenery you've created, if your scenery or storytelling can be combined with music, I think that would be much better. We have 2,070 pieces of music in our museum. For the next step, if you want to combine your storytelling and beautiful scenery with music—either the years-old chime of the Chinese nation or the piano played by the master Beethoven—it would be a very wonderful thing. Our museum is about 1,500 meters from here, and it is a very important reception venue for Davos. We welcome you to visit our museum to help inspire your next project. A museum is a bridge connecting with the world, and music is the shared language across different countries. As an artist, we hope that in the future you can use music and AI technology to create more wonderful projects so that we can share the music and enrich the whole world. Thank you very much. Yes, thank you for your kind words and also the invitation. Zhang Jinjun has invited me to experience the museum that he's part of, and I would love to. Thank you for your invitation. He was talking about how integrating orchestra, music, and piano into the worlds will make them more immersive. Thank you for your question and invitation. Thank you for taking us through such an artistic, subtle, poetic tour. I wonder, given the technology—because you work on VR technology, but given the generative AI capability is just astonishing now—what is your next dream? Where are you going? What kind of possibilities do you think may open up to artists with these AI models? Personally, I don't use AI in the work that I do. I think AI is amazing. It's wonderful in the sense that it gives people the opportunity to be creative; everyone has their tools. But for me, that's why I create my own algorithms. With the algorithms that I make, I know what they will generate because I make the rules. Think about it like this: when I create the rules and the parameters, whether I run the system today, next week, or a year from now, I know exactly how it will be. With AI, because I'm not privy to all the back-end work, what it generates will be different each time. In the worlds I create, they are really inspired by my experiences, my culture, and nature. Even meeting you today, I'm sure it will inspire me to create something new. What I see in my mind is very clear at times, and I use the tools that I know to help me visualize it and make it a reality. Sometimes I wouldn't want to just type something and have it come up with an amazing world or image that is not what I had in mind. So, I try to materialize and realize the worlds and things I'm inspired by through tools like algorithms and 3D modeling. Thank you. That's kind of a problem I think many artists face when they try to use AI—it is, as you summarized last, the controllability. It's hard at a micro-level to really map what you want, but I think the technology is getting there. Thank you. Thank you. Yes, I think with the algorithms and scripts we create, it's in your control. I have full control of what I create. Yeah. I have a question. Your work is visually super impressive, and you are representing a kind of virtual world. What struck me as almost an irony—but probably it's intentional—is the fact that it becomes way more powerful, despite being virtual, because it is real on a big scale. The impression of this screen, if I looked at it on my iPhone, would look completely different than this. If you look at Times Square, this immersive, cathedral-like scale... is that something that is part of your creative process? How do you play with it? Working in virtual spaces but building them real—but not really for real—how is that part of your creative process? Thank you. Actually, what's fascinating about that is that my worlds come alive the moment you see them. I work late at night, overnight, and don't sleep because I really love what I do. But for me, even when working at home on my desktop, it really only gets its life when I know people get to experience it, and scale is a big part of that. When it is experienced on such a big scale, then I know what I've done. It's like people can actually be in it, and they can only be in it when it's experienced at its maximum capabilities. That's why, even at the Outernet, the screens are in such a high resolution—at times it's like 4K. That one was not 8,000; it was 16,000, so I knew every detail had to be seen. So yes, I think when I build the worlds, they come alive when they are on big screens and they're immersive, because I think that's also the purpose. I love creating worlds we step into, because that's how it becomes part of us, part of the surprise we experience through entering the artworks. First of all, I'd like to add what we're all probably thinking: you are a very gentle soul, and your presence is very soothing, which shows up in the art as well. I'm thinking a bit more about the muscle or the habit of imagination and creativity as one of the most important skills to cultivate for resilience in the future, to create the kind of world that we actually want to live in. So my question is: what habits in your daily life do you have to cultivate and expand your imagination? Thank you for your question and for your kind words. For me, I get a lot of my inspiration from my family. I think I'm very family-oriented, and I value just being together—togetherness. I'm also a new mother, and that inspires me every day. Even if it's challenging at times, they inspire me each day. As I mentioned, travel, seeing new things, and meeting new people also inspire me. I love people and learning about cultures. Growing up, because I also come from many different worlds, I think I see the world in that way. I think just remaining very open—your heart is open, your mind is open to learn. Having that heart helps me keep my imagination flowing. It's about being open to what is in front of us with a positive mindset, knowing that we're here to learn, to absorb, and to be better. Hello, good afternoon. First of all, I want to thank you for your efforts in making art more accessible to the public. We understand that not everyone has access, and what you're doing is really making an impact in that way. I just wanted to pick your brain: as a creative and an artist, have you experienced burnout, and how do you deal with it? Do you have any tips for artists who might deal with the same thing? Yes, I have. I guess it's just taking it a step at a time. You know, we cannot always control everything, but we just have to understand what we can do. When it's time to rest, I think you just have to listen to your body and give yourself the grace to be able to do it. There is strength in being able to continue, and it also takes strength to know when you need some time to recover, just so you don't fully burn out. Sometimes there's not even a balance, but you just have to listen to your body and listen to your surroundings. Don't put so much pressure on yourself, because really, at times, you cannot control everything. All we can do is our best, and we set our own limits with things. Just give yourself the grace to know that it's okay. In the end, I know it will be finished, and you'll be able to do it excellently. Just have peace that it will be okay. Thank you. Thank you so much. Please come join us downstairs if you'd like to experience our exhibit of Momentum. Thank you for your time. Thank you.

Renminbi and the World

World Economic Forum(1.1M)·47:36·373 views·2026-06-23
TL;DRThe internationalization of the Renminbi (RMB) has shifted from a prestige project to a critical strategic resilience effort, achieving substantial progress in cross-border trade settlements but remaining constrained as a global store of value by China's capital controls.

TL;DR

The internationalization of the Renminbi (RMB) has shifted from a prestige project to a critical strategic resilience effort, achieving substantial progress in cross-border trade settlements but remaining constrained as a global store of value by China's capital controls.

***

The Strategic Evolution of RMB Internationalization

  • Shift from Prestige to Resilience: Zoe Liu highlights that RMB internationalization has transitioned from a status symbol to a vital strategic project designed to build economic resilience and insulate China from geopolitical risks.
  • A Twenty-Year Cumulative Effort: Zhu Ning stresses that recent milestones are the result of a two-decade-long process, supported by a decade of building offshore clearing centers and standardizing financial products, rather than a short-term policy push.
  • The Global South’s Focus on Diversification: For middle powers like South Africa, transacting in RMB is driven by a desire to avoid systemic vulnerabilities rather than picking sides in a bipolar currency regime.
      • Bilateral Swaps: The South African Reserve Bank and the People's Bank of China (PBOC) have maintained a bilateral currency swap agreement since 2015 to buffer short-term balance-of-payment pressures.
      • Direct Clearing Infrastructure: In November 2023, South Africa’s Standard Bank became the first African bank authorized to clear and settle transactions directly in RMB through the Cross-Border Interbank Payment System (CIPS).

Measuring RMB Performance: The Three Functions of Money

  • Means of Exchange (High Progress): The RMB has made significant inroads as a payment currency, particularly in bilateral trade with major partners.
      • Sino-Russian Trade: Approximately 99% of bilateral trade between China and Russia is now settled in local currencies, primarily the RMB.
      • Cross-Border Settlement: Between 60% and 80% of China’s own cross-border trade is now settled in RMB.
      • The "Invisible" SWIFT Shift: Diana Choyleva notes that while SWIFT data suggests RMB usage has slightly dipped in global payments, this is misleading. An increasing volume of RMB trade has moved entirely off traditional Western "dollar rails" to CIPS and digital currency platforms like mBridge, escaping SWIFT visibility.
  • Unit of Account (Emerging Potential): China is actively promoting RMB-denominated commodity contracts—including copper, Shanghai oil futures, Guangzhou lithium, and Ganzhou rare earths—to establish the currency as a benchmark in non-oil energy markets.
  • Store of Value (Limited Progress): Despite trade gains, the RMB accounts for less than 2% of official global foreign exchange reserves (compared to the U.S. dollar's 58%), primarily due to China's strict capital controls.

Currency Stability, Valuation, and Exchange Rate Regimes

  • Managed Flexibility vs. Market Forces: The PBOC is gradually engineering a "two-way fluctuation" exchange rate mechanism. Zhu Ning explains that "stability" in this context refers to a predictable, market-reflective mechanism rather than a fixed exchange rate.
  • Predictability and Geopolitical Backing: Zoe Liu argues that the PBOC has successfully maintained currency predictability since the end of COVID-19. Despite domestic economic weaknesses, the RMB has avoided competitive devaluation because of its role as a strategic resilience tool.
  • The Manufacturing-Currency Disconnect: A stark gap exists between China's industrial footprint and its currency status. China commands nearly 30% of global manufacturing power but holds under 2% of global currency reserves.

Addressing the Shortage of Safe Assets and Investment Channels

  • Expanding Bond Markets: While China lacks a risk-free benchmark equivalent to U.S. Treasuries, it has rapidly expanded its municipal and corporate bond markets over the last 20 years to offer viable fixed-income alternatives.
  • The Green Transition Advantage: Zoe Liu suggests that as the global energy mix diversifies away from oil, China’s primary financial benchmark advantage will lie in green bonds and transition finance, heavily backed by its massive domestic industrial capacity.
  • Bypassing Controls via "Connect" Schemes: To attract foreign capital without fully opening its capital account, China is leveraging regulated investment pathways like the Bond Connect and Stock Connect programs, linking mainland exchanges with Hong Kong and London.

The Evolving Roles of Hong Kong and Shanghai as Financial Hubs

  • Complementary Financial Roles: Rather than competing destructively, Shanghai and Hong Kong have established distinct operational zones:
      • Shanghai: Focuses on onshore wealth management, supporting the domestic economy, and managing onshore exchange rates.
      • Hong Kong: Functions as the world’s largest international wealth management center, facilitating two-way global investment flows.
  • Internationalizing Capital Controls: Diana Choyleva argues that Beijing's crackdowns on illicit cross-border retail brokerages actually strengthen Hong Kong's position. The crackdowns signal that while illegal capital flight is blocked, legitimate capital will increasingly flow through Hong Kong via regulated connect schemes, essentially allowing China to internationalize its capital controls on its own terms.

***

Notable Quotes

"The biggest change for RMB internationalization is that it has transformed from more of a prestige project to a very important strategic and resilience project." — Zoe Liu, Senior Fellow, Council on Foreign Relations
"The critical thing is not to create single points of dependency but rather to focus on diversification. And for us, the future obviously is diversified and not necessarily bipolar between two hegemons and currencies." — Ashor Nick Sarupen, Deputy Minister of Finance, South Africa
"We are not in a position where there are significant bilateral exchange rate moves that have no connection to what the dollar is doing. We're still very much in that dollar-tethered global system, and one that will change at the margin depending on which country ends up in which sphere of geopolitical influence." — Diana Choyleva, Founder and Chief Economist, Enodo Economics
Hello everyone, and welcome to this session on the Renminbi in the world. I'm Robin Harding, Asia Editor of the Financial Times, and it's my great pleasure to introduce an extremely distinguished panel: Zhu Ning, Deputy Dean and professor of finance at the Shanghai Advanced Institute of Finance; Ashor Nick Sarupen, Deputy Minister of Finance of South Africa; Diana Choyleva, founder and chief economist of Enodo Economics; and Zoe Liu, senior fellow at the Council of Foreign Relations. So, thank you to our panelists for joining us, and thank you to all of you for being here in the room. This topic of RMB internationalization—I think probably every holding of this conference has had a session about it, but over the last year I think there has been real movement. We have seen greater use of the Renminbi in trade settlements in particular. There's been talk of petroyuan and all sorts of things brought about by the war in Iran, so I think it's a great time to revisit the topic. And my first question to the panel is to ask them: how much real change do you think there has been over the last year? Do you think we are now actually seeing a real move towards RMB internationalization? And Zhu Ning, let me start with you at the end of the panel, and we'll come this way. Okay, it's a pleasure to be here. Well, I think before I answer the question, I would like to say RMB internationalization is a very long-term process which started some 20 years ago. So, I think we are talking about the accumulation of a very long-term project, and we are probably seeing some of the fruit-bearing process in the past 12 months or so. In my observation, yes, China has been continuously pushing for the wider adoption of the RMB overseas, and in the past few months, there have probably been even more visible outcomes coming out of that. I think there are probably two reasons, maybe three reasons, for that. One is, as I mentioned, China has been building offshore RMB internationalization centers for more than a decade by now, so there are a lot of institutions, opportunities, and products being produced which will facilitate the adoption of the RMB overseas. The second is, of course, the strength and the resilience of the Chinese domestic economy, which attracts RMB's internationalization and its attractiveness in long-term holding of the RMB, either as a money swap option or as a way of future investment. And last but certainly not least, China and Hong Kong have been fairly stable both in terms of economic growth, geopolitical tension, and also in terms of their own financial markets, which attracts more money to hedge some of the increasing risks going on in other parts of the world. I just came over from the Lujiazui Forum in Shanghai last week, which focuses more on the further, continuous high-quality reform and opening up of China's financial sector. In that forum, trying to create or facilitate increasing transactions of offshore RMB in the free trade zone in Shanghai was mentioned as a very specific area which could further propel the adoption of RMB both onshore and offshore for Chinese-related trade. So, in a nutshell, I think yes, we are seeing major happenings with RMB internationalization. I just don't want to give the impression this is a short-term push; this is rather the result of a longer-term project. Okay, well, we might come back to that offshore RMB point if we have time. But Ashor, let me come to you next. What are you seeing from South Africa? Are you seeing greater use of the RMB by your financial institutions, or by yourselves in managing your reserves? Yeah, so look, the process is an ongoing process of internationalization for us. The critical thing is not to create single points of dependency, but rather to focus on diversification. For us, the future obviously is diversified, and not necessarily bipolar between two hegemons and currencies. For us, it's about interoperability, transparency, and predictable rules on currency exchange, which is the basis on which we look at these things. What we have, of course, is that between the South African Reserve Bank and the People's Bank of China, since 2015, there's been a bilateral currency swap agreement. This is intended to support trade and investment, and it does provide a potential buffer against short-term balance of payments pressures between the two countries. Our two central banks have also established cooperation on RMB clearing and settlement within South Africa. This helps reduce the cost and complexity of settling bilateral trade through intermediary currencies, which is also quite important. And then, towards the end of last year, one of our major banks, Standard Bank, became the first African bank that was authorized to offer transactions through the Cross-Border Interbank Payment System (CIPS), so it allows participating African and Chinese institutions to clear and settle transactions directly in RMB. So, we are seeing all of this. But again, for us, it's not about picking winners and losers; it's about making sure that there are no single points of failure, and again, having predictable rules in the game. And how much use has there been of those facilities? So, for example, has the swap line been drawn? Is the CIPS usage starting to pick up? What are you actually seeing in terms of... Well, it's early days. The CIPS agreement was only signed in November last year, and because it is the very first African bank, it is beginning to take shape. But because our central banks have established cooperation, we are seeing good clearing between the two central banks, which does help with cross-border transactions quite substantially. So, we have seen that. We haven't drawn on the currency line, but the swap agreement is in place. Okay, thank you. Zoe, let me come to you. Do you think—well, maybe let me ask you about what Zhu Ning said. Do you think it's right to see a fairly high level of positivity about the progress over the last year or so, or would it be easy to get carried away and think there is more progress towards RMB internationalization than is actually going on? Yeah, Robin. I'd say directionally there has really been meaningful progress there. But I'd say perhaps the biggest change from my vantage point, if we compare 2009 versus now, is that RMB internationalization has transformed from more of a prestige project to a very important strategic and resilience project. I think that is a meaningful change; that's meaningful progress. But if it comes down to currency as a form of money—RMB as a form of money—and what kind of progress RMB internationalization has made over the decade-long period that Zhu Ning was talking about, I'd say you really need to measure it against the three functions of money. Number one is as a means of exchange. I'd say definitely an A-minus, if not an A, right? In terms of means of exchange, if we only look at China-Russia trade, 99% of China-Russia trade is conducted in local currency, primarily RMB. And if you take a look at China's own cross-border trade and settlement, again, it is more than 60%, and some statistics would even push to 80% in terms of using RMB for cross-border trade. It makes sense because China is the world's largest trading economy; it's the largest trading partner for over 120 countries in the world. So, it makes a lot of sense. But if you take a look at the other two functions of money, say in terms of store of value, I'd say that's really an area where RMB internationalization has not made significant progress. One important—though not the entire—reason is capital controls, right? Then, if you look at the third function of money, in terms of unit of account, I'd say China has made progress in promoting the use of RMB in the trading of a lot of commodities, from copper to even oil futures in Shanghai, the lithium exchange in Guangzhou, or the rare metals in Ganzhou. In a lot of these things, China has been making progress. And I'd say this third function of money is probably going to be an area where China has huge potential in terms of making meaningful progress in RMB internationalization. That has a lot of implications for the RMB in terms of being an important currency in a world where oil is no longer the dominant form of energy resources. Okay. I notice you gave a grade on the first function then didn't on the other two, but maybe I'll ask Diana for her grade for RMB internationalization and how the global financial markets see this. Do you see a change in global investor appetite for the RMB and RMB assets? Yes, there has been a change, but that's much more to do with the revival in the Chinese equity market than it has been with necessarily any kind of policy action with respect to the global use of the Chinese currency. But let me answer as well your original question as to how much progress there has been and bring this down to more recent developments. While it's true that this whole effort started in 2009, there was a distinct shifting of gears from 2018–2019 onwards. If we are trying to measure progress, walking everyone through the functions of money that Zoe described and using the Yuan as a payment currency, well, actually, over the last couple of years, if we try and judge that by looking at the SWIFT data measurement of the role of the Yuan in global payments, you would notice that the Yuan has come down in the rankings. So, on the face of it, it would appear that the progress it has made—this A-minus that Zoe gave—is kind of nudging downwards. But I would caution against this interpretation. With so much more of the Yuan trade, or the trade in Yuan, moving off the dollar rails—if we think of the dollar rails as the US correspondent banking system together with SWIFT for messaging—the CIPS that China created has its own messaging system that has been used more and more. But importantly, there is also the explosion of using digital currencies for cross-border payments. There, we have no visibility because these are either bilateral mechanisms or run through platforms like mBridge. And so, we no longer have a credible yardstick with which to measure the use of the Yuan for cross-border trade. That's very important to realize. I think before moving to discussions about the store of value, I want to make the point that while China may not move from 0 to 100 extremely swiftly, along this process of RMB internationalization there are markers where critical mass is achieved to then produce meaningful change in global financial markets. One of the most important considerations here is the cost of using dollars versus using Yuan for cross-border payments, and then, at the next stage, the cost of funding in either dollars or Yuan. While countries like South Africa and others might want to have no single point of failure, to an extent that would depend on whether they can use their own currency as well as whether the cost of doing transactions in dollars is less or more than in Yuan. Now, using digital currencies here makes it much easier because it's instantaneous and the costs have come down significantly. But trade finance—where the data in SWIFT is interesting because that's where China has gone up the rankings quite significantly—suggests that, at the margin, the cost of dollar funding outside of the US is set to increase. I would argue more generally that over the next decade, the cost of dollar funding outside and inside the US will increase substantially. You mentioned the Iran war, and I think that... oh yeah, I'll finish my point... well, you know, I can talk about this endlessly, so... Okay, well, we'll come back to you and you can talk about it endlessly in a moment. I wanted to move to a related subject, because the other change in the RMB regime this year has been appreciation—slow appreciation of the currency—and I'd be interested to ask the panel what they make of that and how they see it continuing. So maybe, Zhu Ning, I'll ask you again to start. What's the nature of that shift? Should we expect from now on that the RMB will slowly appreciate, and appreciate against what? The dollar? A basket? How should we read it? Yes, I think over the past decade or so, the PBOC has been trying to engineer a two-way fluctuation which is based on market forces eventually. I think we're approaching that ideal; the range of the fluctuation is getting wider and wider, and the direction is no longer one-sided. As you mentioned, it is going towards that direction, but then everything that my fellow panelists have been talking about is put to work to determine the market-based exchange rate. So in that regard, stability in my mind is the mechanism; it's not the precise exchange rate. Ashor, does an appreciating Renminbi make it more attractive and viable as a reserve holding for a country like South Africa, or for South African companies to use? Well, I mean, the RMB is about 2% of global reserves at the moment. I think it's not really linked to interest rates or what makes exchange rates that makes it attractive. Critically, I think exchange rates tell us more about market conditions than they do about whether or not a currency is internationalized and something to hold in reserve. It could be any currency market, cyclical interest rate growth, capital flows, or dollar weakness, which may well explain a lot of what's going on in the market at the moment. So, currency internationalization and holding it as a store of value really depends on certain reliability factors: depth and openness of financial markets, confidence in the domestic institutions that back the currency, convertibility, liquidity, trade usage (which is increasing, of course), settlement infrastructure, the availability of safe assets (which is also quite important), and of course central bank and, most importantly, private sector willingness to transact in a given currency. Those are the factors that would make it more attractive as a holding point and a store of value, rather than just rising values on the international markets. But it presumably makes some difference. I mean, for anyone looking at holding it, if it's steadily appreciating, then it's a more attractive thing to hold than if it has been, as in recent years, depreciating. Yes, well, of course, naturally. But again, treating exchange rate movements as a basis on its own is not what makes it more attractive; it really is its increasing use in international trade which makes it far more attractive in that sense. Okay. Zoe, let me come to you. How do you read the RMB's appreciation over the last year or so? Yeah, Robin. I'd say the PBOC has done a really good job along two dimensions. One is the predictability of the currency in terms of slow and steady appreciation. I've been having this fight with my hedge fund friends since the end of COVID. Everybody was like, "Oh, you know, the consumption is weak, so the currency is probably going to get weaker." And then when Trump was showing signs of winning the election, people were like, "Oh, you know, if you look at the first Trump administration, there was a huge RMB depreciation once the trade war started," so people were betting against the RMB. But I was like, "Yeah, no, this time is different, because specifically for resilience project reasons, the RMB is not going to depreciate." So, from that point of view, I'd say in terms of predictability, they have done a pretty good job. In terms of the longer-term trajectory, I think the bigger challenge is stability, especially considering the fact that there is a mix of currents—not just geopolitical tensions, but also market fragmentation. There are also manufacturing sectors domestically trying to upgrade, and Chinese companies are trying to shift manufacturing power overseas. I talk about manufacturing power because although the Chinese RMB, in terms of reserve currency, is less than 2% (1.98% in global reserves) and the US dollar is about 58%... If you compare this, people can very easily miss the point that China has almost a third of global manufacturing power. In a way, if China were like the United States—the US basically leverages its share of global GDP, which is about one-fourth, changing that into over half of global reserves. But China has been lagging behind. From that point of view, you have over 30% of global manufacturing power, but your reserve currency status in terms of global official foreign exchange reserves is less than 2%. There is a huge gap there. So this is why I think geopolitics, shifting manufacturing structures, and, more importantly, the rise of other forms of currencies, including stablecoins, could bring a lot of challenges there. I must say, though, I still don't quite know how to read the RMB's current regime. You've talked about stability, predictability, and market conditions driving exchange rates, but if you have too much stability and predictability in a currency's appreciation, we know that leads to one-way bets and doesn't necessarily lead to stability. Similarly, I don't know how it can be market-determined if it goes up in a very straight line, which it does if you look at it on a chart. So, I still put this to you as a panel. Maybe, Diana, you can come in and say: how should we understand what the regime of the RMB is now? Is it just going to keep appreciating in this steady line unless something happens, or what? Well, you really want to dig into this question and get our read on it, and I... I want to know what the currency is going to do. Yeah, I know, I see where you're coming from. But look, we have been in a regime of a managed currency. Originally it was against the dollar; now it's against a basket of currencies. So, of course, what the dollar does with respect to other currencies then has an impact on how the dollar-Yuan rate moves, and the Yuan vis-a-vis the basket of currencies. But what I would say is that there has been more flexibility that the central bank has allowed in the setting of the exchange rate. It is more responsive to market conditions within this strategic objective, as Zoe was talking about, of resilience. China has tied economic development to national security. With respect to the currency, if you look through the long history since letting it go, China has always decided against competitive devaluation. It never wanted to, in every crisis—whether the Asian financial crisis or the global financial crisis—it has always been against using the currency, partly for this geopolitical reason. It wants to present the world with a stable alternative to the dollar to then eventually get to higher usage as a store of value. Of course, higher usage as a store of value also requires much broader, deeper financial markets in Yuan, which is an area where China has been making progress. Now, with respect to where the currency goes from here... So let me put the question this way. What does stability mean in terms of how the RMB behaves from here? If I may, I mean very briefly, I think over the past decade or so, the PBOC has been trying to engineer a two-way fluctuation which is based on market forces eventually. I think we're approaching that ideal; the range of the fluctuation is getting wider and wider, and the direction is no longer one-sided. As you mentioned, it is going towards that direction, but then everything that my fellow panelists have been talking about is put to work to determine the market-based exchange rate. So in that regard, stability in my mind is the mechanism; it's not the precise exchange rate. Okay. Okay, that makes sense. Anyone else want to add anything further on this? No? Okay, if not, are there more hands? Yes, there's a hand there. Wait for the mic, and just give your name and affiliation, please. Yeah, hi. My name is Bharat. Well, look, I understand that we focus a lot on the value of the currency, which we want to look at in the current circumstances and with respect to not just the dollar but other currencies. In economics, you have the direction of causation that can swap around. Sometimes it can be the trade surplus driving the appreciation in the currency, but in other situations, you could have the inflow of capital that is pushing up the currency, and that then causes your current account to correct. So, I think it's very important to think about that when we are looking at China's development here, because up until the last couple of years, it has been predominantly driven by trade flows and current account flows. But as China is really pushing for this RMB internationalization, it is opening up its financial sector, and it is reforming its financial system to make it more broad and deep, making it more attractive to foreigners to invest. In those circumstances, it's more likely we end up in the second type of appreciating currency, where the driver is capital flows and then trade reacts. On that front, the bilateral trade surpluses with other countries will play much less of a role, I think, than what they currently do. But at the end of the day, a lot comes off the Yuan-dollar rate, and then the dollar rate vis-a-vis other currencies determines the rate between the Yuan and those currencies. We are not in a position where there are significant bilateral exchange rate moves that have no connection to what the dollar is doing. We're still very much in that dollar-tethered global system—one that will change at the margin depending on which country ends up in which sphere of geopolitical influence. Okay. Yes, I think there will be some overlap and competition, for sure, but I want to emphasize that there is actually a greater differentiation between the two cities—or the two global financial centers, if I may. I think Shanghai is largely focusing on: one, onshore wealth; two, China's own domestic economy and listed companies; and three, China's own onshore exchange rate and the Shanghai Stock Exchange. Whereas, I think Hong Kong has replaced Switzerland to become the largest international wealth management center in the world, and it is helping facilitate investment both ways—from China to the rest of the world and vice versa.
