I'm happy to take this one because, you know, if we look at how this is naturally developing with trade being the focus, and more and more trade being done in RMB, the natural step would be for the treasury departments of all those companies using the currency for payment to want to hold it. They would want to hold it in something reliable and short-term. So, I would argue that China will ramp up the development of its fixed income market, in particular offshore, but as well as onshore. That's the natural progression in the direction of store of value, just because if you are transacting in RMB, you want to hold the RMB, because you know in three months' time you're going to need it again, so why incur the cost of exchange? I think that will become the next phase of expansion on that front. It's interesting to look at the AI race here as well, because China and the US are running very different AI races. America has gone down the hyperscaling route, and China has gone down the AI diffusion route, which matters for global investors, particularly in the Global South, because of course it matters which parts of the world you are coming from. If you are a US pension fund, you're probably not looking at investing in China in the same way as if you are an Indonesian sovereign wealth fund. And so, this dichotomy in the way the two countries are running this all-important AI race allows for very meaningful diversification, which I think will also be another driver. All things equal—without any other geopolitical risks and issues arising over the next couple of years—that will be a key driver, together with just the broader push. I want to actually make a point about Hong Kong and Shanghai. The role of Hong Kong is changing, and I would argue that the authorities made the decision a few years ago, post-national security law, that Hong Kong is really the solution in terms of a global financial center for China. This competition between Shanghai and Hong Kong was put to the side, and it was at the last Lujiazui Forum, or the one before that, where it was decided that the two cities are supposed to cooperate on this front: Shanghai very much for domestic use, Hong Kong very much for international use. The rest of the panel on this question of safe asset supply? Very quickly, I'd say the most underappreciated aspect of the US dollar or US Treasury is that it is the risk-free asset benchmark against every other single financial asset, and there is no way that any other kind of asset is going to compete against that. However, that was in an era when oil supplied 80% of global energy demand, and oil was traded globally. Now, as the global energy market diversifies and energy is traded not just globally but very much in a localized way, this is where China is going to have an advantage. I think this is why, going forward, China's unique advantage in terms of providing a financial benchmark and providing standards is going to be in green bonds and the green transition. It's not just in the financial market; there is also a tremendous amount of industrial capacity backing that. Yeah, I just want to say maybe it really depends on how you define safe assets. If it's strictly Treasuries, maybe there's a long way to go, but if it's a broader range of fixed income assets, I think China over the past 20 years has been developing its bond markets to a far greater scale than before. I actually co-authored one of the first papers on China's bond market development some 20 years ago, in 2005. So, I think China's municipal bonds and China's corporate bonds have all become attractive to certain types of foreign investors. There are two caveats, though, that I want to mention. One is, given what Diana mentioned about the different approach to AI, I want to mention the different approach to monetary policy. The US is probably in the process of increasing its interest rates, whereas China is on a trajectory to taper down its interest rates, so that may somehow affect safe assets' attractiveness to foreign investors, given the potential interest rate direction. The other is, I think several of my fellow panelists have mentioned capital account controls. If you can't make a good investment, how do you get your money back, or is there going to be a haircut? I think China has engineered or installed this Bond Connect program with a few financial centers, particularly in London. In a similar vein to the Stock Connect program—the Shanghai-Hong Kong and Shenzhen-Hong Kong programs—I think China is going to expand the connect programs through which there will be fewer constraints, and that will facilitate more foreign holdings and investment into Chinese fixed income assets. Okay. Yes, there's a hand up back there, please. Thank you very much. Sorry, I'm also from the South China Morning Post in Hong Kong. Professor Zhu, actually, I agree with you: the so-called competition between Hong Kong and Shanghai has been talked about for decades, but I do believe the two cities can complement each other. My question is, how do you see Hong Kong's status as an RMB offshore center? How much further can Hong Kong go in that role in the run-up to the RMB's internationalization? And also, another question is: recently, the CSRC cracked down on Tiger Securities, Futu, etc., for these cross-border illegal or illicit transactions. How do you see this latest round of crackdowns affecting or further strengthening Hong Kong's role as an RMB offshore center? Thank you. My question is also open to other panelists if you feel free to answer. Okay, I'll probably take a first stab very briefly in the interest of time. I think Hong Kong has been a leading international RMB center for offshore China, and I think that trend will continue for a very long period of time. That is my forecast. I don't think the recent crackdown on local brokerage firms' business with mainland residents has too much to do with the future of Hong Kong remaining or continuing as an offshore RMB international center. But I will leave... I would actually disagree with that and would argue that these moves make the importance of Hong Kong even bigger. What the authorities are doing here is saying "no" to illegal capital outflows. But legally, there is an increase in opening up the flow of capital through Hong Kong via the connect schemes. So, the role of Hong Kong is changing from being a gateway for foreign money to get into China, to being the place where China's domestic savings will be intermediated on China's own terms, on Chinese rails, with the rest of the world, because the connect schemes are an extension of China's capital controls. I would argue that if the common reading is, "Okay, this crackdown suggests China is tightening capital controls and there will be less capital outflow out of China," my reading is the opposite. On the contrary, they are making sure that the illegal stuff doesn't happen—and this has been going on for a few years, and these fines were the culmination of that process. It's really making the point clear, while simultaneously widening the connect schemes, allowing more capital to flow through Hong Kong, but within China's own system. It has kind of internationalized, if you like, its capital controls. I think that has to be the last word, because we are basically out of time. What I interpret our panelists to have said is that there is real forward momentum now in the RMB's internationalization, particularly for payments and as a unit of account. Pools of assets and store of value remain a long-term work in progress. It remains very complicated with all these different schemes, offshore and onshore, and we would have to ask you to untangle all the rest. But I'm sure you will agree with me that our panelists have shed a lot of light, and you will join me in giving them a round of applause. Thank you very much. Thank you.

What if Fixing the System Isn’t the Answer?

World Economic Forum(1.1M)·35:50·283 views·2026-06-23
Paulo SavagetAssociate Professor, University of Oxford Hilary CottamSocial Entrepreneur and Author
TL;DRComplex systems cannot be "fixed" like static machines because their challenges are deeply intertwined and constantly evolving; instead, leaders should utilize "workarounds"—creative, low-cost entry points that leverage existing networks, rules, and resources to trigger massive systemic shifts.

TL;DR

Complex systems cannot be "fixed" like static machines because their challenges are deeply intertwined and constantly evolving; instead, leaders should utilize "workarounds"—creative, low-cost entry points that leverage existing networks, rules, and resources to trigger massive systemic shifts.

***

The Fallacy of "Fixing" Complex Systems

  • The Machine Metaphor Failure: Standard problem-solving assumes systems are like broken machines where a single faulty component can be replaced. In complex systems, this approach fails because problems are deeply intertwined and lack a single root cause.
      • Intertwined Systems: Attempting to solve one issue, such as obesity in healthcare, quickly pulls in food systems, metabolic conditions, and even agricultural fertilizer cycles.
      • Evolving Challenges: Problems shift as we try to address them. For example, the nature of poverty today is fundamentally different from poverty 100 years ago, and urban poverty looks completely different from rural poverty.
  • The Power of Entry Points: If a root cause cannot be isolated, leaders must find accessible "entry points" (often visible symptoms of a problem) to build momentum, learn, and scale change.
  • Hacker-Inspired Methodology: Computer hackers excel at finding entry points in systems designed to lock them out. Non-technical leaders can adopt this "hacky" mindset to bypass barriers in education, healthcare, and environmental protection without trying to break down the walls of the system.

Three Categories of Workarounds

Paulo Savaget identified three distinct patterns of workarounds based on over 100 global case studies:

  • The Piggyback (Relationships): This approach involves crossing systems to take a "free ride" on an infrastructure that was never designed for your purpose.
      • The ColaLife Example: In Zambia, life-saving diarrhea medicine was unavailable in remote regions, yet Coca-Cola reached everywhere. The organization ColaLife designed medicine packaging to fit precisely in the unused space between bottles in decentralized Coca-Cola crates.
      • Systemic Impact: This tiny-budget workaround increased medicine uptake from less than 1% to approximately 50% in targeted regions, ultimately prompting policy changes at the World Health Organization (WHO).
  • The Loophole (Rules): This approach identifies and exploits legal or procedural gaps for a noble cause.
      • Bypassing Academic Paywalls: Academic research is heavily subsidized by public/philanthropic funds, yet journals charge high fees for access. Savaget and other academics use platforms like *ResearchGate* to upload preprints of their articles. This bypasses paywalls while keeping the work citable.
  • The Next Best (Resources): This involves creatively recombining or repurposing discarded or unconventional resources.
      • Acoustic Monitoring for Deforestation: Social entrepreneur Topher White addressed illegal logging in Borneo by repurposing discarded cell phones. Powered by solar energy and placed in tree canopies, the phones "listen" to the forest.
      • Real-Time AI Intervention: An AI program distinguishes the sound of chainsaws from ambient forest noise within a two-kilometer radius and automatically sends a geolocation text alert to local forest patrols.

Designing, Scaling, and Adapting Workarounds

  • The Extraordinary and the Mundane: Successful innovators either find mundane uses for extraordinary technology or extraordinary uses for mundane resources.
      • Mundane Use of High-Tech: Zipline utilized drone technology—initially hyped for commercial retail delivery—for the mundane but life-saving task of transporting blood for transfusions across Rwanda's challenging terrain.
      • Extraordinary Use of Low-Tech: Topher White's use of trash-bin smartphones to halt illegal logging showcases how simple, discarded tools can solve high-level environmental crises.
  • How Workarounds Scale: A workaround is not a static silver bullet; it is an entry point. For a workaround to scale, innovators must build momentum, engage with unexpected stakeholders, and adapt. ColaLife succeeded because they redesigned their packaging to serve as a liquid-measuring cup and collaborated with the Zambian Ministry of Health to alter regulations.

Q&A: Accountability, Hostile Systems, and Cultural Context

  • The Risk of Relieving Government Accountability: Somto (Global Shaper, Africa) raised concerns that institutionalizing workarounds might allow failing governments to avoid building permanent public infrastructure. Savaget noted that while this "dark side" exists, many workarounds naturally evolve into the new, highly adaptive default systems of the future.
  • Cultural Equivalents: Audience members noted that workarounds are highly prevalent in the Global South, operating under regional terms:
      • Jugaad (India): Resourceful, improvisational innovation born out of severe constraints.
      • Jeitinho and Gambiarra (Brazil): Ingenious ways of bypassing rigid systems using minimal resources.
  • Navigating Hostile Systems: Gustavo (Global Shaper, US) asked how to navigate systems that actively close loopholes (e.g., medical debt forgiveness). Savaget explained that closing one loophole usually opens others. He cited Uber's strategy of growing rapidly under unregulated "sharing economy" definitions to become "too big to ban" before regulators could react.

***

Notable Quotes

"We are told that we have to fix a system... But this is not how complex systems work... There is no root cause to these problems because not only the problems are intertwined, but the problems change as we try to address them." — Paulo Savaget
"Hackers taught me that we are wired as humans to look at a system and find an obstacle and think that we have to remove this obstacle. But we constantly find ourselves banging our heads against the wall... [Instead], you don't need to break the walls or the gates to find a way in. You can piggyback." — Paulo Savaget
"It's not trying to fix a system, it's trying to shift them. It's trying to stir them up. It's trying to adapt, learn, and evolve along with the changes that happen in the system." — Paulo Savaget
Hello everyone, my name is Paulo Savaget. I'm a professor at Oxford, and I would like to start with a provocation. We are very often told that we have to fix a system. It could be the healthcare system, a political system, education system—just name it. There are so many systems out there to be fixed. And I would like to challenge that assumption, and I'm going to walk you through it. It kind of sounds logical because if you have, say, a broken machine, you think that you just have to find the faulty component, and then you replace that component and the machine works back again. But this is not how complex systems work. And there are two primary reasons for that. The first one is because problems are completely intertwined, and the systems are also intertwined. So, let's say that you work in the healthcare system of a high-income country and you are very concerned about obesity, for example. Then suddenly you are thinking about diabetes, or you are thinking about metabolic conditions. And then you think about food systems. Or, you're going to start thinking about the nitrogen cycle involved in the use of fertilizers. Once you start pulling a thread, it leads you to another problem, and another problem, and other systems as well. So, the second complication is that there is no root cause to these problems, because not only are the problems intertwined, but the problems change as well as we try to address them. Poverty today is very different from how poverty looked like 100 years ago, for example. Someone who's poor today, for example in this country, considering the technologies and the services and the life expectancy that this person has, would probably have been very affluent in the Middle Ages. And even today, if you look across different countries, poverty looks completely different depending on where you are—rural poverty, urban poverty, poverty in Somaliland or Sweden. There's no reason to think that systems can be fixed because there's no single root cause to these problems. The problems evolve as we try to address them. And what I would like to propose here is a different way of looking at systems change—one that is much more adaptive. And I know that it can be a bit nerve-wracking to say that we cannot fix systems, but at the same time it can feel very liberating because it opens up so many opportunities for all of us to approach systems change and to try to make big change. Change sometimes in ways that can be very resourceful, very immediate, and here and now with what you have available. And there are many ways of doing that. What I'm going to try to show you here is that if you don't have a root cause of a problem that you can easily identify—and that's normally the case for most complex problems today—you can find an entry point. And an entry point might be a symptom, for example, of the problem that you're trying to address. It might be something visible, something that gets you started, something that you can get started with and then you learn, you build momentum, you scale, and sometimes you make change in completely unexpected ways. And there are many ways of trying to find these entry points. And what I'm going to try to show you today is what I learned from computer hackers. Computer hackers, as some of you may know, are amazing in finding entry points in systems that were created to keep them out. And for that reason, I started studying computer hackers, precisely because with a laptop and internet they can break sometimes into a very complex cybersecurity system and make change like this. So, can we learn from them to be hacky in how we approach other systems, say education systems, healthcare systems, sanitation systems? You may have already heard of the most notorious computer hack that is called the Trojan horse. Raise your hand if you have already heard of the Trojan horse. It's named after the Greek myth. Do you know the the Greek myth, everyone? So, what is the metaphor here that was used by computer hackers? You don't need to break the walls or the gates to find a way in. You can piggyback onto those who have access, or you can hide yourself in a wooden horse and be brought in. And that's the essence of a computer hack: it's that they work around. And hackers taught me that we are wired as humans to look at a system and find an obstacle and think that we have to remove this obstacle, but we constantly find ourselves banging our heads against the wall. And they showed that there are many ingenious workarounds that give you precisely what I told you before. They give you an entry point into making systems change. And after you find that entry point with a workaround, you can make very big change. I'm going to walk you through what I've learned studying hacks in many systems that are not computational. So, I'm not going to teach anyone here how to break into a cybersecurity system, but I'm going to show you what I've learned, for example, in addressing healthcare problems in low-income regions, or how, for example, to promote gender equality, or how to fight deforestation. These are some of the cases that I've studied. I studied over 100 cases, and I identified some patterns in how you can find these workarounds that are very accessible, resourceful, and that you can make change very quickly as well. So, let me show you the first one. The first one that I'm going to show you is the one that I call the piggyback. And the reason I call it a piggyback is because you can crisscross systems; you can piggyback onto something that was not designed for that purpose. I learned this from an organization working in Zambia. They realized that you don't find life-saving medicines in remote regions of Zambia and many other low-income countries around the world, but you find Coca-Cola everywhere. Has anyone here been in a remote region? Raise your hands. Did you find Coca-Cola? You don't find some basic products like life-saving medicines for diarrhea. Diarrhea is the second biggest killer of children under the age of five in many low-income countries around the world. The medicine is extremely cheap, to the point that people living in extreme poverty can afford it if these medicines were available to be purchased. They don't have to be sold in pharmacies because they're over the counter. There's no overdosage. Why are these medicines not found? Many organizations try to find the obstacles. It might be poor infrastructure; it might be poor funding for medicines. This organization worked around these obstacles. They identified that Coca-Cola reaches these places, so they started to literally piggyback on Coca-Cola's distribution chain to make these medicines available. Do you know those crates of Coca-Cola, the red crates of Coca-Cola with the bottles there? They started feeding medicines between the bottles in a Coca-Cola crate to take a free ride with the bottles. And heads up: Coca-Cola has no idea where the bottles end up going to because the entire system is very decentralized, okay? Coca-Cola doesn't take the bottles of Coca-Cola to remote regions. It's a bunch of different actors moving the products and selling the products. So, they started feeding these medicines between bottles, and this was an entry point—an entry point that ultimately led this organization to improve the uptake of this medicine from less than 1% to about 50% in many regions in Zambia, with a tiny, tiny budget and only a few years of intervention. This intervention later changed policies. It changed, for example, a policy at the World Health Organization because it was so successful. And it shows that sometimes you can crisscross systems. We think that we only have to address healthcare problems by thinking with the head, with—through the system of healthcare. You map out who are the actors in healthcare, you map out what are the obstacles in healthcare or the funding of healthcare, and we forget that there are other systems that you can piggyback on. You can piggyback on fast-moving consumer goods. There's nothing that prevents you from doing so. So, try to piggyback, try to crisscross systems, find these relationships that may not be obvious but they are possible, and you can get immediate and amazing results if you do so. The second workaround that I'm going to tell you about is the one that I call the loophole. And I know that "loophole" has somewhat of a controversial connotation because people immediately think of things like tax avoidance or casinos in indigenous lands. But there are loopholes that can be used for more noble causes as well. I'm an academic. I work at Oxford, and I, very luckily, have access to pretty much every academic article I want. But most people around the world don't because they are behind a paywall. The problem that some people like me see is that my research is primarily funded through philanthropic funds or public funds. I work at the university that pays my salary. When I submit a paper to a journal, the editor is probably unpaid—it's usually unpaid. The reviewers are unpaid. And yet, when the paper is published, and it's kind of like a PDF that goes online, it—it's charged 40, 50 dollars just to have access to that article. It's a problem that many people like me, academics, get frustrated about. I'm not going to fight against that industry, right? I—I don't have the power to fight against that industry, and neither do many of my academic colleagues. But what we can do is to find a loophole. A loophole that I use, for example, is called ResearchGate. With ResearchGate, I can upload the preprint of my article, which is exactly the same but it doesn't have the logo of the journal, and then anyone can access that paper. And in reality, you know that if you're going to cite me—and you know that academics love to be cited—you can cite the one that was actually published in the journal even though you read the preprint. And I'm sure that many of you are using loopholes, like a VPN here in this country while you are here, trying to use your Whatsapps. That's also a loophole; it's a workaround. The third workaround that I'm going to show you is the one that I call the next best. And just for a quick recap, if the first one is about relationships and crisscrossing systems, the second one is about rules, and the third one is about resources. So, it's about recombining resources or repurposing resources, sometimes in very unconventional ways. And let's face it, there's just so much stuff around us, so much that we can use, and a lot of potential that literally goes to waste. And I'm going to give you an example from a social entrepreneur called Topher White. Topher White was in Borneo, and he realized the challenges of illegal logging. For those who don't know about illegal logging, it primarily happens in tropical forests, and in regions where governments don't necessarily have a big budget to monitor illegal logging. It's very expensive to monitor as well. It may involve patrols, helicopters, drones, satellite data; and even with some of these tools like satellite data, it takes time to monitor and to respond, and you may not be able to identify illegal logging in the act. But what he observed was that there were many phones that were literally in the waste, in the bin. How many phones have you had in your life? Can someone help me out? From the old Nokias that you played that snake game to your iPhone, I don't even know if it's 10, 11—I can't keep track. Five? 10? Yeah. And you probably discarded the old phones even though they were still working, but perhaps they were not working for the newest apps that you wanted or—or the camera that got better. But the phones still worked. So, he started taking these phones and using the phones for what phones already do: phones listen. Phones capture sounds. And then he put these phones that were in the bin, he took them and put them in tree canopies. And the phones were charged with solar energy. There's no shortage of solar energy in a rainforest. The phones were constantly listening to the sounds around them, the sounds of the forest. And then he and his organization created an AI that is so simple that with ChatGPT today you would probably do in like a few hours, that distinguishes the sounds of chainsaws from the other sounds of the forest. So, let's say that this phone here captured the sound of a chainsaw. And this phone captures a radius of 2 kilometers. It sends a message—phones do that as well, they send messages—to the patrol with the precise geolocation of where it's happening. And the phones can be scattered in a rainforest in a way that maximizes coverage. So, depending on where the message comes from, they know which phone sent the message and they know the geolocation of where illegal logging is happening, and they have a higher likelihood of going there and capturing illegal logging in the act. There's a lot of stuff out there. We can think beyond their original design. Phones were not designed for illegal logging. They were designed for something else, but there's nothing that prevents you from repurposing or recombining resources creatively to address some complex problems. So, one of the suggestions I have if you are going to try to find some of these workarounds is something that I learned from anthropologists. They say that you've gotta find the extraordinary in the mundane and the mundane in the extraordinary. So, the mundane in the extraordinary could be, for example, when you take a new technology, say drones. When drones were first developed or in the early stages of drones, people were thinking about the future in which Amazon Prime would be delivered with drones. But the reality was that the technology was not ready for that. The very busy airspaces in New York, it—it wasn't going to happen. But there were more mundane ways of using the technology that were viable at the time. For example, an organization called Zipline in Rwanda that started using drones to send blood for blood transfusion from the capital, Kigali, to rural regions because it took so long if you took the actual roads with cars, trucks, that some people died in the meantime. With drones, blood could be delivered very quickly. Great workaround, and you see how you can use those kinds of more mundane uses for extraordinary technologies or resources. But the opposite is also true. What I've just shown you, the case of Topher White using phones that were in the bin, is a case of that. Find extraordinary use for something that is literally in the waste. Use resources creatively. And a quick recap here: so, the three workarounds that I've shown you, one is about relationships, the other one is about rules, the other one is about resources. And all of them help you address your complex problems with minimal fuss. And all of them will give you that entry point that I told you about. If there's no root cause to a complex challenge—and I hope I have convinced you that it's not possible to fix complex systems because they're constantly changing—you may try to find something that gives you that entry point that allows you to learn, to build momentum, to scale, and to make change, sometimes big shifts in entire systems. It's not trying to fix a system; it's trying to shift them. It's trying to stir them up. It's trying to adapt, learn, and evolve along with the changes that happen in the system. So, if you can't fix the system, I would encourage you to find workarounds, and I would love to hear some of your comments, some of your questions, and whether you have also used workarounds to tackle some of your most complex challenges. Thank you. "From India, we have a word for this." "Jugaad?" "Jugaad. Yes. Pretty much there, because—the word is jugaad, which basically means to find an alternate option without kind of ruffling or changing too many things there, because I think a lot of innovation comes, you know, when there are a lot of constraints. So, I think that's what, to some extent, you've alluded to, and I think I'm glad you know the word already." And I've learned a lot from India as well; I did fieldwork in India for about two months. And several of the cases that I've learned from came out of India, and I prompted people to ask about jugaad solutions. But jugaad were usually a bit more improvisational, right? Whereas these hacks are clearly about workarounds, different ways of working around complex obstacles. But yeah, I'm from Brazil, and in Brazil we have some words like jeitinho or gambiarra. In many countries, especially in the Global South, we have similar words that connote that idea of being ingenious with few resources or financial resources. Thank you. Yes. Thank you very much. "Hi everyone, my name is Somto. I'm a Global Shaper, and I also spearhead a program called Investing in Innovation. We've invested and supported over 60 healthtech companies across Africa. So, reflections and maybe a question for you. One is, I think Africa is a continent of workarounds. We have a lot of failing systems, and so we have had to innovate. You have shared some examples of companies that we know and already support, even a lot of the healthtech companies that are building out of system failures. And it's a great thing because health is important, and they are solving gaps." "However, my only worry and concern is how do we still hold the leaders who should be building the systems accountable, as opposed to the sort of comfort that the workarounds then introduce. Thank you." That's a great point. And first, I'm happy to hear from a Global Shaper; I was a Global Shaper about 15 years ago, when I was not as concerned about my receding hairline. That's exactly—that's a great point. Sometimes workarounds become so institutionalized that they become the default way of getting things done. We see that very often; that might be considered a dark side of workarounds. But I would also encourage you to think that some of the fixes may never happen, and some of them don't exist because the systems will change as well. So, workarounds may actually lead to more permanent solutions, some that might be more adaptive than the ones that you started with. The workaround may become the solution, or the—or the default solution to that problem. Thank you. "Congratulations... Congratulations for the successful presentation. I'm the representative from Dalian, my name is Ren Bojie. Very honored to receive so many guests. And we need to learn, and we need to raise questions. I'm studying the local literature, and I have a book entitled 'The Dialogue Between the Scholar and the Book of E'. And also, I have published a book about the source of virtue and how does it go beyond the rational thinking. It has a very wide impact in the scholar community." "I also had a lot of practice. The Dalian municipal government entrusted me to establish the merchandising center to sell the commodities. And in 2000, the British government provided assistance to the Liaoning government, and I provided consultancy to that project. After listening to your presentation, I believe you have touched upon the deeper meaning of global governance. What is the relationship between virtue and the system? Because we know that if we only stop at what we do, the solution will be very difficult to find." "The Book of E mentioned that we need to be versatile, we need to be flexible in identifying solutions to our challenge. The global economy meets a shared challenge. We focus on the material well-being. Now we come to the digital era. Should we change the underlying logic? How to change the underlying logic? Where should we start in the digital era?" Thank you for your—your comment and the suggestion of the book as well, I would love to read the book. I think you—you brought some interesting discussions on the underlying logic of political economies and so on. And I agree with you that it must be much more adaptive, right? The way that we've gotta think about big systems is not as if they were stable, or if they were somewhat immutable. What we—we have to think about is how these systems are constantly changing and how they change each other, right? If you change healthcare systems, they change education systems, and so on and so forth. So, yes, thank you for your comment. Thank you. "Javier?" "So, thank you so much for a very inspiring talk about the importance of working around and not giving up. But you said that you have studied over 100 cases. Could you tell us what works and what doesn't work in working around big problems?" Some workarounds will address relatively simple problems very quickly. It might be, for example, because you don't want to go to the gym, you find a workaround to get some steps in, right? And some workarounds might be much more ambitious, more complex, and so on. "Overall..." "Those are the ones that I'm interested in." "Exactly, yeah, yeah." So, the—the complex workarounds, they depend a lot on what happens next and how they are pursued, right? So, the—it's very difficult to predict whether they're going to scale or not because they're entry points. And it depends on how much you're going to learn from that entry point and how much you're going to build momentum upon that entry point. The first case I've shown you was ColaLife, this organization that piggybacked on Coca-Cola. They realized that Coca-Cola was available everywhere, but not medicines. That was an entry point. They started engaging with everyone that was involved in moving that bottle from Lusaka to many other regions around the country. But they also learned, for example, that they had to change the package, not only to fit between bottles but also because ORS and zinc—ORS especially—is—these salts have to dilute in water, and the package had to serve as a container. They also learned that they had to engage with the Ministry of Health to change some of the regulations. So, don't think of a workaround as a standalone solution—it's only that, a silver bullet, and it will get done. It depends a lot on how you're going to build momentum on the—the workaround that you started pursuing. Obrigado. Muito obrigado. "Thank you, my name is Mazu, also a Global Shaper. Mark Pincus: How to Spot a Fake CEO

Sourcery with Molly O'Shea(51.6K)·87:02·5.1K views·2026-06-23
Mark PincusFounder, Zynga Molly O'SheaHost, Sourcery
TL;DRZynga founder Mark Pincus outlines the frameworks required to build enduring consumer products, emphasizing the importance of separating winning instincts from mediocre execution, utilizing "founder mode" to maintain intellectual honesty, and…
training "technical...

TL;DR

Zynga founder Mark Pincus outlines the frameworks required to build enduring consumer products, emphasizing the importance of separating winning instincts from mediocre execution, utilizing "founder mode" to maintain intellectual honesty, and training "technical assistants" to scale a founder's decision-making style.

Separating Instincts from Ideas: The "Proven, Better, New" Framework

  • Instincts vs. Ideas: Founders must separate winning human instincts (identifying emotional pain points or broken experiences) from specific product ideas, which are often mediocre.
      • The B+ Trap: A B+ idea is the enemy of an A instinct. Sticking heroically to a B+ idea distracts founders from finding the true "A" execution.
      • Historical Misses: Pincus registered *smstaxi.com* in the early internet era, proving his instinct was right (foreshadowing Uber), but his execution idea was wrong. Similarly, his early social network, *tribe.net*, failed despite correct social media instincts.
  • The "Proven, Better, New" Framework:
      • Proven: Deconstruct what has already worked for the exact target audience in the market.
      • Better: Introduce highly visible, incremental improvements (e.g., free/half price, no download friction) that 10 out of 10 users immediately appreciate.
      • New: Introduce a high-risk, novel element to capture attention. Isolating the "new" prevents the product from failing for the wrong reasons.

Mastering User Retention in a Brutal App Economy

  • The Modern Landscape: The current consumer app market is incredibly difficult. Users download an average of zero new apps per month. Out of 40,000 games launched in the App Store last year, 0% sustained a top 25 position.
  • Day 365 Retention: Success requires building products that users can picture themselves using a year from now.
  • The Zynga Playbook: Rather than trying to force new user behaviors, builders should meet users where they already hang out (e.g., MySpace and Facebook in 2007; X and ChatGPT today).
      • Social Loops & Status: Zynga utilized social mechanics to drive daily retention. The ultimate high-value reward was social connection—Zynga even tracked real-world marriages that resulted from players meeting in *Words with Friends* or *FarmVille*.

"Founder Mode" vs. The Trap of the "Fake CEO"

  • The CEO’s Real Job: A CEO's core responsibility is to work directly with product teams to build experiences users love. Pincus tracked his own calendar with advisor Bing Gordon to ensure at least 50% of his time was dedicated to core product work.
  • Identifying "Fake CEOs": "Fake" CEOs prioritize the global speaking circuit, public relations, and lecturing about culture over product development.
      • On Culture: Pincus believes corporate culture is like *Fight Club*—if you are actively trying to manufacture or talk about it scientifically, you are likely failing to live it.
  • Founder Mode: True founder mode is having the courage and intellectual honesty to make highly unpopular decisions against investors and your own team.
      • *Examples:* Jeff Bezos launching Amazon Prime despite heavy pushback from his CFO, or Elon Musk completely restarting xAI after realizing early iterations were built incorrectly.

Technical Assistants: Scaling the Founder's "Vampire Blood"

  • The Technical Assistant (TA) Role: Originating with Andy Grove and Bill Gates, and popularized by Jeff Bezos, the TA role is a high-potential product track rather than an administrative Chief of Staff role.
  • Passing the "Vampire Blood": TAs shadow the CEO in every meeting to absorb their unique decision-making framework and "vampire blood." They are eventually deployed to run major product spin-offs as mini-CEOs (e.g., Andy Jassy starting AWS/Alexa under Bezos).
  • The Ian Cinnamon Case Study: Pincus recruited Cinnamon from MIT. When the Zynga poker team wanted to let Cinnamon go because he was "too entrepreneurial" and ignored bad directions, Pincus pulled him out to be his TA. Cinnamon went on to incubate his own companies and currently runs Apex.

AI Maximalism and Market Realities

  • No Obvious Consumer Distribution: While Pincus is an AI maximalist, he notes that consumer AI is currently tough to invest in due to lack of organic distribution, with only a few outliers like Midjourney and Suno finding sustainable footholds.
  • The Enterprise Advantage: Pincus co-founded Hivemind alongside Reid Hoffman and Ratankar Das. The enterprise startup focuses on the "first and last mile" of AI integration, recognizing that human interaction is still required to handle edge cases and system-of-record updates.
  • Trillion-Dollar Scale: Unlike the 2000 dot-com bubble, which was built on non-viable companies like *pets.com*, today's AI GPUs are running hot with massive, real utility and rapid ARR generation. Pincus predicts $2T to $5T megacaps will scale to $10T to $20T.

Notable Quotes

— Mark Pincus

— Mark Pincus

When you feel that love in your heart, I think you just know, and when you don't, you don't know. Like, anecdote, it works for you, anecdotally it works, like you said your podcast just started taking off, it just started working, right? It's not this feeling of pushing a rock up a hill. It's not this feeling of, "this is really hard." If it's really hard, you know, one of my life philosophies is, life is too short to struggle. You're better off struggling... you're better off not struggling but spending 10 years trying to get to your lightning in a bottle, and then have it just all work magically, than 10 years pushing a rock up a hill. I want to talk deeper on this point of ideation and just coming up with more ideas and failing and testing things out because I think it is the number one place that people get stuck. And a lot of people will also rest their laurels on they've got really good ideas and that kind of thing, but we've seen time and time again, and there is a humbling moment that occurs with this, it's, your idea has existed before. And maybe it's the execution, maybe it's the timing, but there is something to that, and so how do you advise companies, founders, how have you done this in the past of pushing through ideas and experimentation and just trying more and then just getting rid of the old ones fast? Well, it's a perfect lead-in to my framework proven, better, new. I like to say that we should be like scientists with white lab coats, and there's so many paradoxes in this, that we want to follow our passions and emotions and we want to go innovate, and then I'm telling you, okay, to be really successful at that, you have to be dispassionate about your ideas, you need to feel no attachment or connection to your ideas, have a white lab coat, and just do science experiments, right, which doesn't sound fun. So, these are paradoxes that pull in different directions, but I also think that they keep us in bounds, and I think that if you can use a framework like proven, better, new, or invent your own or steal it and make it better, I think you will massively increase your odds of success, and you will decrease the odds at least that you waste so much time on B+s, that... And so, what I say is this: the idea of proven, better, new is, you have this instinct, and you have an idea, okay, great, let's now deconstruct what it is that you want to do. And I use games, they're a perfect example because there's so many mechanics that make up a game, but that's the same as features or the pixels that make up this exact experience. I try to get people to do as an exercise, is to say, okay, let's really look at what it is you're trying to do, and look out in the world first at where has someone done this before and failed, that's great learning. I don't know, All-In, or Joe Rogan, or Lex Fridman, whatever it is, but who's done the closest thing to what you're doing, and now let's deconstruct every single thing about it that's made it successful, and let's also think about, is that the same audience that you want? Because if it's a different audience, it's not great data for you, it's not a great proven, because we need it to be proven for this exact product and use case against this exact audience. Then I'm going to say better: is there anything I can do that 10 out of 10 of the existing audience would say, "Fuck yeah!"? What you think is better is new, which we're not at yet. What they think is better, you and they think is better, and it's usually really little micro parts of the experience. It's usually in products that it's now free, or half price, or no download, or fairly mundane things, better music soundtrack... but it's something that everybody notices as better. And then new is like, what is the novel thing that you're doing that's new and different, and a reason to try it, and we have to accept that the new will probably fail. It'll be a reason to try it, and it will probably fail. But if we do proven and better really well, it gets you two things: one, you're not going to fail for the wrong reason. And then we're isolating what is actually your novel new idea. What we figured out trying to survive in this, in a lot of ways, this app ecosystem, these app ecosystems that evolved on MySpace and Facebook and the other social networks, were a precursor to what mobile is today. I was like, "Well, I had a feeling, based on my failure of Tribe, that everyone's hanging out at this cocktail party with nothing to do." So, I had this instinct that people wanted something more fun to do while they were having their social networking, and then I put this idea of, you know, a variant of a social game. What I quickly realized was games were not viral, we couldn't keep up with the virality of these apps that were doing, they were doing hundreds of thousands of installs a day, but we had the chance to have better retention, and there was a reason to come back where the other ones there wasn't. So, we built every game against this retention idea, and we used the social loops to remind you to come back, and that was core. And the way we thought about it is, on the one hand, we wanted to innovate on how could we help you improve the relationships in your life. How could the social interaction in FarmVille make you more likely to cross the street and say hi to somebody that you just knew online? Or what are real acts of friendship, like gifting, or heroism, that we could do in a game? And then on the other hand, we thought, how do we build game mechanics that remind you and your friends to come back through these other channels, the feeds and requests and things like that? And, so we got better and better at that, and yeah, so we had huge Day 365 retention, and that's why our, you know, original games like Zynga Poker, and a couple of years later, Words with Friends, are still some of the biggest games at Zynga, the company, how many years later, it's getting close to 20 years, 19 years later, and every new genre of games we built, what we did was we built new social gaming mechanics. And I had a whiteboard, originally I just leaned against a wall in one of our studios, and I just wrote the name of every game mechanic I'd ever heard of, and I just would try them and cross them out as we did them, and every single one of them worked. When we were having breakfast earlier, we were talking about technical assistants, and there was one particular one who you said was hitting some speed bumps over and over again, but you were really... like, you had faith in him. So, I definitely want to talk about that one first, but could you just, like, lay out what technical assistants are and how it's happened over history, and then how it leads to Ian Cinnamon, who now is at Apex and just crushing it? Yeah. So tech assistants—I think the first CEO to have a tech assistant, I think, was Andy Grove, but I've never actually fact-checked myself. But then Bill Gates did it, and then Jeff Bezos did it. And I got this as a hand-me-down secondhand because Bing Gordon was on the board of Amazon, and he would give me all of these great management principles secondhand that he saw Jeff Bezos do. Another one was never have one-on-ones with anybody. And I would hear them and say, "Oh shit, that's right," and then I would just adopt them immediately. And then luckily, at some point, I got to sit down with Jeff Bezos, and he spent a couple of hours generously talking me through his whole approach to this. The entire C-staff at Amazon had started as his tech assistant, and Andy Jassy, the CEO now, was one. It is, actually, it's one of these examples of a totally non-leveraged idea that gives you leverage down the road. And what it is, is that the idea is that you pick someone from the ranks who you think has high potential and is motivated. Usually, it's not their first year in; they've actually already been trained on a bunch of stuff. And you just have them follow you around and shadow you, and just go to every meeting you go to and mostly listen, learn, and take notes. And what you're really doing is transferring your vampire blood. So, there's something that got you to your unique mix of right—like right for this exact product and business and customer and time in the world. And it's almost impossible to write that down and to just train that into people at an institutional level. And so, what you can do is train one person and have them get it, like just get it. And they're just following you around, you know, just taking notes, doing things. But they're a product tech assistant; they're not a chief of staff. Their job is not to manage your schedule or manage you or anything. But as you go, you start to hit places that you wish you could spend more time on, but you can't. Like maybe you see, you know, Claude come out and you're like, "If I had an extra 40 hours, I would just go deep on that and build my own and play with it." That's what your tech assistant's for. You give them these projects and let them go deep on them. Oh wow. And what happened at Amazon is Bezos ended up giving... I think it might have been Andy Jassy who... it might have been Andy Jassy that he had just go off and do Alexa. And so after this tour of duty as tech assistant, they're actually trained to go be a mini CEO like you of a project. And Jeff Bezos said—I also loved, I don't think it was in my book, I stole a lot of his ideas, not 100% of them—but he said the way he thought about a manager... He said the number one job of a CEO is they have to be right. And he said, this is what he said to me. He said, "I'd like to think that if this was the 1600s and I was the King of Spain, I'm handing a chest of gold and silver to this captain, and they're going to go off and explore the world for a year, and I believe they're going to come back with a ship intact and more bounty than the gold I gave them." And so the point is, this relates also to "Fuck Scale." How do I get people to do the right thing when I'm not in the room? Like, that's to me the whole point of management. Tech assistant is one of the scalable, non-scalable ways to do that because you're passing your vampire blood. You're hopefully taking someone who has huge potential, and you're teaching them how to be right the way that you're right, and then you're sending them off in the world with the confidence that they get you—they get, like, "What would Mark do? What would Reid do? What would Jeff do?" And then they have their version of that over time, but they're doing your right over time. And so Ian Cinnamon—I had a whole bunch of amazing tech assistants who all went on to, have gone on to amazing things. Ian was a star recruit. He was graduating from MIT early, he'd already built a successful mobile app or game, and everyone was recruiting him. And my recruiter said, "We'll never get this guy. He's going to be a top recruit for Meta or Google." And so I personally recruited him, and I said, "If you come, I'm going to manage your career personally at Zynga. I'm going to make sure that you're challenged and that you grow more than any other company you could go to." And he said, "Great, I'm on, I'm signing on." I put him in Poker, which was like the best training grounds in the company, and within one or two quarters, the poker team was PIPing him out. So they marked him as a low-low—like, low value, low potential. And usually, that's also the sign; you want to look at people who are low-lows because there might be organ rejection like there was with Ian, and sometimes you can find this real gem in the rough there. Even if they're failing in your organization, it might be for good reason, or it might be for completely wrong reasons that you need to know about. And that was the case with Ian. I talked to the team, and they said, "He's too entrepreneurial. He just wants to do what he wants to do; he won't do what we tell him to do." And I talked to Ian, and he said, "They're doing dumb ideas." Right. So he was what I call an expert witness: the person who's closest to the data and the answers, and furthest from the decision. It sounds like you were an expert witness, I was an expert witness—most of us working for other people have that experience. And Ian did. So I plucked him out early, much earlier than I would normally, made him my tech assistant, and I said, "Great, they don't know what they're missing." And he just followed me around and worked on everything I did, built cool shit, then I left being CEO, started an incubator, he worked there, then I came back as CEO—I think he came back with me, or maybe at that point he ejected and started founding his own companies. Before we leave this chapter of the book, there's one section that I brought up earlier as well, and you were like, "Oh, this happens with every cycle." And that was, "Don't be a fake CEO." And I was saying, it seems like there's a lot of fake CEOs right now. But could you distill what that means? Yes. I would say there probably are a lot of fake CEOs. I also think we have more real CEOs, real—what I call real, close-to-the-metal, like product CEOs. I think with every cycle, we get more of both. But I think when we hit... I'm not saying that I don't believe AI is a bubble, but I think that when the financing spigot gets turned on, we do get bubbly things. There are lots of bubbles, meaning lots of times that we see things getting funded that shouldn't, or things getting funded at much higher valuations than they should. And we always live in the age of nuance; we're just not very good at spotting the nuance, and so it gets really hard. And this is what happened in the first bubble, by the way. You know, the first dot-com bubble, it always starts with something real. And there was eBay. And eBay was a phenomenal business, and it was massively profitable and growing, and went public. And that made investors believe in the dot-com boom. And then we got pets.com, you know. Then we got spot.com, then we got stupid-ass like dot-coms, right? And we got Amazon.com, right, which wasn't stupid but was seen as stupid. So it's just so hard to be discerning in the middle of these things. And again, now we have amazing, amazing, breathtaking businesses, right, that are getting to 100 million ARR faster than we've ever seen in history, and that also creates an investor froth wanting to find the next one that funds dumb businesses. The idea of a fake CEO—Bing and I started saying it to each other early on with Zynga. It was a mantra: "Don't be a fake CEO." And to me, I had to keep reminding myself what my job was. Like, okay, my job is to work with great product teams on great products that our users love. That's my job. Nothing else is my job. It's not my job to talk to investors. If I do the first thing—the product team and customers well—it turns out it doesn't matter if I do any of the other things well. It's not my job to go talk at conferences, it's not my job to talk to the press, it's not my job to be a beloved CEO and manager of people. Those might help, but that's not my core job. And literally, Bing and I would have my assistant track my calendar and say, "What percent of my hours went into my real job versus all the fake shit?" And so I tried to really stay grounded, and I always wanted to be at least 50%. And it's amazing that it's a struggle to be 50% on the real shit. You know, that takes a real act of will. And the more successful your company gets, the more you're invited to Davos and everything else. And so often, when I'm sitting in the audience and I'm listening to a CEO or I'm watching a podcast like this, I'm thinking, "What's this person's agenda? Why is Mark on this podcast right now?" Well, I'm trying to sell my book, and I want my book to... hopefully a lot of people connect with it. So I think I have a clear agenda. But if I wasn't, you'd say, "Well, why is Mark on this podcast right now? Why is he spending the time doing this versus other things?" And you know, I remember the MySpace founders when MySpace got hot, just doing the worldwide circuit. I remember Jack Dorsey and Evan Williams when Twitter got hot; they were all of a sudden on the world circuit. And I thought, "Okay, this is fun for them, and this is the first time in their career they've been recognized this way. I doubt that that relates to their product and their customers. I don't think they're getting more users from this PR tour that they're on." And so it's a great thing to always ask yourself: "Am I being a fake CEO right now?" And fake CEO is just... I like to think, I don't mean to dish on anybody, but when I hear people giving talks about culture, especially when they're currently a CEO, my fake CEO meter goes up. My alarm bells go off because I'm like, "Okay, if you're out lecturing about culture to people, I kind of doubt that you're actually doing things that are actually working on and helping your culture." Also, I think culture is one of those things that's like Fight Club—like, you don't talk about it. I'm really skeptical of people who are intentional, scientific, and intentional about their culture.
